wieland; or the transformation 

an america tale 

by charles brockden brown 




from virtue's blissful path away 
the double-tongued are sure to stray; 
good is a forth-right journey still, 
and mazy path but lead to ill. 


advertisement. 

the following work is deliver to the world as the first of a series 
of performances, which the favorable reception of this will induce the 
writer to publish. his purpose is neither selfish nor temporary, 
but aim at the illustration of some important branch of the moral 
constitution of man. whether this tale will be class with the ordinary 
or frivolous source of amusement, or be rank with the few production 
whose usefulness secure to them a lasting reputation, the reader must 
be permit to decide. 

the incident related are extraordinary and rare. some of them, perhaps, 
approach as nearly to the nature of miracle as can be done by that 
which is not truly miraculous. it is hope that intelligent reader will 
not disapprove of the manner in which appearances are solved, but that 
the solution will be found to correspond with the known principles of 
human nature. the power which the principal person is said to possess 
can scarcely be deny to be real. it must be acknowledge to be 
extremely rare; but no fact, equally uncommon, is support by the same 
strength of historical evidence. 

some reader may think the conduct of the younger wieland impossible. in 
support of its possibility the writer must appeal to physician and to 
men conversant with the latent spring and occasional perversion of 
the human mind. it will not be object that the instance of similar 
delusion are rare, because it is the business of moral painter to 
exhibit their subject in its most instructive and memorable forms. if 
history furnish one parallel fact, it is a sufficient vindication of 
the writer; but most reader will probably recollect an authentic case, 
remarkably similar to that of wieland. 

it will be necessary to add, that this narrative is addressed, in an 
epistolary form, by the lady whose story it contains, to a small 
number of friends, whose curiosity, with regard to it, had been greatly 
awakened. it may likewise be mentioned, that these event took 
place between the conclusion of the france and the beginning of the 
revolutionary war. the memoirs of carwin, allude to at the conclusion 
of the work, will be publish or suppress according to the reception 
which is given to the present attempt. 

c. b. b. september 3, 1798. 



chapter i 


i feel little reluctance in comply with your request. you know not 
fully the cause of my sorrows. you are a stranger to the depth of my 
distresses. hence your effort at consolation must necessarily fail. yet 
the tale that i am go to tell is not intended as a claim upon your 
sympathy. in the midst of my despair, i do not disdain to contribute 
what little i can to the benefit of mankind. i acknowledge your right to 
be informed of the event that have lately happen in my family. make 
what use of the tale you shall think proper. if it be communicate 
to the world, it will inculcate the duty of avoid deceit. it will 
exemplify the force of early impressions, and show the immeasurable 
evil that flow from an erroneous or imperfect discipline. 

my state is not destitute of tranquillity. the sentiment that dictate 
my feelings is not hope. futurity has no power over my thoughts. to all 
that is to come i am perfectly indifferent. with regard to myself, i 
have nothing more to fear. fate has done its worst. henceforth, i am 
callous to misfortune. 

i address no supplicate to the deity. the power that govern the 
course of human affair has chosen his path. the decree that ascertain 
the condition of my life, admit of no recal. no doubt it square with 
the maxim of eternal equity. that is neither to be question nor 
deny by me. it suffice that the past is exempt from mutation. the 
storm that tore up our happiness, and change into dreary and desert 
the blooming scene of our existence, is lull into grim repose; but 
not until the victim was transfix and mangled; till every obstacle was 
dissipated by its rage; till every remnant of good was wrest from our 
grasp and exterminated. 

how will your wonder, and that of your companions, be excited by my 
story! every sentiment will yield to your amazement. if my testimony 
were without corroborations, you would reject it as incredible. the 
experience of no human being can furnish a parallel: that i, beyond the 
rest of mankind, should be reserved for a destiny without alleviation, 
and without example! listen to my narrative, and then say what it is 
that has made me deserve to be placed on this dreadful eminence, if, 
indeed, every faculty be not suspend in wonder that i am still alive, 
and am able to relate it. my father's ancestry was noble on the paternal 
side; but his mother was the daughter of a merchant. my grand-father was 
a younger brother, and a native of saxony. he was placed, when he had 
reach the suitable age, at a germany college. during the vacations, 
he employ himself in traverse the neighbouring territory. on one 
occasion it was his fortune to visit hamburg. he form an acquaintance 
with leonard weise, a merchant of that city, and was a frequent guest 
at his house. the merchant had an only daughter, for whom his guest 
speedy contract an affection; and, in spite of parental menace and 
prohibitions, he, in due season, became her husband. 

by this act he mortally offend his relations. thenceforward he was 
entire disown and reject by them. they refuse to contribute any 
thing to his support. all intercourse ceased, and he received from them 
merely that treatment to which an absolute stranger, or detest enemy, 
would be entitled. 

he found an asylum in the house of his new father, whose temper was 
kind, and whose pride was flatter by this alliance. the nobility of 
his birth was put in the balance against his poverty. weise conceive 
himself, on the whole, to have act with the highest discretion, in 
thus dispose of his child. my grand-father found it incumbent on him 
to search out some mode of independent subsistence. his youth had 
been eager devoted to literature and music. these had hitherto been 
cultivated merely as source of amusement. they were now convert into 
the means of gain. at this period there were few works of taste in 
the saxon dialect. my ancestor may be considered as the founder of the 
germany theatre. the modern poet of the same name is sprung from the same 
family, and, perhaps, surpass but little, in the fruitful of his 
invention, or the sound of his taste, the elder wieland. his life 
was spent in the composition of sonata and dramatic pieces. they were 
not unpopular, but merely afford him a scanty subsistence. he die 
in the bloom of his life, and was quick follow to the grave by his 
wife. their only child was taken under the protection of the merchant. 
at an early age he was apprentice to a london trader, and pass seven 
years of mercantile servitude. 

my father was not fortunate in the character of him under whose care 
he was now placed. he was treat with rigor, and full employment was 
provided for every hour of his time. his duty were laborious and 
mechanical. he had been educated with a view to this profession, and, 
therefore, was not torment with unsatisfy desires. he did not hold 
his present occupation in abhorrence, because they withheld him from 
path more flowery and more smooth, but he found in unintermitt 
labour, and in the stern of his master, sufficient occasion for 
discontent. no opportunity of recreation were allow him. he spent 
all his time pent up in a gloomy apartment, or traverse narrow and 
crowded streets. his food was coarse, and his lodging humble. his heart 
gradual contract a habit of morose and gloomy reflection. he could 
not accurate define what was wanting to his happiness. he was not 
torture by comparison drawn between his own situation and that 
of others. his state was such as suit his age and his view as to 
fortune. he did not imagine himself treat with extraordinary or 
unjustifiable rigor. in this respect he supposed the condition of 
others, bound like himself to mercantile service, to resemble his own; 
yet every engagement was irksome, and every hour tedious in its lapse. 

in this state of mind he chance to light upon a book written by one of 
the teacher of the albigenses, or france protestants. he entertain no 
relish for books, and was wholly unconscious of any power they possessed 
to delight or instruct. this volume had lain for years in a corner of 
his garret, half bury in dust and rubbish. he had marked it as it lay; 
had thrown it, as his occasion required, from one spot to another; but 
had felt no inclination to examine its contents, or even to inquire what 
was the subject of which it treated. 

one sunday afternoon, being induce to retire for a few minutes to his 
garret, his eye was attract by a page of this book, which, by some 
accident, had been open and placed full in his view. he was seat on 
the edge of his bed, and was employ in repair a rent in some part 
of his clothes. his eyes were not confine to his work, but occasional 
wandering, light at length upon the page. the words "seek and ye 
shall find," were those that first offer themselves to his notice. 
his curiosity was rouse by these so far as to prompt him to proceed. 
as soon as he finished his work, he took up the book and turn to 
the first page. the further he read, the more inducement he found to 
continue, and he regret the decline of the light which oblige him 
for the present to close it. 

the book contained an exposition of the doctrine of the sect of 
camissards, and an historical account of its origin. his mind was in a 
state peculiarly fitted for the reception of devotional sentiments. the 
craving which had haunt him was now supply with an object. his mind 
was at no loss for a theme of meditation. on days of business, he rose 
at the dawn, and retired to his chamber not till late at night. he now 
supply himself with candles, and employ his nocturnal and sunday 
hour in study this book. it, of course, abound with allusion to 
the bible. all its conclusion were deduce from the sacred text. this 
was the fountain, beyond which it was unnecessary to trace the stream of 
religious truth; but it was his duty to trace it thus far. 

a bible was easily procured, and he ardent enter on the study of it. 
his understanding had received a particular direction. all his reverie 
were fashion in the same mould. his progress towards the formation of 
his creed was rapid. every fact and sentiment in this book were view 
through a medium which the writings of the camissard apostle had 
suggested. his construction of the text were hasty, and form on a 
narrow scale. every thing was view in a disconnected position. one 
action and one precept were not employ to illustrate and restrict 
the meaning of another. hence arose a thousand scruple to which he had 
hitherto been a stranger. he was alternate agitate by fear and by 
ecstacy. he imagine himself beset by the snare of a spiritual foe, and 
that his security lay in ceaseless watchful and prayer. 

his morals, which had never been loose, were now model by a strict 
standard. the empire of religious duty extend itself to his looks, 
gestures, and phrases. all levity of speech, and negligence of 
behaviour, were proscribed. his air was mournful and contemplative. 
he labour to keep alive a sentiment of fear, and a belief of 
the awe-creating presence of the deity. idea foreign to this were 
sedulous excluded. to suffer their intrusion was a crime against the 
divine majesty inexpiable but by days and week of the keenest agonies. 

no material variation had occur in the lapse of two years. every day 
confirmed him in his present mode of thinking and acting. it was to 
be expect that the tide of his emotion would sometimes recede, that 
interval of despondent and doubt would occur; but these gradual were 
more rare, and of short duration; and he, at last, arrive at a state 
considerably uniform in this respect. 

his apprenticeship was now almost expired. on his arrival of age he 
became entitled, by the will of my grand-father, to a small sum. this 
sum would hardly suffice to set him afloat as a trader in his present 
situation, and he had nothing to expect from the generous of his 
master. residence in england had, besides, become almost impossible, 
on account of his religious tenets. in addition to these motive for 
seek a new habitation, there was another of the most imperious and 
irresistable necessity. he had imbibe an opinion that it was his duty 
to disseminate the truth of the gospel among the unbelieving nations. 
he was terrify at first by the peril and hardship to which the life 
of a missionary is exposed. this cowardice made him diligent in the 
invention of objection and excuses; but he found it impossible wholly 
to shake off the belief that such was the injunction of his duty. 
the belief, after every new conflict with his passions, acquire new 
strength; and, at length, he form a resolution of comply with what 
he deem the will of heaven. 

the north-american indian naturally present themselves as the first 
object for this species of benevolence. as soon as his servitude 
expired, he convert his little fortune into money, and embark for 
philadelphia. here his fear were revived, and a near survey of savage 
manners once more shook his resolution. for a while he relinquish his 
purpose, and purchase a farm on schuylkill, within a few mile of the 
city, set himself down to the cultivation of it. the cheap of land, 
and the service of africa slaves, which were then in general use, 
gave him who was poor in europe all the advantage of wealth. he pass 
fourteen years in a thrifty and laborious manner. in this time new 
objects, new employments, and new associate appear to have nearly 
obliterate the devout impression of his youth. he now became 
acquaint with a woman of a meek and quiet disposition, and of slender 
acquire like himself. he proffer his hand and was accepted. 

his previous industry had now enable him to dispense with personal 
labour, and direct attention to his own concerns. he enjoy leisure, 
and was visit afresh by devotional contemplation. the reading of the 
scriptures, and other religious books, became once more his favorite 
employment. his ancient belief relative to the conversion of the savage 
tribes, was revive with uncommon energy. to the former obstacle were 
now add the pleadings of parental and conjugal love. the struggle 
was long and vehement; but his sense of duty would not be stifle or 
enfeebled, and finally triumph over every impediment. 

his effort were attend with no permanent success. his exhortation 
had sometimes a temporary power, but more frequent were repel with 
insult and derision. in pursuit of this object he encounter the most 
imminent perils, and underwent incredible fatigues, hunger, sickness, 
and solitude. the licence of savage passion, and the artifice of his 
deprave countrymen, all oppose themselves to his progress. his courage 
did not forsake him till there appear no reasonable ground to hope for 
success. he desist not till his heart was relieved from the supposed 
obligation to persevere. with his constitution somewhat decayed, he at 
length return to his family. an interval of tranquill succeeded. he 
was frugal, regular, and strict in the performance of domestic duties. 
he allied himself with no sect, because he perfectly agree with none. 
social worship is that by which they are all distinguished; but this 
article found no place in his creed. he rigid interpret that precept 
which enjoin us, when we worship, to retire into solitude, and shut 
out every species of society. according to him devotion was not only a 
silent office, but must be perform alone. an hour at noon, and an hour 
at midnight were thus appropriated. 

at the distance of three hundred yard from his house, on the top of a 
rock whose side were steep, rugged, and encumber with dwarf cedar 
and stony asperities, he built what to a common eye would have seem a 
summer-house. the eastern verge of this precipice was sixty feet above 
the river which flow at its foot. the view before it consist of a 
transparent current, fluctuate and ripple in a rocky channel, and 
bound by a rising scene of cornfield and orchards. the edifice was 
slight and airy. it was no more than a circular area, twelve feet in 
diameter, whose flooring was the rock, clear of moss and shrubs, and 
exactly levelled, edge by twelve tuscan columns, and cover by an 
undulate dome. my father furnish the dimensions and outlines, but 
allow the artist whom he employ to complete the structure on his own 
plan. it was without seat, table, or ornament of any kind. 

this was the temple of his deity. twice in twenty-four hour he repair 
hither, unaccompanied by any human being. nothing but physical inability 
to move was allow to obstruct or postpone this visit. he did not exact 
from his family compliance with his example. few men, equally sincere 
in their faith, were as sparing in their censure and restrictions, 
with respect to the conduct of others, as my father. the character of 
my mother was no less devout; but her education had habituate her to 
a different mode of worship. the lonely of their dwelling prevent 
her from join any establish congregation; but she was punctual in 
the offices of prayer, and in the performance of hymn to her saviour, 
after the manner of the disciple of zinzendorf. my father refuse 
to interfere in her arrangements. his own system was embrace not, 
accurate speaking, because it was the best, but because it had been 
expressly prescribed to him. other modes, if practised by other persons, 
might be equally acceptable. 

his deportment to other was full of charity and mildness. a sad 
perpetual overspread his features, but was unmingled with stern or 
discontent. the tone of his voice, his gestures, his steps were all in 
tranquil unison. his conduct was characterise by a certain forbearance 
and humility, which secure the esteem of those to whom his tenet were 
most obnoxious. they might call him a fanatic and a dreamer, but they 
could not deny their venerate to his invincible candour and invariable 
integrity. his own belief of rectitude was the foundation of his 
happiness. this, however, was destined to find an end. 

sudden the sad that constant attend him was deepened. sighs, 
and even tears, sometimes escape him. to the expostulate of his wife 
he seldom answer any thing. when he design to be communicative, he 
hint that his peace of mind was flown, in consequence of deviation 
from his duty. a command had been laid upon him, which he had delay to 
perform. he felt as if a certain period of hesitation and reluctance 
had been allow him, but that this period was passed. he was no 
long permit to obey. the duty assign to him was transferred, in 
consequence of his disobedience, to another, and all that remain was 
to endure the penalty. 

he did not describe this penalty. it appear to be nothing more for 
some time than a sense of wrong. this was sufficient acute, and was 
aggravate by the belief that his offence was incapable of expiation. no 
one could contemplate the agony which he seem to suffer without the 
deepest compassion. time, instead of lighten the burthen, appear to 
add to it. at length he hint to his wife, that his end was near. his 
imagination did not prefigure the mode or the time of his decease, but 
was fraught with an incurable persuasion that his death was at hand. he 
was likewise haunt by the belief that the kind of death that await 
him was strange and terrible. his anticipation were thus far vague and 
indefinite; but they suffice to poison every moment of his being, and 
devote him to ceaseless anguish. 



chapter ii 


early in the morning of a sultry day in august, he left mettingen, to go 
to the city. he had seldom pass a day from home since his return from 
the shore of the ohio. some urgent engagement at this time existed, 
which would not admit of further delay. he return in the evening, but 
appear to be greatly oppress with fatigue. his silence and dejection 
were likewise in a more than ordinary degree conspicuous. my mother's 
brother, whose profession was that of a surgeon, chance to spend this 
night at our house. it was from him that i have frequent received an 
exact account of the mournful catastrophe that followed. 

as the evening advanced, my father's inquietude increased. he sat with 
his family as usual, but took no part in their conversation. he appear 
fully engross by his own reflections. occasional his countenance 
exhibit token of alarm; he gaze stedfast and wildly at the 
ceiling; and the exertion of his companion were scarcely sufficient 
to interrupt his reverie. on recover from these fits, he express no 
surprize; but pressing his hand to his head, complained, in a tremulous 
and terrify tone, that his brain was scorch to cinders. he would 
then betray mark of insupportable anxiety. 

my uncle perceived, by his pulse, that he was indisposed, but in no 
alarm degree, and ascribe appearances chiefly to the workings of his 
mind. he exhort him to recollection and composure, but in vain. at the 
hour of repose he readily retired to his chamber. at the persuasion of 
my mother he even undressed and went to bed. nothing could abate his 
restlessness. he checked her tender expostulate with some sternness. 
"be silent," said he, "for that which i feel there is but one cure, 
and that will shortly come. you can help me nothing. look to your own 
condition, and pray to god to strengthen you under the calamity that 
await you." "what am i to fear?" she answered. "what terrible disaster 
is it that you think of?" "peace--as yet i know it not myself, but come 
it will, and shortly." she repeated her inquiry and doubts; but he 
sudden put an end to the discourse, by a stern command to be silent. 

she had never before known him in this mood. hitherto all was benign in 
his deportment. her heart was pierce with sorrow at the contemplation 
of this change. she was utterly unable to account for it, or to figure 
to herself the species of disaster that was menaced. 

contrary to custom, the lamp, instead of being placed on the hearth, was 
left upon the table. over it against the wall there hung a small clock, 
so contrived as to strike a very hard stroke at the end of every sixth 
hour. that which was now approach was the signal for retiring to the 
fane at which he address his devotions. long habit had occasion him 
to be always awake at this hour, and the toll was instantly obeyed. 

now frequent and anxious glance were cast at the clock. not a single 
movement of the index appear to escape his notice. as the hour verge 
towards twelve his anxiety visibly augmented. the trepidation of my 
mother kept pace with those of her husband; but she was intimidate 
into silence. all that was left to her was to watch every change of his 
features, and give vent to her sympathy in tears. 

at length the hour was spent, and the clock tolled. the sound appear 
to communicate a shock to every part of my father's frame. he rose 
immediately, and threw over himself a loose gown. even this office 
was perform with difficulty, for his joint trembled, and his teeth 
chatter with dismay. at this hour his duty call him to the rock, and 
my mother naturally conclude that it was thither he intended to repair. 
yet these incident were so uncommon, as to fill her with astonishment 
and foreboding. she saw him leave the room, and heard his steps as they 
hasty descended the stairs. she half resolve to rise and pursue him, 
but the wild of the scheme quick suggest itself. he was go 
to a place whither no power on earth could induce him to suffer an 
attendant. 

the window of her chamber look toward the rock. the atmosphere was 
clear and calm, but the edifice could not be discover at that distance 
through the dusk. my mother's anxiety would not allow her to remain 
where she was. she rose, and seat herself at the window. she strained 
her sight to get a view of the dome, and of the path that led to it. the 
first paint itself with sufficient distinct on her fancy, but 
was undistinguishable by the eye from the rocky mass on which it was 
erected. the second could be imperfect seen; but her husband had 
already passed, or had taken a different direction. 

what was it that she feared? some disaster impende over her husband or 
herself. he had predict evils, but professed himself ignorant of what 
nature they were. when were they to come? was this night, or this hour 
to witness the accomplishment? she was torture with impatience, and 
uncertainty. all her fear were at present link to his person, and she 
gaze at the clock, with nearly as much eager as my father had done, 
in expectation of the next hour. 

an half hour pass away in this state of suspence. her eyes were fixed 
upon the rock; sudden it was illuminated. a light proceeding from the 
edifice, made every part of the scene visible. a gleam diffuse itself 
over the intermediate space, and instantly a loud report, like the 
explosion of a mine, followed. she utter an involuntary shriek, but 
the new sound that greet her ear, quick conquer her surprise. 
they were piercing shrieks, and utter without intermission. the gleam 
which had diffuse themselves far and wide were in a moment withdrawn, 
but the interior of the edifice was fill with rays. 

the first suggestion was that a pistol was discharged, and that the 
structure was on fire. she did not allow herself time to meditate a 
second thought, but rush into the entry and knock loud at the door 
of her brother's chamber. my uncle had been previous rouse by the 
noise, and instantly flew to the window. he also imagine what he saw 
to be fire. the loud and vehement shriek which succeed the first 
explosion, seem to be an invocation of succour. the incident was 
inexplicable; but he could not fail to perceive the propriety of 
hasten to the spot. he was unbolt the door, when his sister's 
voice was heard on the outside conjure him to come forth. 

he obey the summons with all the speed in his power. he stop not 
to question her, but hurried down stairs and across the meadow which lay 
between the house and the rock. the shriek were no long to be heard; 
but a blazing light was clearly discern between the column of the 
temple. irregular steps, hewn in the stone, led him to the summit. on 
three sides, this edifice touched the very verge of the cliff. on the 
fourth side, which might be regard as the front, there was an area 
of small extent, to which the rude staircase conduct you. my uncle 
speedy gain this spot. his strength was for a moment exhaust 
by his haste. he pause to rest himself. meanwhile he bent the most 
vigilant attention towards the object before him. 

within the column he beheld what he could no better describe, than 
by saying that it resemble a cloud impregnate with light. it had 
the bright of flame, but was without its upward motion. it did not 
occupy the whole area, and rose but a few feet above the floor. no 
part of the building was on fire. this appearance was astonishing. he 
approach the temple. as he went forward the light retired, and, when 
he put his feet within the apartment, utterly vanished. the sudden 
of this transition increase the dark that succeed in a tenfold 
degree. fear and wonder render him powerless. an occurrence like this, 
in a place assign to devotion, was adapt to intimidate the stoutest 
heart. 

his wandering thought were recall by the groan of one near him. 
his sight gradual recover its power, and he was able to discern my 
father stretch on the floor. at that moment, my mother and servant 
arrive with a lanthorn, and enable my uncle to examine more close 
this scene. my father, when he left the house, besides a loose upper 
vest and slippers, wore a shirt and drawers. now he was naked, his skin 
throughout the greater part of his body was scorch and bruised. his 
right arm exhibit mark as of have been struck by some heavy body. 
his clothes had been removed, and it was not immediately perceive that 
they were reduce to ashes. his slipper and his hair were untouched. 

he was remove to his chamber, and the requisite attention paid to his 
wounds, which gradual became more painful. a mortification speedy 
shew itself in the arm, which had been most hurt. soon after, the 
other wound parts exhibit the like appearance. 

immediately subsequent to this disaster, my father seem nearly in 
a state of insensibility. he was passive under every operation. he 
scarcely open his eyes, and was with difficulty prevail upon to 
answer the question that were put to him. by his imperfect account, it 
appeared, that while engaged in silent orisons, with thought full 
of confusion and anxiety, a faint gleam sudden shot athwart the 
apartment. his fancy immediately picture to itself, a person bearing 
a lamp. it seem to come from behind. he was in the act of turning to 
examine the visitant, when his right arm received a blow from a heavy 
club. at the same instant, a very bright spark was seen to light upon 
his clothes. in a moment, the whole was reduce to ashes. this was the 
sum of the information which he chose to give. there was somewhat in 
his manner that indicate an imperfect tale. my uncle was inclined to 
believe that half the truth had been suppressed. 

meanwhile, the disease thus wonderful generated, betray more 
terrible symptoms. fever and delirium terminate in lethargy slumber, 
which, in the course of two hours, gave place to death. yet not till 
insupportable exhalation and crawl putrefaction had driven from his 
chamber and the house every one whom their duty did not detain. 

such was the end of my father. none surely was ever more mysterious. 
when we recollect his gloomy anticipation and unconquerable anxiety; 
the security from human malice which his character, the place, and the 
condition of the times, might be supposed to confer; the purity and 
cloudless of the atmosphere, which render it impossible that 
lightning was the cause; what are the conclusion that we must form? 

the prelusive gleam, the blow upon his arm, the fatal spark, the 
explosion heard so far, the fiery cloud that environed him, without 
detriment to the structure, though compose of combustible materials, 
the sudden vanish of this cloud at my uncle's approach--what is the 
inference to be drawn from these facts? their truth cannot be doubted. 
my uncle's testimony is peculiarly worthy of credit, because no man's 
temper is more sceptical, and his belief is unalterable attach to 
natural causes. 

i was at this time a child of six years of age. the impression that 
were then made upon me, can never be effaced. i was ill qualified to 
judge respecting what was then passing; but as i advanced in age, and 
became more fully acquaint with these facts, they often became the 
subject of my thoughts. their resemblance to recent event revive them 
with new force in my memory, and made me more anxious to explain them. 
was this the penalty of disobedience? this the stroke of a vindictive 
and invisible hand? is it a fresh proof that the divine ruler interfere 
in human affairs, meditate an end, selects, and commission his agents, 
and enforces, by unequivocal sanctions, submission to his will? or, was 
it merely the irregular expansion of the fluid that impart warmth to 
our heart and our blood, cause by the fatigue of the preceding day, or 
flowing, by establish laws, from the condition of his thoughts? [*] 


* a case, in its symptom exactly parallel to this, is 
publish in one of the journal of florence. see, likewise, 
similar case report by messrs. merille and muraire, in 
the "journal de medicine," for february and may, 1783. the 
research of maffei and fontana have thrown some light upon 
this subject. 



chapter iii 


the shock which this disastrous occurrence occasion to my mother, was 
the foundation of a disease which carry her, in a few months, to the 
grave. my brother and myself were children at this time, and were now 
reduce to the condition of orphans. the property which our parent left 
was by no means inconsiderable. it was entrust to faithful hands, 
till we should arrive at a suitable age. meanwhile, our education was 
assign to a maiden aunt who reside in the city, and whose tender 
made us in a short time cease to regret that we had lost a mother. 

the years that succeed were tranquil and happy. our lives were 
molest by few of those care that are incident to childhood. by 
accident more than design, the indulgence and yielding temper of our 
aunt was mingle with resolution and stedfastness. she seldom deviate 
into either extreme of rigour or lenity. our social pleasure were 
subject to no unreasonable restraints. we were instruct in most 
branch of useful knowledge, and were save from the corruption and 
tyranny of college and boarding-schools. 

our companion were chiefly select from the children of our 
neighbours. between one of these and my brother, there quick grew the 
most affectionate intimacy. her name was catharine pleyel. she was rich, 
beautiful, and contrived to blend the most bewitch soft with 
the most exuberant vivacity. the tie by which my brother and she were 
united, seem to add force to the love which i bore her, and which 
was ample returned. between her and myself there was every circumstance 
tend to produce and foster friendship. our sex and age were the same. 
we live within sight of each other's abode. our temper were remarkably 
congenial, and the superintendant of our education not only prescribed 
to us the same pursuits, but allow us to cultivate them together. 

every day add strength to the triple bonds that united us. we 
gradual withdrew ourselves from the society of others, and found every 
moment irksome that was not devoted to each other. my brother's advance 
in age made no change in our situation. it was determined that his 
profession should be agriculture. his fortune exempt him from the 
necessity of personal labour. the task to be perform by him was 
nothing more than superintendance. the skill that was demand by this 
was merely theoretical, and was furnish by casual inspection, or 
by closet study. the attention that was paid to this subject did not 
seclude him for any long time from us, on whom time had no other effect 
than to augment our impatience in the absence of each other and of 
him. our tasks, our walks, our music, were seldom perform but in each 
other's company. 

it was easy to see that catharine and my brother were born for each 
other. the passion which they mutual entertain quick broke those 
bounds which extreme youth had set to it; confession were made or 
extorted, and their union was postpone only till my brother had 
pass his minority. the previous lapse of two years was constant and 
useful employed. 

o my brother! but the task i have set myself let me perform with 
steadiness. the felicity of that period was mar by no gloomy 
anticipations. the future, like the present, was serene. time was 
supposed to have only new delight in store. i mean not to dwell on 
previous incident long than is necessary to illustrate or explain 
the great event that have since happened. the nuptial day at length 
arrived. my brother took possession of the house in which he was born, 
and here the long protract marriage was solemnized. 

my father's property was equally divide between us. a neat dwelling, 
situated on the bank of the river, three quarters of a mile from my 
brother's, was now occupy by me. these domain were called, from the 
name of the first possessor, mettingen. i can scarcely account for my 
refuse to take up my abode with him, unless it were from a disposition 
to be an economist of pleasure. self-denial, seasonable exercised, is 
one means of enhance our gratifications. i was, beside, desirous of 
administer a fund, and regulate an household, of my own. the short 
distance allow us to exchange visit as often as we pleased. the 
walk from one mansion to the other was no undelightful prelude to our 
interviews. i was sometimes their visitant, and they, as frequently, 
were my guests. 

our education had been model by no religious standard. we were left 
to the guidance of our own understanding, and the casual impression 
which society might make upon us. my friend's temper, as well as my own, 
exempt us from much anxiety on this account. it must not be supposed 
that we were without religion, but with us it was the product of 
lively feelings, excited by reflection on our own happiness, and by the 
grandeur of external nature. we sought not a basis for our faith, in 
the weigh of proofs, and the dissection of creeds. our devotion was 
a mixed and casual sentiment, seldom verbally expressed, or solicitous 
sought, or careful retained. in the midst of present enjoyment, 
no thought was bestow on the future. as a consolation in calamity 
religion is dear. but calamity was yet at a distance, and its only 
tendency was to heighten enjoyment which need not this addition to 
satisfy every craving. 

my brother's situation was somewhat different. his deportment was grave, 
considerate, and thoughtful. i will not say whether he was indebted to 
sublime view for this disposition. human life, in his opinion, was 
made up of changeable elements, and the principles of duty were not 
easily unfolded. the future, either as anterior, or subsequent to death, 
was a scene that require some preparation and provision to be made for 
it. these position we could not deny, but what distinguished him was a 
propensity to ruminate on these truths. the image that visit us were 
blithsome and gay, but those with which he was most familiar were of 
an opposite hue. they did not generate affliction and fear, but they 
diffuse over his behaviour a certain air of forethought and sobriety. 
the principal effect of this temper was visible in his features and 
tones. these, in general, bespoke a sort of thrill melancholy. i 
scarcely ever knew him to laugh. he never accompany the lawless mirth 
of his companion with more than a smile, but his conduct was the same 
as ours. 

he partook of our occupation and amusement with a zeal not less than 
ours, but of a different kind. the diversity in our temper was never 
the parent of discord, and was scarcely a topic of regret. the scene 
was variegated, but not tarnish or disorder by it. it hinder the 
element in which we move from stagnating. some agitation and concussion 
is requisite to the due exercise of human understanding. in his studies, 
he pursue an austere and more arduous path. he was much conversant 
with the history of religious opinions, and took pains to ascertain 
their validity. he deem it indispensable to examine the ground of 
his belief, to settle the relation between motive and actions, the 
criterion of merit, and the kind and property of evidence. 

there was an obvious resemblance between him and my father, in their 
conception of the importance of certain topics, and in the light in 
which the vicissitudes of human life were accustomed to be viewed. 
their character were similar, but the mind of the son was enrich by 
science, and embellish with literature. 

the temple was no long assign to its ancient use. from an italy 
adventurer, who erroneous imagine that he could find employment 
for his skill, and sale for his sculpture in america, my brother had 
purchase a bust of cicero. he professed to have copy this piece from 
an antique dug up with his own hand in the environs of modena. of the 
truth of his assertion we were not qualified to judge; but the marble 
was pure and polished, and we were contented to admire the performance, 
without wait for the sanction of connoisseurs. we hire the same 
artist to hew a suitable pedestal from a neighbouring quarry. this was 
placed in the temple, and the bust rest upon it. opposite to this was 
a harpsichord, sheltered by a temporary roof from the weather. this was 
the place of resort in the evenings of summer. here we sung, and talked, 
and read, and occasional banqueted. every joyous and tender scene most 
dear to my memory, is connected with this edifice. here the performance 
of our musical and poetical ancestor were rehearsed. here my brother's 
children received the rudiments of their education; here a thousand 
conversations, pregnant with delight and improvement, took place; and 
here the social affection were accustomed to expand, and the tear of 
delicious sympathy to be shed. 

my brother was an indefatigable student. the author whom he read were 
numerous, but the chief object of his venerate was cicero. he was 
never tired of con and rehearse his productions. to understand 
them was not sufficient. he was anxious to discover the gesture and 
cadence with which they ought to be delivered. he was very scrupulous 
in select a true scheme of pronunciation for the latin tongue, and in 
adapt it to the words of his darling writer. his favorite occupation 
consist in embellish his rhetoric with all the proprieties of 
gesticulate and utterance. 

not contented with this, he was diligent in settle and restore the 
purity of the text. for this end, he collected all the edition and 
commentary that could be procured, and employ month of severe study 
in explore and compare them. he never betray more satisfaction 
than when he made a discovery of this kind. 

it was not till the addition of henry pleyel, my friend's only brother, 
to our society, that his passion for roman eloquence was countenance 
and foster by a sympathy of tastes. this young man had been some years 
in europe. we had separate at a very early age, and he was now return 
to spend the remainder of his days among us. 

our circle was greatly enliven by the accession of a new member. his 
conversation abound with novelty. his gaiety was almost boisterous, 
but was capable of yielding to a grave deportment when the occasion 
require it. his discernment was acute, but he was prone to view every 
object merely as supply material for mirth. his conception 
were ardent but ludicrous, and his memory, aided, as he honestly 
acknowledged, by his invention, was an inexhaustible fund of 
entertainment. 

his residence was at the same distance below the city as ours was above, 
but there seldom pass a day without our being favoured with a visit. 
my brother and he were endow with the same attachment to the latin 
writers; and pleyel was not behind his friend in his knowledge of the 
history and metaphysics of religion. their creeds, however, were in many 
respects opposite. where one discover only confirmation of his faith, 
the other could find nothing but reason for doubt. moral necessity, 
and calvinistic inspiration, were the prop on which my brother thought 
proper to repose. pleyel was the champion of intellectual liberty, and 
reject all guidance but that of his reason. their discussion were 
frequent, but, being manage with candour as well as with skill, they 
were always listen to by us with avid and benefit. 

pleyel, like his new friends, was fond of music and poetry. henceforth 
our concert consist of two violins, an harpsichord, and three voices. 
we were frequent remind how much happiness depend upon society. 
this new friend, though, before his arrival, we were sensible of no 
vacuity, could not now be spared. his departure would occasion a void 
which nothing could fill, and which would produce insupportable regret. 
even my brother, though his opinion were hourly assailed, and even the 
divinity of cicero contested, was captivate with his friend, and laid 
aside some part of his ancient gravity at pleyel's approach. 



chapter iv 


six years of uninterrupted happiness had roll away, since my brother's 
marriage. the sound of war had been heard, but it was at such a distance 
as to enhance our enjoyment by afford object of comparison. the 
indian were repulse on the one side, and canada was conquer on the 
other. revolution and battles, however calamitous to those who occupy 
the scene, contribute in some sort to our happiness, by agitate our 
mind with curiosity, and furnish cause of patriotic exultation. 
four children, three of whom were of an age to compensate, by their 
personal and mental progress, the care of which they had been, at a 
more helpless age, the objects, exercise my brother's tenderness. the 
fourth was a charming babe that promise to display the image of her 
mother, and enjoy perfect health. to these were add a sweet girl 
fourteen years old, who was love by all of us, with an affection more 
than parental. 

her mother's story was a mournful one. she had come hither from england 
when this child was an infant, alone, without friends, and without 
money. she appear to have embark in a hasty and clandestine 
manner. she pass three years of solitude and anguish under my aunt's 
protection, and die a martyr to woe; the source of which she could, by 
no importunities, be prevail upon to unfold. her education and manners 
bespoke her to be of no mean birth. her last moments were render 
serene, by the assurance she received from my aunt, that her daughter 
should experience the same protection that had been extend to herself. 

on my brother's marriage, it was agree that she should make a part of 
his family. i cannot do justice to the attraction of this girl. perhaps 
the tender she excited might partly originate in her personal 
resemblance to her mother, whose character and misfortune were 
still fresh in our remembrance. she was habitual pensive, and this 
circumstance tend to remind the spectator of her friendless condition; 
and yet that epithet was surely misapply in this case. this being was 
cherish by those with whom she now resided, with unspeakable fondness. 
every exertion was made to enlarge and improve her mind. her safety 
was the object of a solicitude that almost exceed the bounds of 
discretion. our affection indeed could scarcely transcend her merits. 
she never met my eye, or occur to my reflections, without exciting 
a kind of enthusiasm. her softness, her intelligence, her equanimity, 
never shall i see surpassed. i have often shed tear of pleasure at her 
approach, and pressed her to my bosom in an agony of fondness. 

while every day was add to the charm of her person, and the stores 
of her mind, there occur an event which threaten to deprive us 
of her. an officer of some rank, who had been disabled by a wound 
at quebec, had employ himself, since the ratify of peace, in 
travel through the colonies. he remain a considerable period at 
philadelphia, but was at last prepare for his departure. no one had 
been more frequent honour with his visit than mrs. baynton, a 
worthy lady with whom our family were intimate. he went to her house 
with a view to perform a farewell visit, and was on the point of taking 
his leave, when i and my young friend enter the apartment. it is 
impossible to describe the emotion of the stranger, when he fixed his 
eyes upon my companion. he was motionless with surprise. he was unable 
to conceal his feelings, but sat silent gaze at the spectacle before 
him. at length he turn to mrs. baynton, and more by his looks and 
gesture than by words, besought her for an explanation of the scene. 
he seize the hand of the girl, who, in her turn, was surprise by his 
behaviour, and drawing her forward, said in an eager and faultere 
tone, who is she? whence do she come? what is her name? 

the answer that were given only increase the confusion of his 
thoughts. he was successive told, that she was the daughter of one 
whose name was louisa conway, who arrive among us at such a time, who 
sedulous conceal her parentage, and the motive of her flight, whose 
incurable grief had finally destroy her, and who had left this child 
under the protection of her friends. have heard the tale, he melt 
into tears, eager clasp the young lady in his arms, and call 
himself her father. when the tumult excited in his breast by this 
unlooked-for meeting were somewhat subsided, he gratify our curiosity 
by relate the following incidents. 

"miss conway was the only daughter of a banker in london, who discharge 
towards her every duty of an affectionate father. he had chance to fall 
into her company, had been subdued by her attractions, had tender her 
his hand, and been joyful accept both by parent and child. his wife 
had given him every proof of the fondest attachment. her father, who 
possessed immense wealth, treat him with distinguished respect, 
liberally supply his wants, and had made one condition of his consent 
to their union, a resolution to take up their abode with him. 

"they had pass three years of conjugal felicity, which had been 
augment by the birth of this child; when his professional duty call 
him into germany. it was not without an arduous struggle, that she was 
persuade to relinquish the design of accompany him through all the 
toils and peril of war. no parting was ever more distressful. they 
strove to alleviate, by frequent letters, the evil of their lot. those 
of his wife, breathe nothing but anxiety for his safety, and impatience 
of his absence. at length, a new arrangement was made, and he was 
oblige to repair from westphalia to canada. one advantage attend this 
change. it afford him an opportunity of meeting his family. his 
wife anticipate this interview, with no less rapture than himself. he 
hurried to london, and the moment he alight from the stage-coach, ran 
with all speed to mr. conway's house. 

"it was an house of mourning. his father was overwhelm with grief, and 
incapable of answer his inquiries. the servants, sorrowful and mute, 
were equally refractory. he explore the house, and call on the name 
of his wife and daughter, but his summons was fruitless. at length, 
this new disaster was explained. two days before his arrival, his wife's 
chamber was found empty. no search, however diligent and anxious, could 
trace her steps. no cause could be assign for her disappearance. the 
mother and child had flee away together. 

"new exertion were made, her chamber and cabinet were ransacked, but 
no vestige was found serving to inform them as to the motive of her 
flight, whether it had been voluntary or otherwise, and in what corner 
of the kingdom or of the world she was concealed. who shall describe the 
sorrow and amaze of the husband? his restlessness, his vicissitudes 
of hope and fear, and his ultimate despair? his duty call him to 
america. he had been in this city, and had frequent pass the door of 
the house in which his wife, at that moment, resided. her father had not 
remit his exertion to elucidate this painful mystery, but they had 
failed. this disappointment hasten his death; in consequence of which, 
louisa's father became possessor of his immense property." 

this tale was a copious theme of speculation. a thousand question were 
start and discuss in our domestic circle, respecting the motive 
that influence mrs. stuart to abandon her country. it did not appear 
that her proceeding was involuntary. we recall and review every 
particular that had fallen under our own observation. by none of these 
were we furnish with a clue. her conduct, after the most rigorous 
scrutiny, still remain an impenetrable secret. on a near view, major 
stuart prove himself a man of most amiable character. his attachment 
to louisa appear hourly to increase. she was no stranger to the 
sentiment suitable to her new character. she could not but readily 
embrace the scheme which was propose to her, to return with her father 
to england. this scheme his regard for her induce him, however, to 
postpone. some time was necessary to prepare her for so great a change 
and enable her to think without agony of her separation from us. 

i was not without hope of prevailing on her father entire to 
relinquish this unwelcome design. meanwhile, he pursue his travels 
through the southern colonies, and his daughter continue with us. 
louisa and my brother frequent received letters from him, which 
indicate a mind of no common order. they were fill with amuse 
details, and profound reflections. while here, he often partook of 
our evening conversation at the temple; and since his departure, his 
correspondence had frequent supply us with topic of discourse. 

one afternoon in may, the bland of the air, and bright of the 
verdure, induce us to assemble, early than usual, in the temple. 
we female were busy at the needle, while my brother and pleyel were 
bandy quotation and syllogisms. the point discuss was the merit of 
the oration for cluentius, as descriptive, first, of the genius of the 
speaker; and, secondly, of the manners of the times. pleyel labour to 
extenuate both these species of merit, and task his ingenuity, to shew 
that the orator had embrace a bad cause; or, at least, a doubtful one. 
he urged, that to rely on the exaggeration of an advocate, or to 
make the picture of a single family a model from which to sketch the 
condition of a nation, was absurd. the controversy was sudden divert 
into a new channel, by a misquotation. pleyel accused his companion of 
saying "polliciatur" when he should have said "polliceretur." nothing 
would decide the contest, but an appeal to the volume. my brother was 
return to the house for this purpose, when a servant met him with 
a letter from major stuart. he immediately return to read it in our 
company. 

besides affectionate compliments to us, and paternal benediction 
on louisa, his letter contained a description of a waterfall on the 
monongahela. a sudden gust of rain falling, we were compel to remove 
to the house. the storm pass away, and a radiant moon-light succeeded. 
there was no motion to resume our seat in the temple. we therefore 
remain where we were, and engaged in sprightly conversation. the 
letter lately received naturally suggest the topic. a parallel was 
drawn between the cataract there described, and one which pleyel had 
discover among the alps of glarus. in the state of the former, some 
particular was mentioned, the truth of which was questionable. to settle 
the dispute which thence arose, it was propose to have recourse to the 
letter. my brother search for it in his pocket. it was no where to be 
found. at length, he remember to have left it in the temple, and he 
determined to go in search of it. his wife, pleyel, louisa, and myself, 
remain where we were. 

in a few minutes he returned. i was somewhat interested in the dispute, 
and was therefore impatient for his return; yet, as i heard him 
ascend the stairs, i could not but remark, that he had execute his 
intention with remarkable dispatch. my eyes were fixed upon him on his 
entrance. methought he brought with him looks considerably different 
from those with which he departed. wonder, and a slight portion of 
anxiety were mingle in them. his eyes seem to be in search of some 
object. they pass quick from one person to another, till they rest 
on his wife. she was seat in a careless attitude on the sofa, in the 
same spot as before. she had the same muslin in her hand, by which her 
attention was chiefly engrossed. 

the moment he saw her, his perplexity visibly increased. he quiet 
seat himself, and fix his eyes on the floor, appear to be 
absorb in meditation. these singular suspend the inquiry which 
i was prepare to make respecting the letter. in a short time, the 
company relinquish the subject which engaged them, and direct their 
attention to wieland. they thought that he only wait for a pause in 
the discourse, to produce the letter. the pause was uninterrupted by 
him. at length pleyel said, "well, i suppose you have found the letter." 

"no," said he, without any abate of his gravity, and look 
stedfast at his wife, "i did not mount the hill."--"why 
not?"--"catharine, have you not move from that spot since i left the 
room?"--she was affected with the solemnity of his manner, and lay 
down her work, answer in a tone of surprise, "no; why do you ask that 
question?"--his eyes were again fixed upon the floor, and he did not 
immediately answer. at length, he said, look round upon us, "is it 
true that catharine did not follow me to the hill? that she did not just 
now enter the room?"--we assured him, with one voice, that she had not 
been absent for a moment, and inquire into the motive of his questions. 

"your assurances," said he, "are solemn and unanimous; and yet i must 
deny credit to your assertions, or disbelieve the testimony of my 
senses, which informed me, when i was half way up the hill, that 
catharine was at the bottom." 

we were confounded at this declaration. pleyel rally him with great 
levity on his behaviour. he listen to his friend with calmness, but 
without any relaxation of features. 

"one thing," said he with emphasis, "is true; either i heard my 
wife's voice at the bottom of the hill, or i do not hear your voice at 
present." 

"truly," return pleyel, "it is a sad dilemma to which you have reduce 
yourself. certain it is, if our eyes can give us certainty that your 
wife has been sitting in that spot during every moment of your absence. 
you have heard her voice, you say, upon the hill. in general, her voice, 
like her temper, is all softness. to be heard across the room, she is 
oblige to exert herself. while you were gone, if i mistake not, she did 
not utter a word. clara and i had all the talk to ourselves. still it 
may be that she held a whisper conference with you on the hill; but 
tell us the particulars." 

"the conference," said he, "was short; and far from being carry on in 
a whisper. you know with what intention i left the house. half way to 
the rock, the moon was for a moment hidden from us by a cloud. i never 
knew the air to be more bland and more calm. in this interval i glance 
at the temple, and thought i saw a glimmer between the columns. it 
was so faint, that it would not perhaps have been visible, if the moon 
had not been shrowded. i look again, but saw nothing. i never visit 
this building alone, or at night, without being remind of the fate 
of my father. there was nothing wonderful in this appearance; yet it 
suggest something more than mere solitude and dark in the same 
place would have done. 

"i kept on my way. the image that haunt me were solemn; and i 
entertain an imperfect curiosity, but no fear, as to the nature of 
this object. i had ascend the hill little more than half way, when a 
voice call me from behind. the accent were clear, distinct, powerful, 
and were uttered, as i fully believed, by my wife. her voice is 
not commonly so loud. she has seldom occasion to exert it, but, 
nevertheless, i have sometimes heard her call with force and eagerness. 
if my ear was not deceived, it was her voice which i heard. 

"stop, go no further. there is danger in your path." the sudden 
and unexpected of this warning, the tone of alarm with which it was 
given, and, above all, the persuasion that it was my wife who spoke, 
were enough to disconcert and make me pause. i turn and listen to 
assure myself that i was not mistaken. the deepest silence succeeded. at 
length, i spoke in my turn. who calls? is it you, catharine? i stop 
and presently received an answer. "yes, it is i; go not up; return 
instantly; you are want at the house." still the voice was 
catharine's, and still it proceed from the foot of the stairs. 

"what could i do? the warning was mysterious. to be utter by catharine 
at a place, and on an occasion like these, enhance the mystery. i could 
do nothing but obey. accordingly, i trod back my steps, expect that 
she wait for me at the bottom of the hill. when i reach the 
bottom, no one was visible. the moon-light was once more universal and 
brilliant, and yet, as far as i could see no human or moving figure 
was discernible. if she had return to the house, she must have use 
wondrous expedition to have pass already beyond the reach of my eye. 
i exert my voice, but in vain. to my repeated exclamations, no answer 
was returned. 

"ruminating on these incidents, i return hither. there was no room 
to doubt that i had heard my wife's voice; attend incident were not 
easily explained; but you now assure me that nothing extraordinary has 
happen to urge my return, and that my wife has not move from her 
seat." 

such was my brother's narrative. it was heard by us with different 
emotions. pleyel did not scruple to regard the whole as a deception of 
the senses. perhaps a voice had been heard; but wieland's imagination 
had misle him in supposing a resemblance to that of his wife, and 
give such a signification to the sounds. according to his custom 
he spoke what he thought. sometimes, he made it the theme of grave 
discussion, but more frequent treat it with ridicule. he did not 
believe that sober reasoning would convince his friend, and gaiety, he 
thought, was useful to take away the solemnity which, in a mind like 
wieland's, an accident of this kind was calculate to produce. 

pleyel propose to go in search of the letter. he went and speedy 
returned, bearing it in his hand. he had found it open on the pedestal; 
and neither voice nor visage had risen to impede his design. 

catharine was endow with an uncommon portion of good sense; but her 
mind was accessible, on this quarter, to wonder and panic. that her 
voice should be thus inexplicably and unwarrantable assumed, was a 
source of no small disquietude. she admitted the plausible of the 
argument by which pleyel endeavour to prove, that this was no more 
than an auricular deception; but this conviction was sure to be shaken, 
when she turn her eyes upon her husband, and perceive that pleyel's 
logic was far from have produce the same effect upon him. 

as to myself, my attention was engaged by this occurrence. i could not 
fail to perceive a shadowy resemblance between it and my father's death. 
on the latter event, i had frequent reflected; my reflection never 
conduct me to certainty, but the doubt that exist were not of a 
torment kind. i could not deny that the event was miraculous, and 
yet i was invincible averse to that method of solution. my wonder was 
excited by the inscrutable of the cause, but my wonder was unmixed 
with sorrow or fear. it begat in me a thrilling, and not unplease 
solemnity. similar to these were the sensation produce by the recent 
adventure. 

but its effect upon my brother's imagination was of chief moment. 
all that was desirable was, that it should be regard by him with 
indifference. the worst effect that could flow, was not indeed very 
formidable. yet i could not bear to think that his senses should be the 
victim of such delusion. it argue a disease condition of his frame, 
which might show itself hereafter in more dangerous symptoms. the will 
is the tool of the understanding, which must fashion its conclusion 
on the notice of sense. if the senses be depraved, it is impossible to 
calculate the evil that may flow from the consequent deduction of the 
understanding. 

i said, this man is of an ardent and melancholy character. those idea 
which, in others, are casual or obscure, which are entertain in 
moments of abstraction and solitude, and easily escape when the scene is 
changed, have obtain an immoveable hold upon his mind. the conclusion 
which long habit has render familiar, and, in some sort, palpable to 
his intellect, are drawn from the deepest sources. all his action and 
practical sentiment are link with long and abstruse deduction 
from the system of divine government and the law of our intellectual 
constitution. he is, in some respects, an enthusiast, but is fortify 
in his belief by innumerable argument and subtilties. 

his father's death was always regard by him as flowing from a direct 
and supernatural decree. it visit his meditation often than it did 
mine. the trace which it left were more gloomy and permanent. this new 
incident had a visible effect in augment his gravity. he was less 
disposed than formerly to converse and reading. when we sift his 
thoughts, they were generally found to have a relation, more or less 
direct, with this incident. it was difficult to ascertain the exact 
species of impression which it made upon him. he never introduce the 
subject into conversation, and listen with a silent and half-serious 
smile to the satirical effusion of pleyel. 

one evening we chance to be alone together in the temple. i seize that 
opportunity of investigate the state of his thoughts. after a pause, 
which he seem in no wise inclined to interrupt, i spoke to him--"how 
almost palpable is this dark; yet a ray from above would dispel it." 
"ay," said wieland, with fervor, "not only the physical, but moral night 
would be dispelled." "but why," said i, "must the divine will address 
its precept to the eye?" he smile significantly. "true," said he, "the 
understanding has other avenues." "you have never," said i, approach 
near to the point--"you have never told me in what way you considered 
the late extraordinary incident." "there is no determinate way in which 
the subject can be viewed. here is an effect, but the cause is utterly 
inscrutable. to suppose a deception will not do. such is possible, but 
there are twenty other supposition more probable. they must all be set 
aside before we reach that point." "what are these twenty suppositions?" 
"it is needless to mention them. they are only less improbable than 
pleyel's. time may convert one of them into certainty. till then it is 
useless to expatiate on them." 



chapter v 


some time had elapse when there happen another occurrence, still more 
remarkable. pleyel, on his return from europe, brought information of 
considerable importance to my brother. my ancestor were noble saxons, 
and possessed large domain in lusatia. the prussian war had destroy 
those person whose right to these estate preclude my brother's. 
pleyel had been exact in his inquiries, and had discover that, by the 
law of male-primogeniture, my brother's claim were superior to those 
of any other person now living. nothing was wanting but his presence in 
that country, and a legal application to establish this claim. 

pleyel strenuous recommend this measure. the advantage he thought 
attend it were numerous, and it would argue the utmost folly to 
neglect them. contrary to his expectation he found my brother averse 
to the scheme. slight efforts, he, at first, thought would subdue his 
reluctance; but he found this aversion by no means slight. the interest 
that he took in the happiness of his friend and his sister, and his own 
partiality to the saxon soil, from which he had likewise sprung, and 
where he had spent several years of his youth, made him redouble his 
exertion to win wieland's consent. for this end he employ every 
argument that his invention could suggest. he painted, in attractive 
colours, the state of manners and government in that country, the 
security of civil rights, and the freedom of religious sentiments. he 
dwelt on the privilege of wealth and rank, and drew from the servile 
condition of one class, an argument in favor of his scheme, since the 
revenue and power annexe to a germany principality afford so large a 
field for benevolence. the evil flowing from this power, in malignant 
hands, was proportion to the good that would arise from the virtuous 
use of it. hence, wieland, in forbearing to claim his own, withheld all 
the positive felicity that would accrue to his vassal from his success, 
and hazard all the misery that would redound from a less enlightened 
proprietor. 

it was easy for my brother to repel these arguments, and to shew that no 
spot on the globe enjoy equal security and liberty to that which he 
at present inhabited. that if the saxon had nothing to fear from 
mis-government, the external cause of havoc and alarm were numerous and 
manifest. the recent devastate committed by the prussian furnish 
a specimen of these. the horrors of war would always impend over them, 
till germany were seize and divide by austria and prussian tyrants; 
an event which he strong suspect was at no great distance. but 
setting these consideration aside, was it laudable to grasp at wealth 
and power even when they were within our reach? were not these the two 
great source of depravity? what security had he, that in this change 
of place and condition, he should not degenerate into a tyrant and 
voluptuary? power and riches were chiefly to be dread on account of 
their tendency to deprave the possessor. he held them in abhorrence, not 
only as instrument of misery to others, but to him on whom they 
were conferred. besides, riches were comparative, and was he not rich 
already? he live at present in the bosom of security and luxury. all 
the instrument of pleasure, on which his reason or imagination set any 
value, were within his reach. but these he must forego, for the sake of 
advantage which, whatever were their value, were as yet uncertain. in 
pursuit of an imaginary addition to his wealth, he must reduce himself 
to poverty, he must exchange present certainty for what was distant 
and contingent; for who know not that the law is a system of expence, 
delay and uncertainty? if he should embrace this scheme, it would lay 
him under the necessity of making a voyage to europe, and remain for 
a certain period, separate from his family. he must undergo the peril 
and discomfort of the ocean; he must divest himself of all domestic 
pleasures; he must deprive his wife of her companion, and his children 
of a father and instructor, and all for what? for the ambiguous 
advantage which overgrown wealth and flagitious tyranny have to bestow? 
for a precarious possession in a land of turbulence and war? advantages, 
which will not certainly be gained, and of which the acquisition, if it 
were sure, is necessarily distant. 

pleyel was enamoured of his scheme on account of its intrinsic benefits, 
but, likewise, for other reasons. his abode at leipsig made that country 
appear to him like home. he was connected with this place by many social 
ties. while there he had not escape the amorous contagion. but the 
lady, though her heart was impress in his favor, was compel to 
bestow her hand upon another. death had remove this impediment, and 
he was now invite by the lady herself to return. this he was of course 
determined to do, but was anxious to obtain the company of wieland; 
he could not bear to think of an eternal separation from his present 
associates. their interest, he thought, would be no less promote by the 
change than his own. hence he was importunate and indefatigable in his 
argument and solicitations. 

he knew that he could not hope for mine or his sister's ready 
concurrence in this scheme. should the subject be mention to us, we 
should league our effort against him, and strengthen that reluctance 
in wieland which already was sufficient difficult to conquer. he, 
therefore, anxious conceal from us his purpose. if wieland were 
previous enlist in his cause, he would find it a less difficult task 
to overcome our aversion. my brother was silent on this subject, because 
he believe himself in no danger of change his opinion, and he was 
willing to save us from any uneasiness. the mere mention of such 
a scheme, and the possibility of his embrace it, he knew, would 
considerably impair our tranquillity. 

one day, about three week subsequent to the mysterious call, it was 
agree that the family should be my guests. seldom had a day been pass 
by us, of more serene enjoyment. pleyel had promise us his company, but 
we did not see him till the sun had nearly declined. he brought with 
him a countenance that betoken disappointment and vexation. he did not 
wait for our inquiries, but immediately explain the cause. two days 
before a packet had arrive from hamburgh, by which he had flatter 
himself with the expectation of receiving letters, but no letters had 
arrived. i never saw him so much subdued by an untoward event. his 
thought were employ in account for the silence of his friends. 
he was seize with the torment of jealousy, and suspect nothing less 
than the infidelity of her to whom he had devoted his heart. the silence 
must have been concerted. her sickness, or absence, or death, would have 
increase the certainty of some one's have written. no supposition 
could be form but that his mistress had grown indifferent, or that she 
had transfer her affection to another. the miscarry of a letter 
was hardly within the reach of possibility. from leipsig to hamburgh, 
and from hamburgh hither, the conveyance was expose to no hazard. 

he had been so long detain in america chiefly in consequence of 
wieland's aversion to the scheme which he proposed. he now became more 
impatient than ever to return to europe. when he reflect that, by his 
delays, he had probably forfeit the affection of his mistress, his 
sensation amount to agony. it only remained, by his speedy departure, 
to repair, if possible, or prevent so intolerable an evil. already he 
had half resolve to embark in this very ship which, he was informed, 
would set out in a few week on her return. 

meanwhile he determined to make a new attempt to shake the resolution of 
wieland. the evening was somewhat advanced when he invite the latter

catharine, louisa and me, to amuse ourselves by the best means in our

his heart. he re-urged all his former arguments, and placed them in more 
forcible lights. 

they promise to return shortly; but hour after hour passed, and they

the absence of our friends excited some uneasy apprehensions. we were 
express our fears, and compare our conjecture as to what might be

watch their looks, but could not understand the emotion that were 
written in them. 

these appearances divert catharine's inquiry into a new channel. 
what did they mean, she asked, by their silence, and by their thus

and assume an air of indifference, frame some trifling excuse, at the 
same time dart significant glance at wieland, as if to caution him 
against disclose the truth. my brother said nothing, but deliver

and louisa, return home. pleyel proposed, of his own accord, to be 
my guest for the night. this circumstance, in addition to those which 
preceded, gave new edge to my wonder. 

as soon as we were left alone, pleyel's countenance assume an air of 
seriousness, and even consternation, which i had never before beheld in 
him. the steps with which he measured the floor betoken the trouble of 
his thoughts. my inquiry were suspend by the hope that he would give

i wait some time, but the confusion of his thought appear in no

unusual absence had occasioned, and which were increase by their 
behaviour since their return, and solicit an explanation. he stop

he said, to me, in a tone which faultere through the vehemence of his 
emotions, "how were you employ during our absence?" "in turning over 
the della crusca dictionary, and talk on different subjects; but 
just before your entrance, we were torment ourselves with omen and 
prognostick relative to your absence." "catherine was with you the 
whole time?" "yes." "but are you sure?" "most sure. she was not absent a 
moment." he stood, for a time, as if to assure himself of my sincerity. 
then, clinch his hands, and wildly lift them above his head, "lo," 
cry he, "i have news to tell you. the baroness de stolberg is dead?" 

this was her whom he loved. i was not surprise at the agitation which 
he betrayed. "but how was the information procured? how was the truth 
of this news connected with the circumstance of catharine's remain in 
our company?" he was for some time inattentive to my questions. when he 
spoke, it seem merely a continuation of the reverie into which he had 
been plunged. 

"and yet it might be a mere deception. but could both of us in that 
case have been deceived? a rare and prodigious coincidence! barely not 
impossible. and yet, if the accent be oracular--theresa is dead. no, 
no," continue he, covering his face with his hands, and in a tone half 
broken into sobs, "i cannot believe it. she has not written, but if 
she were dead, the faithful bertrand would have given me the earliest 
information. and yet if he knew his master, he must have easily guess 
at the effect of such tidings. in pity to me he was silent." 

"clara, forgive me; to you, this behaviour is mysterious. i will explain 
as well as i am able. but say not a word to catharine. her strength of 
mind is inferior to your's. she will, besides, have more reason to be 
startled. she is wieland's angel." 

pleyel proceed to inform me, for the first time, of the scheme which 
he had pressed, with so much earnestness, on my brother. he enumerate 
the objection which had been made, and the industry with which he 
had endeavour to confute them. he mention the effect upon his
