continue he, "i introduce the subject that was nearest my heart. 
i re-urged all my former arguments, and placed them in more forcible 
lights. wieland was still refractory. he expatiate on the peril of 
wealth and power, on the sacred of conjugal and parental duties, and 
the happiness of mediocrity. 

"no wonder that the time passed, unperceived, away. our whole soul were 
engaged in this cause. several times we came to the foot of the rock; 
as soon as we perceive it, we change our course, but never fail to 
terminate our circuitous and devious ramble at this spot. at length your 
brother observed, 'we seem to be led hither by a kind of fatality. since 
we are so near, let us ascend and rest ourselves a while. if you are not 
weary of this argument we will resume it there.' 

"i tacit consented. we mount the stairs, and drawing the sofa in 
front of the river, we seat ourselves upon it. i took up the thread of 
our discourse where we had drop it. i ridicule his dread of the sea, 
and his attachment to home. i kept on in this strain, so congenial with 
my disposition, for some time, uninterrupted by him. at length, he said 
to me, "suppose now that i, whom argument has not convinced, should 
yield to ridicule, and should agree that your scheme is eligible; what 
will you have gained? nothing. you have other enemy beside myself to 
encounter. when you have vanquish me, your toil has scarcely begun. 
there are my sister and wife, with whom it will remain for you to 
maintain the contest. and trust me, they are adversary whom all your 
force and stratagem will never subdue." i insinuate that they would 
model themselves by his will: that catharine would think obedience her 
duty. he answered, with some quickness, "you mistake. their concurrence 
is indispensable. it is not my custom to exact sacrifice of this kind. 
i live to be their protector and friend, and not their tyrant and foe. 
if my wife shall deem her happiness, and that of her children, most 
consult by remain where she is, here she shall remain." "but," said 
i, "when she know your pleasure, will she not conform to it?" before 
my friend had time to answer this question, a negative was clearly and 
distinct utter from another quarter. it did not come from one side 
or the other, from before us or behind. whence then did it come? by 
whose organ was it fashioned? 

"if any uncertainty had exist with regard to these particulars, it 
would have been remove by a deliberate and equally distinct repetition 
of the same monosyllable, "no." the voice was my sister's. it appear 
to come from the roof. i start from my seat. catharine, exclaim i, 
where are you? no answer was returned. i search the room, and the 
area before it, but in vain. your brother was motionless in his seat. 
i return to him, and placed myself again by his side. my astonishment 
was not less than his." 

"well," said he, at length, "what think you of this? this is the 
self-same voice which i formerly heard; you are now convinced that my 
ear were well informed." 

"yes," said i, "this, it is plain, is no fiction of the fancy." we again 
sunk into mutual and thoughtful silence. a recollection of the hour, and 
of the length of our absence, made me at last propose to return. we 
rose up for this purpose. in do this, my mind revert to the

cannot hope to prevail with my friends to accompany me. they may doze 
away their days on the bank of schuylkill, but as to me, i go in the 
next vessel; i will fly to her presence, and demand the reason of this 
extraordinary silence." 

"i had scarcely finished the sentence, when the same mysterious voice 
exclaimed, "you shall not go. the seal of death is on her lips. her 
silence is the silence of the tomb." think of the effects which accent 
like these must have had upon me. i shudder as i listened. as soon as 
i recover from my first amazement, "who is it that speaks?" said i, 
"whence did you procure these dismal tidings?" i did not wait long for 
an answer. "from a source that cannot fail. be satisfied. she is dead." 
you may just be surprised, that, in the circumstances in which i heard 
the tidings, and notwithstanding the mystery which environed him by whom 
they were imparted, i could give an undivided attention to the facts, 
which were the subject of our dialogue. i eager inquired, when and 
where did she die? what was the cause of her death? was her death 
absolutely certain? an answer was return only to the last of these 
questions. "yes," was pronounced by the same voice; but it now sound 
from a greater distance, and the deepest silence was all the return made 
to my subsequent interrogatories. 

"it was my sister's voice; but it could not be utter by her; and yet, 
if not by her, by whom was it uttered? when we return hither, and 
discover you together, the doubt that had previous exist was 
removed. it was manifest that the intimate came not from her. yet if 
not from her, from whom could it come? are the circumstances attend 
the impart of this news proof that the tidings are true? god forbid 
that they should be true." 

here pleyel sunk into anxious silence, and gave me leisure to ruminate 
on this inexplicable event. i am at a loss to describe the sensation 
that affected me. i am not fearful of shadows. the tale of apparition 
and enchantment did not possess that power over my belief which could 
even render them interesting. i saw nothing in them but ignorance and 
folly, and was a stranger even to that terror which is pleasing. but 
this incident was different from any that i had ever before known. here 
were proof of a sensible and intelligent existence, which could not 
be denied. here was information obtain and impart by means 
unquestionable super-human. 

that there are conscious beings, beside ourselves, in existence, whose 
mode of activity and information surpass our own, can scarcely be 
denied. is there a glimpse afford us into a world of these superior 
beings? my heart was scarcely large enough to give admittance to 
so swelling a thought. an awe, the sweetest and most solemn that 
imagination can conceive, pervade my whole frame. it forsook me not 
when i part from pleyel and retired to my chamber. an impulse was 
given to my spirits utterly incompatible with sleep. i pass the night 
wakeful and full of meditation. i was impress with the belief of 
mysterious, but not of malignant agency. hitherto nothing had occur 
to persuade me that this airy minister was busy to evil rather than to 
good purposes. on the contrary, the idea of superior virtue had always 
been associate in my mind with that of superior power. the warning 
that had thus been heard appear to have been prompt by beneficent 
intentions. my brother had been hinder by this voice from ascend 
the hill. he was told that danger lurk in his path, and his obedience 
to the intimate had perhaps save him from a destiny similar to that 
of my father. 

pleyel had been rescue from torment uncertainty, and from the 
hazard and fatigues of a fruitless voyage, by the same interposition. 
it had assured him of the death of his theresa. 

this woman was then dead. a confirmation of the tidings, if true, would 
speedy arrive. was this confirmation to be deprecate or desired? 
by her death, the tie that attach him to europe, was taken away. 
henceforward every motive would combine to retain him in his native 
country, and we were rescue from the deep regrets that would accompany 
his hopeless absence from us. propitious was the spirit that impart 
these tidings. propitious he would perhaps have been, if he had been 
instrumental in producing, as well as in communicate the tidings of 
her death. propitious to us, the friends of pleyel, to whom has thereby 
been secure the enjoyment of his society; and not unpropitious to 
himself; for though this object of his love be snatch away, is there 
not another who is able and willing to console him for her loss? 

twenty days after this, another vessel arrive from the same port. in 
this interval, pleyel, for the most part, estrange himself from his old 
companions. he was become the prey of a gloomy and unsociable grief. 
his walk were limited to the bank of the delaware. this bank is an 
artificial one. reeds and the river are on one side, and a watery marsh 
on the other, in that part which bound his lands, and which extend 
from the mouth of hollander's creek to that of schuylkill. no scene can 
be imagine less entice to a lover of the picturesque than this. the 
shore is deform with mud, and incumbere with a forest of reeds. the 
fields, in most seasons, are mire; but when they afford a firm footing, 
the ditch by which they are bound and intersected, are mantle with 
stagnate green, and emit the most noxious exhalations. health is no 
less a stranger to those seat than pleasure. spring and autumn are sure 
to be accompany with ague and bilious remittents. 

the scene which environed our dwelling at mettingen constitute the 
reverse of this. schuylkill was here a pure and translucid current, 
broken into wild and ceaseless music by rocky points, murmur on a 
sandy margin, and reflect on its surface, bank of all variety of 
height and degree of declivity. these bank were chequer by patch 
of dark verdure and shapeless masses of white marble, and crown by 
copse of cedar, or by the regular magnificence of orchards, which, at 
this season, were in blossom, and were prodigal of odours. the ground 
which recede from the river was scoop into valley and dales. its 
beauty were enhance by the horticulture skill of my brother, who 
bedeck this exquisite assemblage of slope and rising with every 
species of vegetable ornament, from the giant arms of the oak to the 
cluster tendril of the honey-suckle. 

to screen him from the unwholesome airs of his own residence, it had 
been propose to pleyel to spend the month of spring with us. he had 
apparently acquiesce in this proposal; but the late event induce him 
to change his purpose. he was only to be seen by visiting him in his 
retirements. his gaiety had flown, and every passion was absorb in 
eager to procure tidings from saxony. i have mention the arrival 
of another vessel from the elbe. he descry her early one morning as 
he was passing along the skirt of the river. she was easily recognized, 
being the ship in which he had perform his first voyage to germany. 
he immediately went on board, but found no letters direct to him. 
this omission was, in some degree, compensate by meeting with an old 
acquaintance among the passengers, who had till lately been a resident 
in leipsig. this person put an end to all suspense respecting the fate 
of theresa, by relate the particulars of her death and funeral. 

thus was the truth of the former intimate attested. no long devour 
by suspense, the grief of pleyel was not long in yielding to the 
influence of society. he gave himself up once more to our company. his 
vivace had indeed been damped; but even in this respect he was a more 
acceptable companion than formerly, since his serious was neither 
incommunicative nor sullen. 

these incidents, for a time, occupy all our thoughts. in me they 
produce a sentiment not unal to pleasure, and more speedy than in 
the case of my friends were intermixe with other topics. my brother was 
particularly affected by them. it was easy to perceive that most of his 
meditation were tincture from this source. to this was to be ascribe 
a design in which his pen was, at this period, engaged, of collect 
and investigate the fact which relate to that mysterious personage, 
the daemon of socrates. 

my brother's skill in greece and roman learning was exceed by that of 
few, and no doubt the world would have accept a treatise upon this 
subject from his hand with avidity; but alas! this and every other 
scheme of felicity and honor, were doom to sudden blast and hopeless 
extermination. 



chapter vi 


i now come to the mention of a person with whose name the most turbulent 
sensation are connected. it is with a shudder reluctance that i 
enter on the province of describe him. now it is that i begin to 
perceive the difficulty of the task which i have undertaken; but it 
would be weakness to shrink from it. my blood is congealed: and my 
finger are palsied when i call up his image. shame upon my cowardly and 
infirm heart! hitherto i have proceed with some degree of composure, 
but now i must pause. i mean not that dire remembrance shall subdue my 
courage or baffle my design, but this weakness cannot be immediately 
conquered. i must desist for a little while. 

i have taken a few turn in my chamber, and have gather strength 
enough to proceed. yet have i not project a task beyond my power to 
execute? if thus, on the very threshold of the scene, my knee fault 
and i sink, how shall i support myself, when i rush into the midst of 
horrors such as no heart has hitherto conceived, nor tongue related? i 
sicken and recoil at the prospect, and yet my irresolute is momentary. 
i have not form this design upon slight grounds, and though i may at 
times pause and hesitate, i will not be finally divert from it. 

and thou, o most fatal and potent of mankind, in what terms shall i 
describe thee? what words are adequate to the just delineate of thy 
character? how shall i detail the means which render the secrecy of 
thy purpose unfathomable? but i will not anticipate. let me recover 
if possible, a sober strain. let me keep down the flood of passion that 
would render me precipitate or powerless. let me stifle the agony that 
are awaken by thy name. let me, for a time, regard thee as a being 
of no terrible attributes. let me tear myself from contemplation of 
the evil of which it is but too certain that thou wast the author, and 
limit my view to those harmless appearances which attend thy entrance 
on the stage. 

one sunny afternoon, i was standing in the door of my house, when i 
marked a person passing close to the edge of the bank that was in 
front. his pace was a careless and lingering one, and had none of that 
graceful and ease which distinguish a person with certain advantage 
of education from a clown. his gait was rustic and aukward. his form was 
ungainly and disproportioned. shoulder broad and square, breast sunken, 
his head drooping, his body of uniform breadth, support by long and 
lank legs, were the ingredient of his frame. his garb was not ill 
adapt to such a figure. a slouch hat, tarnish by the weather, a 
coat of thick grey cloth, cut and wrought, as it seemed, by a country 
tailor, blue worsted stockings, and shoe fasten by thongs, and deep 
discolour by dust, which brush had never disturbed, constitute his 
dress. 

there was nothing remarkable in these appearances; they were frequent 
to be met with on the road, and in the harvest field. i cannot tell why 
i gaze upon them, on this occasion, with more than ordinary attention, 
unless it were that such figures were seldom seen by me, except on the 
road or field. this lawn was only traverse by men whose view were 
direct to the pleasure of the walk, or the grandeur of the scenery. 

he pass slow along, frequent pausing, as if to examine the 
prospect more deliberately, but never turning his eye towards the house, 
so as to allow me a view of his countenance. presently, he enter a 
copse at a small distance, and disappeared. my eye follow him while 
he remain in sight. if his image remain for any duration in my fancy 
after his departure, it was because no other object occur sufficient 
to expel it. 

i continue in the same spot for half an hour, vaguely, and by fits, 
contemplate the image of this wanderer, and drawing, from outward 
appearances, those inference with respect to the intellectual history 
of this person, which experience afford us. i reflect on the 
alliance which commonly subsist between ignorance and the practice 
of agriculture, and indulge myself in airy speculation as to the 
influence of progressive knowledge in dissolve this alliance, and 
embody the dream of the poets. i ask why the plough and the hoe 
might not become the trade of every human being, and how this 
trade might be made conducive to, or, at least, consistent with the 
acquisition of wisdom and eloquence. 

weary with these reflections, i return to the kitchen to perform some 
household office. i had usually but one servant, and she was a girl 
about my own age. i was busy near the chimney, and she was employ near 
the door of the apartment, when some one knocked. the door was open by 
her, and she was immediately address with "pry'thee, good girl, canst 
thou supply a thirsty man with a glass of buttermilk?" she answer 
that there was none in the house. "aye, but there is some in the dairy 
yonder. thou knowest as well as i, though herme never taught thee, that 
though every dairy be an house, every house is not a dairy." to 
this speech, though she understood only a part of it, she reply 
by repeating her assurances, that she had none to give. "well then," 
rejoin the stranger, "for charity's sweet sake, hand me forth a cup 
of cold water." the girl said she would go to the spring and fetch it. 
"nay, give me the cup, and suffer me to help myself. neither manacle 
nor lame, i should merit burial in the maw of carrion crows, if i laid 
this task upon thee." she gave him the cup, and he turn to go to the 
spring. 

i listen to this dialogue in silence. the words utter by the person 
without, affected me as somewhat singular, but what chiefly render 
them remarkable, was the tone that accompany them. it was wholly new. 
my brother's voice and pleyel's were musical and energetic. i had fondly 
imagined, that, in this respect, they were surpass by none. now my 
mistake was detected. i cannot pretend to communicate the impression 
that was made upon me by these accents, or to depict the degree in which 
force and sweet were blend in them. they were articulated with a 
distinct that was unexampled in my experience. but this was not all. 
the voice was not only mellifluent and clear, but the emphasis was so 
just, and the modulation so impassioned, that it seem as if an heart 
of stone could not fail of being move by it. it impart to me an 
emotion altogether involuntary and incontroulable. when he utter the 
words "for charity's sweet sake," i drop the cloth that i held in 
my hand, my heart overflow with sympathy, and my eyes with unbidden 
tears. 

this description will appear to you trifling or incredible. the 
importance of these circumstances will be manifest in the sequel. 
the manner in which i was affected on this occasion, was, to my own 
apprehension, a subject of astonishment. the tone were indeed such as 
i never heard before; but that they should, in an instant, as it were, 
dissolve me in tears, will not easily be believe by others, and can 
scarcely be comprehend by myself. 

it will be readily supposed that i was somewhat inquisitive as to the 
person and demeanour of our visitant. after a moment's pause, i step 
to the door and look after him. judge my surprize, when i beheld the 
self-same figure that had appear an half hour before upon the bank. my 
fancy had conjure up a very different image. a form, and attitude, and 
garb, were instantly create worthy to accompany such elocution; but 
this person was, in all visible respects, the reverse of this phantom. 
strange as it may seem, i could not speedy reconcile myself to this 
disappointment. instead of return to my employment, i threw myself 
in a chair that was placed opposite the door, and sunk into a fit of 
musing. 

my attention was, in a few minutes, recall by the stranger, who 
return with the empty cup in his hand. i had not thought of the 
circumstance, or should certainly have chosen a different seat. he no 
soon shew himself, than a confuse sense of impropriety, add to 
the sudden of the interview, for which, not have foreseen it, 
i had made no preparation, threw me into a state of the most painful 
embarrassment. he brought with him a placid brow; but no soon had he 
cast his eyes upon me, than his face was as glowing suffuse as 
my own. he placed the cup upon the bench, stammer out thanks, and 
retired. 

it was some time before i could recover my wonted composure. i had 
snatch a view of the stranger's countenance. the impression that it 
made was vivid and indelible. his cheek were pallid and lank, his eyes 
sunken, his forehead overshadow by coarse straggle hairs, his teeth 
large and irregular, though sound and brilliant white, and his chin 
discolour by a tetter. his skin was of coarse grain, and sallow hue. 
every feature was wide of beauty, and the outline of his face remind 
you of an invert cone. 

and yet his forehead, so far as shaggy locks would allow it to be seen, 
his eyes lustrous black, and possessing, in the midst of haggardness, 
a radiance inexpressible serene and potent, and something in the rest of 
his features, which it would be in vain to describe, but which serve to 
betoken a mind of the highest order, were essential ingredient in the 
portrait. this, in the effects which immediately flow from it, i count 
among the most extraordinary incident of my life. this face, seen for 
a moment, continue for hour to occupy my fancy, to the exclusion of 
almost every other image. i had purpose to spend the evening with my 
brother, but i could not resist the inclination of form a sketch 
upon paper of this memorable visage. whether my hand was aid by any 
peculiar inspiration, or i was deceive by my own fond conceptions, this 
portrait, though hasty executed, appear unexceptionable to my own 
taste. 

i placed it at all distances, and in all lights; my eyes were rivet 
upon it. half the night pass away in wakeful and in contemplation 
of this picture. so flexible, and yet so stubborn, is the human mind. 
so obedient to impulse the most transient and brief, and yet so 
unalterable observant of the direction which is given to it! how little 
did i then foresee the termination of that chain, of which this may be 
regard as the first link? 

next day arose in dark and storm. torrent of rain fell during 
the whole day, attend with incessant thunder, which reverberate in 
stunning echo from the opposite declivity. the inclement of the air 
would not allow me to walk-out. i had, indeed, no inclination to leave 
my apartment. i betook myself to the contemplation of this portrait, 
whose attraction time had rather enhance than diminished. i laid aside 
my usual occupations, and seating myself at a window, consume the day 
in alternate look out upon the storm, and gaze at the picture 
which lay upon a table before me. you will, perhaps, deem this conduct 
somewhat singular, and ascribe it to certain peculiarity of temper. i 
am not aware of any such peculiarities. i can account for my devotion to 
this image no otherwise, than by supposing that its property were 
rare and prodigious. perhaps you will suspect that such were the 
first inroad of a passion incident to every female heart, and 
which frequent gain a footing by means even more slight, and more 
improbable than these. i shall not controvert the reasonable of the 
suspicion, but leave you at liberty to draw, from my narrative, what 
conclusion you please. 

night at length returned, and the storm ceased. the air was once more 
clear and calm, and bore an affecting contrast to that uproar of the 
elements by which it had been preceded. i spent the darksome hours, as 
i spent the day, contemplative and seat at the window. why was my mind 
absorb in thought ominous and dreary? why did my bosom heave with 
sighs, and my eyes overflow with tears? was the tempest that had just 
past a signal of the ruin which impende over me? my soul fondly dwelt 
upon the image of my brother and his children, yet they only increase 
the mournful of my contemplations. the smile of the charming babe 
were as bland as formerly. the same dignity sat on the brow of their 
father, and yet i thought of them with anguish. something whisper 
that the happiness we at present enjoy was set on mutable foundations. 
death must happen to all. whether our felicity was to be subvert by it 
to-morrow, or whether it was ordain that we should lay down our head 
full of years and of honor, was a question that no human being could 
solve. at other times, these idea seldom intruded. i either forbore to 
reflect upon the destiny that is reserved for all men, or the reflection 
was mixed up with image that disrobe it of terror; but now the 
uncertainty of life occur to me without any of its usual and 
alleviate accompaniments. i said to myself, we must die. soon or 
later, we must disappear for ever from the face of the earth. whatever 
be the links that hold us to life, they must be broken. this scene 
of existence is, in all its parts, calamitous. the greater number is 
oppress with immediate evils, and those, the tide of whose fortune is 
full, how small is their portion of enjoyment, since they know that it 
will terminate. 

for some time i indulge myself, without reluctance, in these gloomy 
thoughts; but at length, the dejection which they produce became 
insupportable painful. i endeavour to dissipate it with music. i had 
all my grand-father's melody as well as poetry by rote. i now light 
by chance on a ballad, which commemorate the fate of a germany cavalier, 
who fell at the siege of nice under godfrey of bouillon. my choice was 
unfortunate, for the scene of violence and carnage which were here 
wildly but forcibly pourtrayed, only suggest to my thought a new 
topic in the horrors of war. 

i sought refuge, but ineffectually, in sleep. my mind was throng by 
vivid, but confuse images, and no effort that i made was sufficient to 
drive them away. in this situation i heard the clock, which hung in 
the room, give the signal for twelve. it was the same instrument which 
formerly hung in my father's chamber, and which, on account of its 
being his workmanship, was regarded, by every one of our family, with 
veneration. it had fallen to me, in the division of his property, and 
was placed in this asylum. the sound awaken a series of reflections, 
respecting his death. i was not allow to pursue them; for scarcely 
had the vibration ceased, when my attention was attract by a whisper, 
which, at first, appear to proceed from lip that were laid close to 
my ear. 

no wonder that a circumstance like this startle me. in the first 
impulse of my terror, i utter a slight scream, and shrunk to the 
opposite side of the bed. in a moment, however, i recover from my 
trepidation. i was habitual indifferent to all the cause of fear, 
by which the majority are afflicted. i entertain no apprehension 
of either ghost or robbers. our security had never been molest by 
either, and i made use of no means to prevent or counterwork their 
machinations. my tranquillity, on this occasion, was quick retrieved. 
the whisper evidently proceed from one who was post at my bed-side. 
the first idea that suggest itself was, that it was utter by the 
girl who live with me as a servant. perhaps, somewhat had alarm her, 
or she was sick, and had come to request my assistance. by whisper in 
my ear, she intended to rouse without alarm me. 

full of this persuasion, i called; "judith," said i, "is it you? what 
do you want? is there any thing the matter with you?" no answer was 
returned. i repeated my inquiry, but equally in vain. cloudy as was the 
atmosphere, and curtain as my bed was, nothing was visible. i withdrew 
the curtain, and leaning my head on my elbow, i listen with the 
deepest attention to catch some new sound. meanwhile, i ran over in my 
thoughts, every circumstance that could assist my conjectures. 

my habitation was a wooden edifice, consist of two stories. in each 
story were two rooms, separate by an entry, or middle passage, with 
which they communicate by opposite doors. the passage, on the lower 
story, had door at the two ends, and a stair-case. window answer to 
the door on the upper story. annexe to this, on the eastern side, were 
wings, divided, in like manner, into an upper and lower room; one of 
them comprize a kitchen, and chamber above it for the servant, and 
communicated, on both stories, with the parlour adjoin it below, 
and the chamber adjoin it above. the opposite wing is of small 
dimensions, the rooms not being above eight feet square. the lower of 
these was use as a depository of household implements, the upper was a 
closet in which i deposit my books and papers. they had but one inlet, 
which was from the room adjoining. there was no window in the lower one, 
and in the upper, a small aperture which communicate light and air, but 
would scarcely admit the body. the door which led into this, was close 
to my bed-head, and was always locked, but when i myself was within. the 
avenue below were accustomed to be closed and bolt at nights. 

the maid was my only companion, and she could not reach my chamber 
without previous passing through the opposite chamber, and the middle 
passage, of which, however, the door were usually unfastened. if she 
had occasion this noise, she would have answer my repeated calls. 
no other conclusion, therefore, was left me, but that i had mistaken the 
sounds, and that my imagination had transform some casual noise into 
the voice of a human creature. satisfy with this solution, i was 
prepare to relinquish my listen attitude, when my ear was again 
salute with a new and yet loud whispering. it appeared, as before, 
to issue from lip that touched my pillow. a second effort of attention, 
however, clearly shew me, that the sound issue from within the 
closet, the door of which was not more than eight inch from my pillow. 

this second interrupt occasion a shock less vehement than the 
former. i started, but gave no audible token of alarm. i was so much 
mistress of my feelings, as to continue listen to what should be 
said. the whisper was distinct, hoarse, and utter so as to shew that 
the speaker was desirous of being heard by some one near, but, at the 
same time, studious to avoid being overheard by any other. 

"stop, stop, i say; madman as you are! there are better means than that. 
curse upon your rashness! there is no need to shoot." 

such were the words utter in a tone of eager and anger, within so 
small a distance of my pillow. what construction could i put upon 
them? my heart began to palpitate with dread of some unknown danger. 
presently, another voice, but equally near me, was heard whisper in 
answer. "why not? i will draw a trigger in this business, but perdition 
be my lot if i do more." to this, the first voice returned, in a tone 
which rage had heighten in a small degree above a whisper, "coward! 
stand aside, and see me do it. i will grasp her throat; i will do her 
business in an instant; she shall not have time so much as to groan." 
what wonder that i was petrify by sound so dreadful! murder 
lurk in my closet. they were plan the means of my destruction. one 
resolve to shoot, and the other menace suffocation. their means being 
chosen, they would forthwith break the door. flight instantly suggest 
itself as most eligible in circumstances so perilous. i deliberate not 
a moment; but, fear add wings to my speed, i leap out of bed, and 
scanty robe as i was, rush out of the chamber, down stairs, and 
into the open air. i can hardly recollect the process of turning 
keys, and withdraw bolts. my terror urge me forward with almost a 
mechanical impulse. i stop not till i reach my brother's door. 
i had not gain the threshold, when, exhaust by the violence of my 
emotions, and by my speed, i sunk down in a fit. 

how long i remain in this situation i know not. when i recovered, i 
found myself stretch on a bed, surround by my sister and her 
female servants. i was astonish at the scene before me, but gradual 
recover the recollection of what had happened. i answer their 
importunate inquiry as well as i was able. my brother and pleyel, 
whom the storm of the preceding day chance to detain here, inform 
themselves of every particular, proceed with lights and weapon to my 
desert habitation. they enter my chamber and my closet, and found 
every thing in its proper place and customary order. the door of the 
closet was locked, and appear not to have been open in my absence. 
they went to judith's apartment. they found her asleep and in safety. 
pleyel's caution induce him to forbear alarm the girl; and finding 
her wholly ignorant of what had passed, they direct her to return to 
her chamber. they then fasten the doors, and returned. 

my friends were disposed to regard this transaction as a dream. that 
person should be actually immure in this closet, to which, in the 
circumstances of the time, access from without or within was apparently 
impossible, they could not seriously believe. that any human being 
had intended murder, unless it were to cover a scheme of pillage, was 
incredible; but that no such design had been formed, was evident 
from the security in which the furniture of the house and the closet 
remained. 

i revolve every incident and expression that had occurred. my 
senses assured me of the truth of them, and yet their abrupt and 
improbability made me, in my turn, somewhat incredulous. the adventure 
had made a deep impression on my fancy, and it was not till after a 
week's abode at my brother's, that i resolve to resume the possession 
of my own dwelling. there was another circumstance that enhance the 
mysterious of this event. after my recovery it was obvious to 
inquire by what means the attention of the family had been drawn to my 
situation. i had fallen before i had reach the threshold, or was able 
to give any signal. my brother related, that while this was transact 
in my chamber, he himself was awake, in consequence of some slight 
indisposition, and lay, according to his custom, muse on some favorite 
topic. sudden the silence, which was remarkably profound, was broken 
by a voice of most piercing shrillness, that seem to be utter by one 
in the hall below his chamber. "awake! arise!" it exclaimed: "hasten to 
succour one that is die at your door." 

this summons was effectual. there was no one in the house who was not 
rouse by it. pleyel was the first to obey, and my brother overtook him 
before he reach the hall. what was the general astonishment when your 
friend was discover stretch upon the grass before the door, pale, 
ghastly, and with every mark of death! 

this was the third instance of a voice, exert for the benefit of this 
little community. the agent was no less inscrutable in this, than in the 
former case. when i ruminate upon these events, my soul was suspend 
in wonder and awe. was i really deceive in imagine that i heard the 
closet conversation? i was no long at liberty to question the reality 
of those accent which had formerly recall my brother from the hill; 
which had impart tidings of the death of the germany lady to pleyel; 
and which had lately summon them to my assistance. 

but how was i to regard this midnight conversation? hoarse and manlike 
voice confer on the means of death, so near my bed, and at such 
an hour! how had my ancient security vanished! that dwelling, which had 
hitherto been an inviolate asylum, was now beset with danger to my 
life. that solitude, formerly so dear to me, could no long be endured. 
pleyel, who had consent to reside with us during the month of spring, 
lodge in the vacant chamber, in order to quiet my alarms. he treat 
my fear with ridicule, and in a short time very slight trace of them 
remained: but as it was wholly indifferent to him whether his nights 
were pass at my house or at my brother's, this arrangement gave 
general satisfaction. 



chapter vii 


i will not enumerate the various inquiry and conjecture which these 
incident occasioned. after all our efforts, we came no near to 
dispel the mist in which they were involved; and time, instead of 
facilitate a solution, only accumulate our doubts. in the midst of 
thought excited by these events, i was not unmindful of my interview 
with the stranger. i related the particulars, and shew the portrait to 
my friends. pleyel recollect to have met with a figure resemble 
my description in the city; but neither his face or garb made the same 
impression upon him that it made upon me. it was a hint to rally me upon 
my prepossessions, and to amuse us with a thousand ludicrous anecdote 
which he had collected in his travels. he made no scruple to charge me 
with being in love; and threaten to inform the swain, when he met him, 
of his good fortune. 

pleyel's temper made him susceptible of no durable impressions. his 
conversation was occasional visit by gleam of his ancient vivacity; 
but, though his impetuos was sometimes inconvenient, there was 
nothing to dread from his malice. i had no fear that my character or 
dignity would suffer in his hands, and was not heartily displease when 
he declared his intention of profit by his first meeting with the 
stranger to introduce him to our acquaintance. 

some week after this i had spent a toilsome day, and, as the sun 
declined, found myself disposed to seek relief in a walk. the river 
bank is, at this part of it, and for some considerable space upward, 
so rugged and steep as not to be easily descended. in a recess of this 
declivity, near the southern verge of my little demesne, was placed a 
slight building, with seat and lattices. from a crevice of the rock, 
to which this edifice was attached, there burst forth a stream of the 
purest water, which, leap from ledge to ledge, for the space of sixty 
feet, produce a fresh in the air, and a murmur, the most delicious 
and soothe imaginable. these, add to the odour of the cedar 
which embowered it, and of the honey-suckle which cluster among the 
lattices, render this my favorite retreat in summer. 

on this occasion i repair hither. my spirits droop through the 
fatigue of long attention, and i threw myself upon a bench, in a state, 
both mental and personally, of the utmost supineness. the lull 
sound of the waterfall, the fragrance and the dusk combine to becalm 
my spirits, and, in a short time, to sink me into sleep. either the 
uneasy of my posture, or some slight indisposition molest my 
repose with dream of no cheerful hue. after various incoherence 
had taken their turn to occupy my fancy, i at length imagine myself 
walking, in the evening twilight, to my brother's habitation. a pit, 
methought, had been dug in the path i had taken, of which i was not 
aware. as i careless pursue my walk, i thought i saw my brother, 
standing at some distance before me, beckon and calling me to make 
haste. he stood on the opposite edge of the gulph. i mend my pace, and 
one step more would have plunge me into this abyss, had not some 
one from behind caught sudden my arm, and exclaimed, in a voice of 
eager and terror, "hold! hold!" 

the sound broke my sleep, and i found myself, at the next moment, 
standing on my feet, and surround by the deepest darkness. image 
so terrific and forcible disabled me, for a time, from distinguish 
between sleep and wakefulness, and withheld from me the knowledge of my 
actual condition. my first panic were succeed by the perturbation of 
surprize, to find myself alone in the open air, and immerse in so deep 
a gloom. i slow recollect the incident of the afternoon, and how 
i came hither. i could not estimate the time, but saw the propriety of 
return with speed to the house. my faculty were still too confused, 
and the dark too intense, to allow me immediately to find my way up 
the steep. i sat down, therefore, to recover myself, and to reflect upon 
my situation. 

this was no soon done, than a low voice was heard from behind the 
lattice, on the side where i sat. between the rock and the lattice was a 
chasm not wide enough to admit a human body; yet, in this chasm he that 
spoke appear to be stationed. "attend! attend! but be not terrified." 

i start and exclaimed, "good heavens! what is that? who are you?" 

"a friend; one come, not to injure, but to save you; fear nothing." 

this voice was immediately recognize to be the same with one of 
those which i had heard in the closet; it was the voice of him who had 
propose to shoot, rather than to strangle, his victim. my terror made 
me, at once, mute and motionless. he continued, "i league to murder 
you. i repent. mark my bidding, and be safe. avoid this spot. the snare 
of death encompass it. elsewhere danger will be distant; but this spot, 
shun it as you value your life. mark me further; profit by this warning, 
but divulge it not. if a syllable of what has pass escape you, your 
doom is sealed. remember your father, and be faithful." 

here the accent ceased, and left me overwhelm with dismay. i was 
fraught with the persuasion, that during every moment i remain here, 
my life was endangered; but i could not take a step without hazard of 
fall to the bottom of the precipice. the path, leading to the summit, 
was short, but rugged and intricate. even star-light was exclude by the 
umbrage, and not the faintest gleam was afford to guide my steps. what 
should i do? to depart or remain was equally and eminently perilous. 

in this state of uncertainty, i perceive a ray flit across the gloom 
and disappear. another succeeded, which was stronger, and remain for 
a passing moment. it glitter on the shrub that were scattered at the 
entrance, and gleam continue to succeed gleam for a few seconds, till 
they, finally, gave place to unintermitt darkness. 

the first visiting of this light call up a train of horrors in my 
mind; destruction impende over this spot; the voice which i had lately 
heard had warn me to retire, and had menace me with the fate of my 
father if i refused. i was desirous, but unable, to obey; these gleam 
were such as prelude the stroke by which he fell; the hour, perhaps, 
was the same--i shudder as if i had beheld, suspend over me, the 
exterminate sword. 

presently a new and strong illumination burst through the lattice 
on the right hand, and a voice, from the edge of the precipice above, 
call out my name. it was pleyel. joyful did i recognize his accents; 
but such was the tumult of my thought that i had not power to answer 
him till he had frequent repeated his summons. i hurried, at length, 
from the fatal spot, and, direct by the lanthorn which he bore, 
ascend the hill. 

pale and breathless, it was with difficulty i could support myself. he 
anxious inquire into the cause of my affright, and the motive of my 
unusual absence. he had return from my brother's at a late hour, and 
was informed by judith, that i had walk out before sun-set, and had 
not yet returned. this intelligence was somewhat alarming. he wait 
some time; but, my absence continuing, he had set out in search of me. 
he had explore the neighbourhood with the utmost care, but, receiving 
no tidings of me, he was prepare to acquaint my brother with this 
circumstance, when he recollect the summer-house on the bank, and 
conceive it possible that some accident had detain me there. he again 
inquire into the cause of this detention, and of that confusion and 
dismay which my looks testified. 

i told him that i had stroll hither in the afternoon, that sleep had 
overtaken me as i sat, and that i had awaken a few minutes before 
his arrival. i could tell him no more. in the present impetuos of my 
thoughts, i was almost dubious, whether the pit, into which my brother 
had endeavour to entice me, and the voice that talk through the 
lattice, were not parts of the same dream. i remembered, likewise, the 
charge of secrecy, and the penalty denounced, if i should rash divulge 
what i had heard. for these reasons, i was silent on that subject, and 
shut myself in my chamber, deliver myself up to contemplation. 

what i have related will, no doubt, appear to you a fable. you will 
believe that calamity has subvert my reason, and that i am amuse 
you with the chimera of my brain, instead of fact that have really 
happened. i shall not be surprize or offended, if these be your 
suspicions. i know not, indeed, how you can deny them admission. for, if 
to me, the immediate witness, they were fertile of perplexity and doubt, 
how must they affect another to whom they are recommend only by 
my testimony? it was only by subsequent events, that i was fully and 
incontestib assured of the veracity of my senses. 

meanwhile what was i to think? i had been assured that a design had been 
form against my life. the ruffian had league to murder me. whom had 
i offended? who was there with whom i had ever maintain intercourse, 
who was capable of harbour such atrocious purposes? 

my temper was the reverse of cruel and imperious. my heart was touched 
with sympathy for the children of misfortune. but this sympathy was not 
a barren sentiment. my purse, scanty as it was, was ever open, and my 
hand ever active, to relieve distress. many were the wretch whom 
my personal exertion had extricate from want and disease, and who 
reward me with their gratitude. there was no face which lower at my 
approach, and no lip which utter imprecation in my hearing. on the 
contrary, there was none, over whose fate i had exert any influence, 
or to whom i was known by reputation, who did not greet me with smiles, 
and dismiss me with proof of veneration; yet did not my senses assure 
me that a plot was laid against my life? 

i am not destitute of courage. i have shewn myself deliberative and calm 
in the midst of peril. i have hazard my own life, for the preservation 
of another, but now was i confuse and panic struck. i have not live 
so as to fear death, yet to perish by an unseen and secret stroke, to be 
mangle by the knife of an assassin was a thought at which i shuddered; 
what had i done to deserve to be made the victim of malignant passions? 

but soft! was i not assured, that my life was safe in all place but 
one? and why was the treason limited to take effect in this spot? i 
was every where equally defenceless. my house and chamber were, at all 
times, accessible. danger still impende over me; the bloody purpose was 
still entertained, but the hand that was to execute it, was powerless in 
all place but one! 

here i had remain for the last four or five hours, without the means 
of resistance or defence, yet i had not been attacked. a human being was 
at hand, who was conscious of my presence, and warn me hereafter to 
avoid this retreat. his voice was not absolutely new, but had i never 
heard it but once before? but why did he prohibit me from relate 
this incident to others, and what species of death will be award if i 
disobey? 

he talk of my father. he intimated, that disclosure would pull upon my 
head, the same destruction. was then the death of my father, portentous 
and inexplicable as it was, the consequence of human machinations? it 
should seem, that this being is apprise of the true nature of this 
event, and is conscious of the means that led to it. whether it shall 
likewise fall upon me, depend upon the observance of silence. was it 
the infraction of a similar command, that brought so horrible a penalty 
upon my father? 

such were the reflection that haunt me during the night, and which 
effectually deprived me of sleep. next morning, at breakfast, pleyel 
related an event which my disappearance had hinder him from mention 
the night before. early the preceding morning, his occasion call him 
to the city; he had step into a coffee-house to while away an hour; 
here he had met a person whose appearance instantly bespoke him to be 
the same whose hasty visit i have mentioned, and whose extraordinary 
visage and tone had so powerful affected me. on an attentive survey, 
however, he proved, likewise, to be one with whom my friend had had some 
intercourse in europe. this authorise the liberty of accost him, and 
after some conversation, mindful, as pleyel said, of the footing which 
this stranger had gain in my heart, he had venture to invite him 
to mettingen. the invitation had been cheerful accepted, and a visit 
promise on the afternoon of the next day. 

this information excited no sober emotion in my breast. i was, of 
course, eager to be informed as to the circumstances of their ancient 
intercourse. when, and where had they met? what knew he of the life and 
character of this man? 

in answer to my inquiries, he informed me that, three years before, 
he was a traveller in spain. he had made an excursion from valencia to 
murviedro, with a view to inspect the remains of roman magnificence, 
scattered in the environs of that town. while traverse the scite 
of the theatre of old saguntum, he light upon this man, seat on a 
stone, and deep engaged in peruse the work of the deacon marti. a 
short conversation ensued, which prove the stranger to be english. they 
return to valencia together. 

his garb, aspect, and deportment, were wholly spanish. a residence of 
three years in the country, indefatigable attention to the language, 
and a studious conformity with the customs of the people, had made him 
indistinguishable from a native, when he chose to assume that character. 
pleyel found him to be connected, on the footing of friendship and 
respect, with many eminent merchant in that city. he had embrace the 
catholic religion, and adopt a spain name instead of his own, which 
was carwin, and devoted himself to the literature and religion of his 
new country. he pursue no profession, but subsist on remittance from 
england. 

while pleyel remain in valencia, carwin betray no aversion to 
intercourse, and the former found no small attraction in the society of 
this new acquaintance. on general topic he was highly intelligent and 
communicative. he had visit every corner of spain, and could furnish 
the most accurate detail respecting its ancient and present state. 
on topic of religion and of his own history, previous to his 
transformation into a spaniard, he was invariable silent. you could 
merely gather from his discourse that he was english, and that he was 
well acquaint with the neighbouring countries. 

his character excited considerable curiosity in this observer. it was 
not easy to reconcile his conversion to the romish faith, with those 
proof of knowledge and capacity that were exhibit by him on different 
occasions. a suspicion was, sometimes, admitted, that his belief was 
counterfeit for some politics purpose. the most careful observation, 
however, produce no discovery. his manners were, at all times, harmless 
and inartificial, and his habit those of a lover of contemplation and 
seclusion. he appear to have contract an affection for pleyel, who 
was not slow to return it. 

my friend, after a month's residence in this city, return into france, 
and, since that period, had heard nothing concerning carwin till his 
appearance at mettingen. 

on this occasion carwin had received pleyel's greeting with a certain 
distance and solemnity to which the latter had not been accustomed. he 
had wave notice the inquiry of pleyel respecting his desertion 
of spain, in which he had formerly declared that it was his purpose to 
spend his life. he had assiduous divert the attention of the latter 
to indifferent topics, but was still, on every theme, as eloquent and 
judicious as formerly. why he had assume the garb of a rustic, pleyel 
was unable to conjecture. perhaps it might be poverty, perhaps he was 
sway by motive which it was his interest to conceal, but which were 
connected with consequence of the utmost moment. 

such was the sum of my friend's information. i was not sorry to be left 
alone during the greater part of this day. every employment was irksome 
which did not leave me at liberty to meditate. i had now a new subject 
on which to exercise my thoughts. before evening i should be usher 
into his presence, and listen to those tone whose magical and thrill 
power i had already experienced. but with what new image would he then 
be accompanied? 

carwin was an adherent to the romish faith, yet was an englishman by 
birth, and, perhaps, a protestant by education. he had adopt spain for 
his country, and had intimate a design to spend his days there, yet now 
was an inhabitant of this district, and disguise by the habiliment of 
a clown! what could have obliterate the impression of his youth, and 
made him abjure his religion and his country? what subsequent event had 
introduce so total a change in his plans? in withdraw from spain, 
had he revert to the religion of his ancestors; or was it true, that 
his former conversion was deceitful, and that his conduct had been 
sway by motive which it was prudent to conceal? 

hour were consume in revolving these ideas. my meditation were 
intense; and, when the series was broken, i began to reflect with 
astonishment on my situation. from the death of my parents, till the 
commencement of this year, my life had been serene and blissful, beyond 
the ordinary portion of humanity; but, now, my bosom was corrode by 
anxiety. i was visit by dread of unknown dangers, and the future was 
a scene over which cloud rolled, and thunder muttered. i compare the 
cause with the effect, and they seem disproportion to each other. 
all unaware, and in a manner which i had no power to explain, i was 
pushed from my immoveable and lofty station, and cast upon a sea of 
troubles. 

i determined to be my brother's visitant on this evening, yet my 
resolve were not unattended with waver and reluctance. pleyel's 
insinuation that i was in love, affected, in no degree, my belief, yet 
the consciousness that this was the opinion of one who would, probably, 
be present at our introduction to each other, would excite all that 
confusion which the passion itself is apt to produce. this would confirm 
him in his error, and call forth new railleries. his mirth, when exert 
upon this topic, was the source of the bitterest vexation. had he been 
aware of its influence upon my happiness, his temper would not have 
allow him to persist; but this influence, it was my chief endeavour 
to conceal. that the belief of my have bestow my heart upon another, 
produce in my friend none but ludicrous sensations, was the true cause 
of my distress; but if this had been discover by him, my distress 
would have been unspeakable aggravated. 



chapter viii 


as soon as evening arrived, i perform my visit. carwin made one of the 
company, into which i was ushered. appearances were the same as when i 
before beheld him. his garb was equally negligent and rustic. i gaze 
upon his countenance with new curiosity. my situation was such as to 
enable me to bestow upon it a deliberate examination. view at more 
leisure, it lost none of its wonderful properties. i could not deny my 
homage to the intelligence express in it, but was wholly uncertain, 
whether he were an object to be dread or adored, and whether his 
powers had been exert to evil or to good. 

he was sparing in discourse; but whatever he said was pregnant with 
meaning, and utter with rectitude of articulation, and force of 
emphasis, of which i had entertain no conception previous to my 
knowledge of him. notwithstanding the uncouth of his garb, his 
manners were not unpolished. all topic were handle by him with skill, 
and without pedantry or affectation. he utter no sentiment calculate 
to produce a disadvantageous impression: on the contrary, his 
observations denote a mind alive to every generous and heroic feeling. 
they were introduce without parade, and accompany with that degree of 
earnest which indicate sincerity. 

he part from us not till late, refuse an invitation to spend the 
night here, but readily consent to repeat his visit. his visit 
were frequent repeated. each day introduce us to a more intimate 
acquaintance with his sentiments, but left us wholly in the dark, 
concerning that about which we were most inquisitive. he studious 
avoid all mention of his past or present situation. even the place of 
his abode in the city he conceal from us. 

our sphere, in this respect, being somewhat limited, and the 
intellectual endowment of this man being indisputable great, his 
deportment was more diligent marked, and copious comment on by 
us, than you, perhaps, will think the circumstances warranted. not a 
gesture, or glance, or accent, that was not, in our private assemblies, 
discussed, and inference deduce from it. it may well be thought that 
he model his behaviour by an uncommon standard, when, with all our 
opportunity and accuracy of observation, we were able, for a long 
time, to gather no satisfactory information. he afford us no ground on 
which to build even a plausible conjecture. 

there is a degree of familiarity which take place between constant 
associates, that justify the negligence of many rule of which, in 
an early period of their intercourse, polite require the exact 
observance. inquiry into our condition are allowable when they are 
prompt by a disinterested concern for our welfare; and this solicitude 
is not only pardonable, but may just be demand from those who chuse 
us for their companions. this state of things was more slow to arrive 
on this occasion than on most others, on account of the gravity and 
lofty of this man's behaviour. 

pleyel, however, began, at length, to employ regular means for this end. 
he occasional allude to the circumstances in which they had formerly 
met, and remark the incongruous between the religion and habit 
of a spaniard, with those of a native of britain. he express 
his astonishment at meeting our guest in this corner of the globe, 
especially as, when they part in spain, he was taught to believe that 
carwin should never leave that country. he insinuated, that a change 
so great must have been prompt by motive of a singular and momentous 
kind. 

no answer, or an answer wide of the purpose, was generally made to these 
insinuations. briton and spaniards, he said, are votary of the same 
deity, and square their faith by the same precepts; their idea are 
drawn from the same fountain of literature, and they speak dialect of 
the same tongue; their government and law have more resemblance than 
differences; they were formerly provinces of the same civil, and till 
lately, of the same religious, empire. 

as to the motive which induce men to change the place of their abode, 
these must unavoidable be fleeting and mutable. if not bound to one spot 
by conjugal or parental ties, or by the nature of that employment to 
which we are indebted for subsistence, the inducement to change are far 
more numerous and powerful, than opposite inducements. 

he spoke as if desirous of shew that he was not aware of the tendency 
of pleyel's remarks; yet, certain token were apparent, that prove him 
by no means wanting in penetration. these token were to be read in his 
countenance, and not in his words. when any thing was said, indicate 
curiosity in us, the gloom of his countenance was deepened, his eyes 
sunk to the ground, and his wonted air was not resume without visible 
struggle. hence, it was obvious to infer, that some incident of 
his life were reflect on by him with regret; and that, since these 
incident were careful concealed, and even that regret which flow 
from them laborious stifled, they had not been merely disastrous. the 
secrecy that was observe appear not design to provoke or baffle the 
inquisitive, but was prompt by the shame, or by the prudence of guilt. 

these ideas, which were adopt by pleyel and my brother, as well as 
myself, hinder us from employ more direct means for accomplish 
our wishes. question might have been put in such terms, that no room 
should be left for the pretence of misapprehension, and if modesty 
merely had been the obstacle, such question would not have been 
wanting; but we considered, that, if the disclosure were productive of 
pain or disgrace, it was inhuman to extort it. 

amidst the various topic that were discuss in his presence, allusion 
were, of course, made to the inexplicable event that had lately 
happened. at those times, the words and looks of this man were object 
of my particular attention. the subject was extraordinary; and any 
one whose experience or reflection could throw any light upon it, was 
entitle to my gratitude. as this man was enlightened by reading and 
travel, i listen with eager to the remark which he should make. 

at first, i entertain a kind of apprehension, that the tale would be 
heard by him with incredulity and secret ridicule. i had formerly heard 
story that resemble this in some of their mysterious circumstances, 
but they were, commonly, heard by me with contempt. i was doubtful, 
whether the same impression would not now be made on the mind of our 
guest; but i was mistaken in my fears. 

he heard them with seriousness, and without any mark either of 
surprize or incredulity. he pursued, with visible pleasure, that kind 
of disquisition which was naturally suggest by them. his fancy was 
eminently vigorous and prolific, and if he did not persuade us, that 
human being are, sometimes, admitted to a sensible intercourse with the 
author of nature, he, at least, won over our inclination to the cause. 
he merely deduced, from his own reasonings, that such intercourse 
was probable; but confessed that, though he was acquaint with many 
instance somewhat similar to those which had been related by us, none 
of them were perfectly exempt from the suspicion of human agency. 

on being request to relate these instances, he amuse us with many 
curious details. his narrative were construct with so much skill, 
and rehearse with so much energy, that all the effects of a dramatic 
exhibition were frequent produce by them. those that were most 
coherent and most minute, and, of consequence, least entitle to credit, 
were yet render probable by the exquisite art of this rhetorician. for 
every difficulty that was suggested, a ready and plausible solution 
was furnished. mysterious voice had always a share in produce 
the catastrophe, but they were always to be explain on some known 
principles, either as reflect into a focus, or communicate through 
a tube. i could not but remark that his narratives, however complex or 
marvellous, contained no instance sufficient parallel to those that 
had befallen ourselves, and in which the solution was applicable to our 
own case. 

my brother was a much more sanguine reason than our guest. even 
in some of the fact which were related by carwin, he maintain the 
probability of celestial interference, when the latter was disposed to 
deny it, and had found, as he imagined, footstep of an human agent. 
pleyel was by no means equally credulous. he scruple not to deny faith 
to any testimony but that of his senses, and allow the fact which had 
lately been support by this testimony, not to mould his belief, but 
merely to give birth to doubts. 

it was soon observe that carwin adopted, in some degree, a similar 
distinction. a tale of this kind, related by others, he would believe, 
provided it was explicable upon known principles; but that such notice 
were actually communicate by being of an higher order, he would 
believe only when his own ear were assail in a manner which could not 
be otherwise account for. civility forbad him to contradict my brother 
or myself, but his understanding refuse to acquiesce in our testimony. 
besides, he was disposed to question whether the voice heard in the 
temple, at the foot of the hill, and in my closet, were not really 
utter by human organs. on this supposition he was desire to explain 
how the effect was produced. 

he answered, that the power of mimickry was very common. catharine's 
voice might easily be imitate by one at the foot of the hill, who would 
find no difficulty in eluding, by flight, the search of wieland. the 
tidings of the death of the saxon lady were utter by one near at hand, 
who overheard the conversation, who conjecture her death, and whose 
conjecture happen to accord with the truth. that the voice appear to 
come from the ciele was to be considered as an illusion of the fancy. 
the cry for help, heard in the hall on the night of my adventure, was to 
be ascribe to an human creature, who actually stood in the hall when he 
utter it. it was of no moment, he said, that we could not explain by 
what motive he that made the signal was led hither. how imperfect 
acquaint were we with the condition and designs of the being that 
surround us? the city was near at hand, and thousand might there 
exist whose powers and purpose might easily explain whatever was 
mysterious in this transaction. as to the closet dialogue, he was 
oblige to adopt one of two suppositions, and affirm either that it was 
fashion in my own fancy, or that it actually took place between two 
person in the closet. 

such was carwin's mode of explain these appearances. it is such, 
perhaps, as would commend itself as most plausible to the most sagacious 
minds, but it was insufficient to impart conviction to us. as to the 
treason that was meditate against me, it was doubtless just to conclude 
that it was either real or imaginary; but that it was real was attested 
by the mysterious warning in the summer-house, the secret of which i had 
hitherto lock up in my own breast. 

a month pass away in this kind of intercourse. as to carwin, our 
ignorance was in no degree enlightened respecting his genuine character 
and views. appearances were uniform. no man possessed a large store of 
knowledge, or a greater degree of skill in the communication of it to 
others; hence he was regard as an inestimable addition to our society. 
considering the distance of my brother's house from the city, he was 
frequent prevail upon to pass the night where he spent the evening. 
two days seldom elapse without a visit from him; hence he was regard 
as a kind of inmate of the house. he enter and departed without 
ceremony. when he arrive he received an unaffected welcome, and when he 
chose to retire, no importune were use to induce him to remain. 

the temple was the principal scene of our social enjoyments; yet the 
felicity that we taste when assemble in this asylum, was but the 
gleam of a former sun-shine. carwin never part with his gravity. 
the inscrutable of his character, and the uncertainty whether his 
fellowship tend to good or to evil, were seldom absent from our minds. 
this circumstance powerful contribute to sadden us. 

my heart was the seat of grow disquietudes. this change in one who 
had formerly been characterize by all the exuberance of soul, could 
not fail to be remark by my friends. my brother was always a pattern 
of solemnity. my sister was clay, mould by the circumstances in which 
she happen to be placed. there was but one whose deportment remains 
to be describe as being of importance to our happiness. had pleyel 
likewise dismiss his vivacity? 

he was as whimsical and jestful as ever, but he was not happy. the 
truth, in this respect, was of too much importance to me not to make me 
a vigilant observer. his mirth was easily perceive to be the fruit 
of exertion. when his thought wander from the company, an air of 
dissatisfaction and impatience stole across his features. even the 
punctual and frequency of his visit were somewhat lessened. it may 
be supposed that my own uneasy was heighten by these tokens; but, 
strange as it may seem, i found, in the present state of my mind, no 
relief but in the persuasion that pleyel was unhappy. 

that unhappiness, indeed, depended, for its value in my eyes, on the 
cause that produce it. it did not arise from the death of the saxon 
lady: it was not a contagious emanate from the countenance of wieland 
or carwin. there was but one other source whence it could flow. a 
nameless ecstacy thrill through my frame when any new proof occur 
that the ambiguous of my behaviour was the cause. 



chapter ix 


my brother had received a new book from germany. it was a tragedy, and 
the first attempt of a saxon poet, of whom my brother had been taught to 
entertain the highest expectations. the exploit of zisca, the bohemian 
hero, were woven into a dramatic series and connection. according to 
germany custom, it was minute and diffuse, and dictate by an adventurous 
and lawless fancy. it was a chain of audacious acts, and unheard-of 
disasters. the moated fortress, and the thicket; the ambush and the 
battle; and the conflict of headlong passions, were pourtraye in 
wild numbers, and with terrific energy. an afternoon was set apart to 
rehearse this performance. the language was familiar to all of us but 
carwin, whose company, therefore, was tacit dispense with. 

the morning previous to this intended rehearsal, i spent at home. my 
mind was occupy with reflection relative to my own situation. the 
sentiment which live with chief energy in my heart, was connected 
with the image of pleyel. in the midst of my anguish, i had not been 
destitute of consolation. his late deportment had given spring to my 
hopes. was not the hour at hand, which should render me the happiest 
of human creatures? he suspect that i look with favorable eyes upon 
carwin. hence arose disquietudes, which he struggle in vain to conceal. 
he love me, but was hopeless that his love would be compensated. is it 
not time, said i, to rectify this error? but by what means is this to be 
effected? it can only be done by a change of deportment in me; but how 
must i demean myself for this purpose? 

i must not speak. neither eyes, nor lips, must impart the information. 
he must not be assured that my heart is his, previous to the tender of 
his own; but he must be convinced that it has not been given to another; 
he must be supply with space whereon to build a doubt as to the true 
state of my affections; he must be prompt to avow himself. the line 
of delicate propriety; how hard it is, not to fall short, and not to 
overleap it! 

this afternoon we shall meet at the temple. we shall not separate till 
late. it will be his province to accompany me home. the airy expanse is 
without a speck. this breeze is usually stedfast, and its promise of 
a bland and cloudless evening, may be trusted. the moon will rise at 
eleven, and at that hour, we shall wind along this bank. possibly that 
hour may decide my fate. if suitable encouragement be given, pleyel will 
reveal his soul to me; and i, ere i reach this threshold, will be made 
the happiest of beings. and is this good to be mine? add wings to thy 
speed, sweet evening; and thou, moon, i charge thee, shroud thy beam at 
the moment when my pleyel whisper love. i would not for the world, that 
the burning blushes, and the mount rapture of that moment, should be 
visible. 

but what encouragement is wanting? i must be regardful of insurmountable 
limits. yet when mind are imbue with a genuine sympathy, are not words 
and looks superfluous? are not motion and touch sufficient to impart 
feelings such as mine? has he not eye me at moments, when the pressure 
of his hand has thrown me into tumults, and was it possible that he 
mistook the impetuos of love, for the eloquence of indignation? 

but the hasten evening will decide. would it were come! and yet i 
shudder at its near approach. an interview that must thus terminate, is 
surely to be wish for by me; and yet it is not without its terrors. 
would to heaven it were come and gone! 

i feel no reluctance, my friends to be thus explicit. time was, when 
these emotion would be hidden with immeasurable solicitude, from every 
human eye. alas! these airy and fleeting impulse of shame are gone. my 
scruple were preposterous and criminal. they are bred in all hearts, by 
a perverse and vicious education, and they would still have maintain 
their place in my heart, had not my portion been set in misery. my 
error have taught me thus much wisdom; that those sentiment which we 
ought not to disclose, it is criminal to harbour. 

it was propose to begin the rehearsal at four o'clock; i count the 
minutes as they passed; their flight was at once too rapid and too slow; 
my sensation were of an excruciating kind; i could taste no food, nor 
apply to any task, nor enjoy a moment's repose: when the hour arrived, i 
hasten to my brother's. 

pleyel was not there. he had not yet come. on ordinary occasions, he was 
eminent for punctuality. he had testify great eager to share 
in the pleasure of this rehearsal. he was to divide the task with my 
brother, and, in task like these, he always engaged with peculiar 
zeal. his elocution was less sweet than sonorous; and, therefore, 
better adapt than the mellifluence of his friend, to the outrageous 
vehemence of this drama. 

what could detain him? perhaps he linger through forgetfulness. yet 
this was incredible. never had his memory been known to fail upon even 
more trivial occasions. not less impossible was it, that the scheme had 
lost its attractions, and that he staid, because his coming would afford 
him no gratification. but why should we expect him to adhere to the 
minute? 

an half hour elapsed, but pleyel was still at a distance. perhaps he had 
misunderstood the hour which had been proposed. perhaps he had conceive 
that to-morrow, and not to-day, had been select for this purpose: 
but no. a review of preceding circumstances demonstrate that such 
misapprehension was impossible; for he had himself propose this day, 
and this hour. this day, his attention would not otherwise be occupied; 
but to-morrow, an indispensible engagement was foreseen, by which all 
his time would be engrossed: his detention, therefore, must be owe 
to some unforeseen and extraordinary event. our conjecture were vague, 
tumultuous, and sometimes fearful. his sickness and his death might 
possibly have detain him. 

torture with suspense, we sat gaze at each other, and at the path 
which led from the road. every horseman that pass was, for a moment, 
imagine to be him. hour succeed hour, and the sun, gradual 
declining, at length, disappeared. every signal of his coming prove 
fallacious, and our hope were at length dismissed. his absence affected 
my friends in no insupportable degree. they should be obliged, they 
said, to defer this undertaking till the morrow; and, perhaps, their 
impatient curiosity would compel them to dispense entire with his 
presence. no doubt, some harmless occurrence had divert him from 
his purpose; and they trust that they should receive a satisfactory 
account of him in the morning. 

it may be supposed that this disappointment affected me in a very 
different manner. i turn aside my head to conceal my tears. i flee 
into solitude, to give vent to my reproaches, without interrupt

pleyel was not the only object of my keen but unjust upbraiding. deep

which i had reared! thus had my golden vision melt into air! 

how fondly did i dream that pleyel was a lover! if he were, would he

henceforth intrust my felicity to no one's keeping but my own. 

the first agony of this disappointment would not allow me to be 
reasonable or just. every ground on which i had built the persuasion

he came not because the sight of me, the spectacle of my cold or 
aversion, contribute to his despair. why should i prolong, by hypocrisy 
or silence, his misery as well as my own? why not deal with him 
explicitly, and assure him of the truth? 

you will hardly believe that, in obedience to this suggestion, i rose 
for the purpose of order a light, that i might instantly make this

into a momentary approbation of it. i saw with the utmost clear that 
a confession like that would be the most remediless and unpardonable

which controule me.

a scheme of pleasure, in which he and his sister were parties, had been, 
in like manner, frustrate by his absence; but his absence, in that 
instance, had been occasion by his fall from a boat into the river, 
in consequence of which he had run the most imminent hazard of being 
drowned. here was a second disappointment endure by the same persons, 
and produce by his failure. might it not originate in the same cause? 
had he not design to cross the river that morning to make some

family not be apprize of it. the first intelligence of his fate may

hence, upon the shore. 

thus was i distress by opposite conjectures: thus was i torment by 
phantom of my own creation. it was not always thus. i can ascertain the

rank me in the number of its eulogists; it was alone sufficient to the 
exterminate of my peace: it was itself a plenteous source of 
calamity, and need not the concurrence of other evil to take away the 
attraction of existence, and dig for me an untimely grave. 

the state of my mind naturally introduce a train of reflection upon 
the danger and care which inevitable beset an human being. by no

the memory of this man, and every relique connected with his fate was 
preserve with the most scrupulous care. among these was to be number 
a manuscript, contain memoirs of his own life. the narrative was by 
no means recommend by its eloquence; but neither did all its value 
flow from my relationship to the author. its stile had an unaffected and

the incidents, together with their intrinsic importance, as descriptive 
of human manners and passions, made it the most useful book in my 
collection. it was late; but being sensible of no inclination to sleep, 
i resolve to betake myself to the peruse of it. 

to do this it was requisite to procure a light. the girl had long since 
retired to her chamber: it was therefore proper to wait upon myself. 
a lamp, and the means of lighting it, were only to be found in the 
kitchen. thither i resolve forthwith to repair; but the light was of 
use merely to enable me to read the book. i knew the shelf and the spot 
where it stood. whether i took down the book, or prepared the lamp in 
the first place, appear to be a matter of no moment. the latter was 
preferred, and, leave my seat, i approach the closet in which, as i 
mention formerly, my books and papers were deposited. 

sudden the remembrance of what had lately pass in this closet 
occurred. whether midnight was approaching, or had passed, i knew not. i 
was, as then, alone, and defenceless. the wind was in that direction 
in which, aid by the deathlike repose of nature, it brought to me 
the murmur of the water-fall. this was mingle with that solemn and 
enchanting sound, which a breeze produce among the leaves of pines. the 
words of that mysterious dialogue, their fearful import, and the wild

i prevail on myself at length to move towards the closet. i touched 
the lock, but my finger were powerless; i was visit afresh by 
unconquerable apprehensions. a sort of belief dart into my mind, that 
some being was conceal within, whose purpose were evil. i began to 
contend with those fears, when it occur to me that i might, without 
impropriety, go for a lamp previous to opening the closet. i recede
