direction. motion seem to produce a mechanical influence upon me. i 
was ashamed of my weakness. besides, what aid could be afford me by a 
lamp? 

my fear had picture to themselves no precise object. it would be 
difficult to depict, in words, the ingredient and hue of that phantom 
which haunt me. an hand invisible and of preternatural strength, 
lift by human passions, and select my life for its aim, were parts 
of this terrific image. all place were alike accessible to this foe, or 
if his empire were restricted by local bounds, those bounds were utterly 
inscrutable by me. but had i not been told by some one in league with 
this enemy, that every place but the recess in the bank was exempt from 
danger? i return to the closet, and once more put my hand upon the 
lock. o! may my ear lose their sensibility, ere they be again assail 
by a shriek so terrible! not merely my understanding was subdued by the 
sound: it act on my nerves like an edge of steel. it appear to cut 
asunder the fibre of my brain, and rack every joint with agony. 

the cry, loud and piercing as it was, was nevertheless human. no 
articulation was ever more distinct. the breath which accompany it did 
not fan my hair, yet did every circumstance combine to persuade me that 
the lip which utter it touched my very shoulder. 

"hold! hold!" were the words of this tremendous prohibition, in whose 
tone the whole soul seem to be wrap up, and every energy convert 
into eager and terror. 

shuddering, i dashed myself against the wall, and by the same 
involuntary impulse, turn my face backward to examine the mysterious 
monitor. the moon-light stream into each window, and every corner of 
the room was conspicuous, and yet i beheld nothing! 

the interval was too brief to be artificial measured, between the 
utterance of these words, and my scrutiny direct to the quarter whence 
they came. yet if a human being had been there, could he fail to have 
been visible? which of my senses was the prey of a fatal illusion? the 
shock which the sound produce was still felt in every part of my frame. 
the sound, therefore, could not but be a genuine commotion. but that i 
had heard it, was not more true than that the being who utter it was 
station at my right ear; yet my attendant was invisible. 

i cannot describe the state of my thought at that moment. surprize 
had master my faculties. my frame shook, and the vital current was 
congealed. i was conscious only to the vehemence of my sensations. this 
condition could not be lasting. like a tide, which sudden mount to 
an overwhelming height, and then gradual subsides, my confusion slow 
gave place to order, and my tumult to a calm. i was able to deliberate 
and move. i resume my feet, and advanced into the midst of the room. 
upward, and behind, and on each side, i threw penetrating glances. i was 
not satisfy with one examination. he that hitherto refuse to be 
seen, might change his purpose, and on the next survey be clearly 
distinguishable. 

solitude impose least restraint upon the fancy. dark is less fertile 
of image than the feeble lustre of the moon. i was alone, and the wall 
were chequer by shadowy forms. as the moon pass behind a cloud and 
emerged, these shadow seem to be endow with life, and to move. the 
apartment was open to the breeze, and the curtain was occasional 
blown from its ordinary position. this motion was not unaccompanied with 
sound. i fail not to snatch a look, and to listen when this motion 
and this sound occurred. my belief that my monitor was post near, 
was strong, and instantly convert these appearances to token of his 
presence, and yet i could discern nothing. 

when my thought were at length permit to revert to the past, the 
first idea that occur was the resemblance between the words of the 
voice which i had just heard, and those which had terminate my dream in 
the summer-house. there are means by which we are able to distinguish a 
substance from a shadow, a reality from the phantom of a dream. the pit, 
my brother beckon me forward, the seizure of my arm, and the voice 
behind, were surely imaginary. that these incident were fashion in my 
sleep, is support by the same indubitable evidence that compel me to 
believe myself awake at present; yet the words and the voice were the 
same. then, by some inexplicable contrivance, i was aware of the danger, 
while my action and sensation were those of one wholly unacquainted 
with it. now, was it not equally true that my action and persuasion 
were at war? had not the belief, that evil lurk in the closet, gain 
admittance, and had not my action betoken an unwarrantable security? 
to obviate the effects of my infatuation, the same means had been used. 

in my dream, he that tempt me to my destruction, was my brother. death 
was ambush in my path. from what evil was i now rescued? what minister 
or implement of ill was shut up in this recess? who was it whose 
suffocate grasp i was to feel, should i dare to enter it? what 
monstrous conception is this? my brother! 

no; protection, and not injury is his province. strange and terrible 
chimera! yet it would not be sudden dismissed. it was surely no vulgar 
agency that gave this form to my fears. he to whom all parts of time are 
equally present, whom no contingency approaches, was the author of that 
spell which now seize upon me. life was dear to me. no consideration 
was present that enjoin me to relinquish it. sacred duty combine 
with every spontaneous sentiment to endear to me my being. should i not 
shudder when my being was endangered? but what emotion should possess me 
when the arm lift aginst me was wieland's? 

idea exist in our mind that can be account for by no establish 
laws. why did i dream that my brother was my foe? why but because an 
omen of my fate was ordain to be communicated? yet what salutary end

incident and those of my dream. surely it was phrenzy that dictate my

tendency of which was to urge me to flight. such had been the effect 
formerly produced. had my mind been simply occupy with this thought at 
present, no doubt, the same impulse would have been experienced; but 
now it was my brother whom i was irresistable persuade to regard as the 
contrive of that ill of which i had been forewarned. this persuasion 
did not extenuate my fear or my danger. why then did i again approach 
the closet and withdraw the bolt? my resolution was instantly conceived, 
and execute without faultering. 

the door was form of light materials. the lock, of simple structure, 
easily forewent its hold. it open into the room, and commonly move 
upon its hinges, after being unfastened, without any effort of mine. 
this effort, however, was bestow upon the present occasion. it was 
my purpose to open it with quickness, but the exertion which i made was 
ineffectual. it refuse to open. 

at another time, this circumstance would not have look with a face of 
mystery. i should have supposed some casual obstruction, and repeated my 
effort to surmount it. but now my mind was accessible to no conjecture 
but one. the door was hinder from opening by human force. surely, here 
was new cause for affright. this was confirmation proper to decide my 
conduct. now was all ground of hesitation taken away. what could be 
supposed but that i desert the chamber and the house? that i at least 
endeavour no long to withdraw the door? 

have i not said that my action were dictate by phrenzy? my reason had 
forborne, for a time, to suggest or to sway my resolves. i reiterate 
my endeavours. i exert all my force to overcome the obstacle, but in 
vain. the strength that was exert to keep it shut, was superior to 
mine. 

a casual observer might, perhaps, applaud the audacious of this 
conduct. whence, but from an habitual defiance of danger, could my 
perseverance arise? i have already assigned, as distinct as i am able, 
the cause of it. the frantic conception that my brother was within, that 
the resistance made to my design was exert by him, had rooted itself 
in my mind. you will comprehend the height of this infatuation, when 
i tell you, that, finding all my exertion vain, i betook myself to 
exclamations. surely i was utterly bereft of understanding. 

now had i arrive at the crisis of my fate. "o! hinder not the door to 
open," i exclaimed, in a tone that had less of fear than of grief in 
it. "i know you well. come forth, but harm me not. i beseech you come 
forth." 

i had taken my hand from the lock, and remove to a small distance from 
the door. i had scarcely utter these words, when the door swung upon 
its hinges, and display to my view the interior of the closet. whoever 
was within, was shroud in darkness. a few seconds pass without 
interrupt of the silence. i knew not what to expect or to fear. my 
eyes would not stray from the recess. presently, a deep sigh was heard. 
the quarter from which it came heighten the eager of my gaze. some 
one approach from the farther end. i quick perceive the outline 
of a human figure. its steps were irresolute and slow. i recoil as it 
advanced. 

by coming at length within the verge of the room, his form was clearly 
distinguishable. i had prefigure to myself a very different personage. 
the face that present itself was the last that i should desire to meet 
at an hour, and in a place like this. my wonder was stifle by my fears. 
assassin had lurk in this recess. some divine voice warn me of 
danger, that at this moment await me. i had spurn the intimation, 
and challenge my adversary. 

i recall the mysterious countenance and dubious character of carwin. 
what motive but atrocious one could guide his steps hither? i was 
alone. my habit suit the hour, and the place, and the warmth of the 
season. all succour was remote. he had placed himself between me and the 
door. my frame shook with the vehemence of my apprehensions. 

yet i was not wholly lost to myself: i vigilant marked his demeanour. 
his looks were grave, but not without perturbation. what species of 
inquietude it betrayed, the light was not strong enough to enable me 
to discover. he stood still; but his eyes wander from one object to 
another. when these powerful organ were fixed upon me, i shrunk into 
myself. at length, he broke silence. earnestness, and not embarrassment, 
was in his tone. he advanced close to me while he spoke. 

"what voice was that which lately address you?" 

he pause for an answer; but observing my trepidation, he resumed, with 
undiminished solemnity: "be not terrified. whoever he was, he hast done 
you an important service. i need not ask you if it were the voice of 
a companion. that sound was beyond the compass of human organs. the 
knowledge that enable him to tell you who was in the closet, was 
obtain by incomprehensible means. 

"you knew that carwin was there. were you not apprize of his intents? 
the same power could impart the one as well as the other. yet, knowing 
these, you persisted. audacious girl! but, perhaps, you confide in his 
guardianship. your confidence was just. with succour like this at hand 
you may safe defy me. 

"he is my eternal foe; the baffle of my best concerted schemes. twice 
have you been save by his accursed interposition. but for him i should 
long ere now have borne away the spoils of your honor." 

he look at me with greater stedfast than before. i became every 
moment more anxious for my safety. it was with difficulty i stammer 
out an entreaty that he would instantly depart, or suffer me to do so. 
he paid no regard to my request, but proceed in a more impassioned

"what is it you fear? have i not told you, you are safe? has not one 
in whom you more reasonably place trust assured you of it? even if i 
execute my purpose, what injury is done? your prejudice will call it 
by that name, but it merit it not. i was impel by a sentiment that 
do you honor; a sentiment, that would sanctify my deed; but, whatever 
it be, you are safe. be this chimera still worshipped; i will do nothing 
to pollute it." there he stopped. 

the accent and gesture of this man left me drain of all courage. 
surely, on no other occasion should i have been thus pusillanimous. my 
state i regard as a hopeless one. i was wholly at the mercy of this 
being. whichever way i turn my eyes, i saw no avenue by which i might 
escape. the resources of my personal strength, my ingenuity, and my 
eloquence, i estimate at nothing. the dignity of virtue, and the force 
of truth, i had been accustomed to celebrate; and had frequent vaunt 
of the conquest which i should make with their assistance. 

i use to suppose that certain evil could never befall a being in 
possession of a sound mind; that true virtue supplies us with energy 
which vice can never resist; that it was always in our power to 
obstruct, by his own death, the designs of an enemy who aim at less 
than our life. how was it that a sentiment like despair had now invade 
me, and that i trust to the protection of chance, or to the pity of my 
persecutor? 

his words impart some notion of the injury which he had meditated. he 
talk of obstacle that had risen in his way. he had relinquish his 
design. these source supply me with slender consolation. there was no

he was silent, museful, and inattentive to my situation, yet made no

confident that reason in this contest would be impotent. i must owe my 
safety to his own suggestions. whatever purpose brought him hither, he 
had change it. why then did he remain? his resolution might fluctuate, 
and the pause of a few minutes restore to him his first resolutions. 

yet was not this the man whom we had treat with unweary kindness? 
whose society was endear to us by his intellectual elevation and 
accomplishments? who had a thousand times expatiate on the usefulness 
and beauty of virtue? why should such a one be dreaded? if i could have 
forgotten the circumstances in which our interview had taken place, i 
might have treat his words as jests. presently, he resumed: 

"fear me not: the space that sever us is small, and all visible succour 
is distant. you believe yourself completely in my power; that you stand 
upon the brink of ruin. such are your groundless fears. i cannot lift 
a finger to hurt you. easy it would be to stop the moon in her course 
than to injure you. the power that protect you would crumble my sinews, 
and reduce me to a heap of ashes in a moment, if i were to harbour a 
thought hostile to your safety. thus are appearances at length solved. 
little did i expect that they originate hence. what a portion is 
assign to you? scan by the eyes of this intelligence, your path 
will be without pit to swallow, or snare to entangle you. environed by 
the arms of this protection, all artifice will be frustrated, and all 
malice repelled." 

here succeed a new pause. i was still observant of every gesture and 
look. the tranquil solemnity that had lately possessed his countenance 
gave way to a new expression. all now was trepidation and anxiety. 

"i must be gone," said he in a falter accent. "why do i linger here? 
i will not ask your forgiveness. i see that your terror are invincible. 
your pardon will be extort by fear, and not dictate by compassion. i 
must fly from you forever. he that could plot against your honor, must 
expect from you and your friends persecution and death. i must doom 
myself to endless exile." 

saying this, he hasty left the room. i listen while he descended the 
stairs, and, unbolt the outer door, went forth. i did not follow him 
with my eyes, as the moon-light would have enable me to do. relieved by 
his absence, and exhaust by the conflict of my fears, i threw myself 
on a chair, and resigned myself to those bewilder idea which 
incident like these could not fail to produce.

order could not readily be introduce into my thoughts. the voice still 
rung in my ears. every accent that was utter by carwin was fresh in my 
remembrance. his unwelcome approach, the recognition of his person, his 
hasty departure, produce a complex impression on my mind which no words 
can delineate. i strove to give a slow motion to my thoughts, and to 
regulate a confusion which became painful; but my effort were nugatory. 
i cover my eyes with my hand, and sat, i know not how long, without 
power to arrange or utter my conceptions. 

i had remain for hours, as i believed, in absolute solitude. no 
thought of personal danger had molest my tranquillity. i had made 
no preparation for defence. what was it that suggest the design of 
peruse my father's manuscript? if, instead of this, i had retired 
to bed, and to sleep, to what fate might i not have been reserved? the 
ruffian, who must almost have suppress his breathing to screen himself 
from discovery, would have notice this signal, and i should have 
awaken only to perish with affright, and to abhor myself. could i have 
remain unconscious of my danger? could i have tranquil slept in the 
midst of so deadly a snare? 

and who was he that threaten to destroy me? by what means could he 
hide himself in this closet? surely he is gifted with supernatural 
power. such is the enemy of whose attempt i was forewarned. daily i had 
seen him and converse with him. nothing could be discern through the 
impenetrable veil of his duplicity. when busy in conjectures, as to 
the author of the evil that was threatened, my mind did not light, for 
a moment, upon his image. yet has he not avowed himself my enemy? why 
should he be here if he had not meditate evil? 

he confess that this has been his second attempt. what was the scene 
of his former conspiracy? was it not he whose whisper betray him? am 
i deceived; or was there not a faint resemblance between the voice of 
this man and that which talk of grasping my throat, and extinguish 
my life in a moment? then he had a colleague in his crime; now he 
is alone. then death was the scope of his thoughts; now an injury 
unspeakable more dreadful. how thankful should i be to the power that 
has interpose to save me! 

that power is invisible. it is subject to the cognizance of one of my 
senses. what are the means that will inform me of what nature it is? 
he has set himself to counterwork the machination of this man, who had 
menace destruction to all that is dear to me, and whose cunning had 
surmount every human impediment. there was none to rescue me from 
his grasp. my rash even hasten the completion of his scheme, and 
preclude him from the benefit of deliberation. i had rob him of the 
power to repent and forbear. had i been apprize of the danger, i should 
have regard my conduct as the means of rendering my escape from it

protector. else why that startle intreaty to refrain from opening the 
closet? by what inexplicable infatuation was i compel to proceed? 

yet my conduct was wise. carwin, unable to comprehend my folly, ascribe 
my behaviour to my knowledge. he conceive himself previous detected, 
and such detection being possible to flow only from my heavenly friend, 
and his enemy, his fear acquire additional strength. 

he is apprize of the nature and intentions of this being. perhaps he 
is a human agent. yet, on that supposition his atchievement are

a mere mortal, should i not recognize some one, whom, benefit impart

benignity with this province? are human faculty adequate to 
receive strong proof of the existence of unfettered and beneficent 
intelligence than i have received? 

but who was this man's coadjutor? the voice that acknowledge an 
alliance in treachery with carwin warn me to avoid the summer-house. 
he assured me that there only my safety was endangered. his assurance, 
as it now appears, was fallacious. was there not deceit in his 
admonition? was his compact really annulled? some purpose was, perhaps, 
to be accomplished by prevent my future visit to that spot. why was 
i enjoin silence to others, on the subject of this admonition, unless 
it were for some unauthorized and guilty purpose? 

no one but myself was accustomed to visit it. backward, it was hidden 
from distant view by the rock, and in front, it was screen from all 
examination, by creep plants, and the branch of cedars. what 
recess could be more propitious to secrecy? the spirit which haunt it

now, perhaps, it is the scene of his meditations. purpose fraught with

are here engendered, and fostered, and rear to maturity. 

such were the idea that, during the night, were tumultuous revolve

but what measure were now to be taken? was the danger which threaten

means of defence. i could not calculate the motive and regulate the

re-assume his purposes, and swift return to the execution of them? 

this idea cover me once more with dismay. how deep did i regret the

as my thought began to flow with few impediments, the image of

again ran over the possible cause of his absence on the preceding day. 
my mind was attune to melancholy. i dwelt, with an obstinacy for which

struggle with the billows, and his last appearance. i imagine myself

which the tide had cast up. these dreary image affected me even to 
tears. i endeavour not to restrain them. they impart a relief which 
i had not anticipated. the more copious they flowed, the more did 
my general sensation appear to subside into calm, and a certain 
restless give way to repose. 

perhaps, relieved by this effusion, the slumber so much want might 
have stolen on my senses, had there been no new cause of alarm. 



chapter xi 


i was arouse from this stupor by sound that evidently arose in the

my way lay through the city. i had scarcely enter it when i was seize

his opinion was not destitute of evidence: yet what proof could

built. these suspicion are foster by you as certainties; the tenor

every sentiment that my tongue and my pen have uttered, bear testimony 
to the rectitude of my mind; but this testimony is rejected. i am 
condemn as brutal profligate: i am class with the stupid and 
sordid wicked. 

"and where are the proof that must justify so foul and so improbable 
an accusation? you have overheard a midnight conference. voice have 
salute your ear, in which you imagine yourself to have recognize 
mine, and that of a detect villain. the sentiment express were 
not allow to outweigh the casual or concerted resemblance of voice. 
sentiment the reverse of all those whose influence my former life had 
attested, denote a mind pollute by grovel vices, and enter 
into compact with that of a thief and a murderer. the nature of these 
sentiment did not enable you to detect the cheat, did not suggest to 
you the possibility that my voice had been counterfeit by another. 

"you were precipitate and prone to condemn. instead of rush on the 
impostors, and compare the evidence of sight with that of hearing, you 
stood aloof, or you fled. my innocence would not now have stood in 
need of vindication, if this conduct had been pursued. that you did not 
pursue it, your present thought incontestib prove. yet this conduct 
might surely have been expect from pleyel. that he would not hasty 
impute the blackest of crimes, that he would not couple my name with 
infamy, and cover me with ruin for inadequate or slight reasons, might 
reasonably have been expected." the sob which convulse my bosom would 
not suffer me to proceed. 

pleyel was for a moment affected. he look at me with some expression 
of doubt; but this quick gave place to a mournful solemnity. he fixed 
his eyes on the floor as in reverie, and spoke: 

"two hour hence i am gone. shall i carry away with me the sorrow that 
is now my guest? or shall that sorrow be accumulate tenfold? what is 
she that is now before me? shall every hour supply me with new proof of 
a wicked beyond example? already i deem her the most abandoned and 
detest of human creatures. her coming and her tear impart a gleam 
of hope, but that gleam has vanished." 

he now fixed his eyes upon me, and every muscle in his face trembled. 
his tone was hollow and terrible--"thou knowest that i was a witness of 
your interview, yet thou comest hither to upbraid me for injustice! thou 
canst look me in the face and say that i am deceived!--an inscrutable 
providence has fashion thee for some end. thou wilt live, no doubt, to 
fulfil the purpose of thy maker, if he repent not of his workmanship, 
and send not his vengeance to exterminate thee, ere the measure of thy 
days be full. surely nothing in the shape of man can vie with thee! 

"but i thought i had stifle this fury. i am not constitute thy judge. 
my office is to pity and amend, and not to punish and revile. i deem 
myself exempt from all tempestuous passions. i had almost persuade 
myself to weep over thy fall; but i am frail as dust, and mutable as 
water; i am calm, i am compassionate only in thy absence.--make this 
house, this room, thy abode as long as thou wilt, but forgive me if i 
prefer solitude for the short time during which i shall stay." saying 
this, he motion as if to leave the apartment. 

the stormy passion of this man affected me by sympathy. i cease to 
weep. i was motionless and speechless with agony. i sat with my hand 
clasped, mute gaze after him as he withdrew. i desire to detain 
him, but was unable to make any effort for that purpose, till he had 
pass out of the room. i then utter an involuntary and piercing 
cry--"pleyel! art thou gone? gone forever?" 

at this summons he hasty returned. he beheld me wild, pale, gasp 
for breath, and my head already sink on my bosom. a painful dizzy 
seize me, and i faint away. 

when i recovered, i found myself stretch on a bed in the outer 
apartment, and pleyel, with two female servant standing beside it. all 
the fury and scorn which the countenance of the former lately expressed, 
had now disappeared, and was succeed by the most tender anxiety. as 
soon as he perceive that my senses were return to me, he clasp his 
hands, and exclaimed, "god be thanked! you are once more alive. i had 
almost despair of your recovery. i fear i have been precipitate and 
unjust. my senses must have been the victim of some inexplicable and 
momentary phrenzy. forgive me, i beseech you, forgive my reproaches. i 
would purchase conviction of your purity, at the price of my existence 
here and hereafter." 

he once more, in a tone of the most fervent tenderness, besought me to 
be composed, and then left me to the care of the women. 



chapter xiii 


here was wrought a surprize change in my friend. what was it that 
had shaken conviction so firm? had any thing occur during my fit, 
adequate to produce so total an alteration? my attendant informed me 
that he had not left my apartment; that the unusual duration of my fit, 
and the failure, for a time, of all the means use for my recovery, had 
fill him with grief and dismay. did he regard the effect which his 
reproach had produce as a proof of my sincerity? 

in this state of mind, i little regard my languor of body. i rose 
and request an interview with him before my departure, on which i was 
resolved, notwithstanding his earnest solicit to spend the night 
at his house. he comply with my request. the tender which he had 
lately betrayed, had now disappeared, and he once more relapse into a 
chill solemnity. 

i told him that i was prepare to return to my brother's; that i had 
come hither to vindicate my innocence from the foul aspersion which he 
had cast upon it. my pride had not taken refuge in silence or distance. 
i had not rely upon time, or the suggestion of his cooler thoughts, to 
confute his charges. conscious as i was that i was perfectly guiltless, 
and entertaining some value for his good opinion, i could not prevail 
upon myself to believe that my effort to make my innocence manifest, 
would be fruitless. adverse appearances might be numerous and specious, 
but they were unquestionable false. i was willing to believe him 
sincere, that he made no charge which he himself did not believe; but 
these charge were destitute of truth. the grounds of his opinion were 
fallacious; and i desire an opportunity of detect their fallacy. 
i entreat him to be explicit, and to give me a detail of what he had 
heard, and what he had seen. 

at these words, my companion's countenance grew darker. he appear 
to be struggle with his rage. he open his lip to speak, but his 
accent die away ere they were formed. this conflict last for some 
minutes, but his fortitude was finally successful. he spoke as follows: 

"i would fain put an end to this hateful scene: what i shall say, will 
be breath idle and unprofitable consumed. the clearest narrative will 
add nothing to your present knowledge. you are acquaint with the 
grounds of my opinion, and yet you avow yourself innocent: why then 
should i rehearse these grounds? you are apprize of the character of 
carwin: why then should i enumerate the discovery which i have made 
respecting him? yet, since it is your request; since, considering the 
limited of human faculties, some error may possibly lurk in those 
appearances which i have witnessed, i will brief relate what i know. 

"need i dwell upon the impression which your conversation and 
deportment originally made upon me? we part in childhood; but our 
intercourse, by letter, was copious and uninterrupted. how fondly did i 
anticipate a meeting with one whom her letters had previous taught 
me to consider as the first of women, and how fully realize were the 
expectations that i had formed! 

"here, said i, is a being, after whom sage may model their transcendent 
intelligence, and painters, their ideal beauty. here is exemplified, 
that union between intellect and form, which has hitherto exist only 
in the conception of the poet. i have watch your eyes; my attention 
has hung upon your lips. i have question whether the enchantment of 
your voice were more conspicuous in the intricacy of melody, or the 
emphasis of rhetoric. i have marked the transition of your discourse, 
the felicity of your expression, your refined argumentation, and 
glowing imagery; and been forced to acknowledge, that all delight were 
meagre and contemptible, compare with those connected with the 
audience and sight of you. i have contemplate your principles, and been 
astonish at the solidity of their foundation, and the perfection of 
their structure. i have trace you to your home. i have view you in 
relation to your servants, to your family, to your neighbours, and to 
the world. i have seen by what skilful arrangement you facilitate 
the performance of the most arduous and complicated duties; what daily 
accession of strength your judicious discipline bestow upon 
your memory; what correct and abundance of knowledge was daily 
experienced by your unweary application to books, and to writing. 
if she that possess so much in the bloom of youth, will go on 
accumulate her stores, what, said i, is the picture she will display 
at a mature age? 

"you know not the accuracy of my observation. i was desirous that other 
should profit by an example so rare. i therefore noted down, in writing, 
every particular of your conduct. i was anxious to benefit by an 
opportunity so seldom afford us. i labour not to omit the slightest 
shade, or the most petty line in your portrait. here there was no other 
task incumbent on me but to copy; there was no need to exaggerate or 
overlook, in order to produce a more unexceptionable pattern. here was 
a combination of harmony and graces, incapable of diminution or 
accession without injury to its completeness. 

"i found no end and no bounds to my task. no display of a scene like 
this could be chargeable with redundancy or superfluity. even the colour 
of a shoe, the knot of a ribband, or your attitude in pluck a 
rose, were of moment to be recorded. even the arrangement of your 
breakfast-table and your toilet have been ample displayed. 

"i know that mankind are more easily entice to virtue by example than 
by precept. i know that the absolute of a model, when supply by 
invention, diminish its salutary influence, since it is useless, we 
think, to strive after that which we know to be beyond our reach. but 
the picture which i drew was not a phantom; as a model, it was devoid 
of imperfection; and to aspire to that height which had been really 
attained, was by no means unreasonable. i had another and more 
interesting object in view. one exist who claim all my tenderness. 
here, in all its parts, was a model worthy of assiduous study, and 
indefatigable imitation. i call upon her, as she wish to secure and 
enhance my esteem, to mould her thoughts, her words, her countenance, 
her actions, by this pattern. 

"the task was exuberant of pleasure, and i was deep engaged in it, 
when an imp of mischief was let loose in the form of carwin. i admire 
his powers and accomplishments. i did not wonder that they were admire 
by you. on the rectitude of your judgement, however, i rely to keep 
this admiration within discreet and scrupulous bounds. i assured myself, 
that the strange of his deportment, and the obscurity of his life, 
would teach you caution. of all errors, my knowledge of your character 
informed me that this was least likely to befall you. 

"you were powerful affected by his first appearance; you were 
bewitch by his countenance and his tones; your description was ardent 
and pathetic: i listen to you with some emotion of surprize. the 
portrait you drew in his absence, and the intensity with which you muse 
upon it, were new and unexpected incidents. they bespoke a sensibility 
somewhat too vivid; but from which, while subject to the guidance of 
an understanding like yours, there was nothing to dread. 

"a more direct intercourse took place between you. i need not apologize 
for the solicitude which i entertain for your safety. he that gifted 
me with perception of excellence, compel me to love it. in the midst

no price was too great by which your safety could be purchased. for 
that end, the sacrifice of ease, of health, and even of life, would 
cheerful have been made by me. what wonder then, that i scrutinize

that i watch your words and your looks when he was present; and that i 
extract cause for the deepest inquietudes, from every token which you 
gave of have put your happiness into this man's keeping? 

"i was cautious in deciding. i recall the various conversation in

refer your conduct to some principle which had not been hitherto 
disclosed, but which was reconcile with those already known. 

"i was not suffer to remain long in this suspence. one evening, you 
may recollect, i came to your house, where it was my purpose, as usual,

"i had gone thus far in error, and had no power to recede. how 
cautious should we guard against the first inroad of temptation! i

no sentiment of yours was of a nature which made it your interest to

all this pass in less than a moment. i then checked myself, and made

trepidation and blush were excessive. you hurried the paper out of 
sight, and seem too anxious to discover whether i knew the contents to 
allow yourself to make any inquiries. i wonder at these appearances

alone, these incident suggest themselves to my reflection anew.

disappearance on a former evening, my tracing you to the recess in the

this incident and this recess had been the subject of conversation.

happen in the presence of carwin, the countenance of the latter 
betray some emotion. could the interview have been with him? 

"this was an idea calculate to rouse every faculty to contemplation. 
an interview at that hour, in this darksome retreat, with a man of this 
mysterious but formidable character; a clandestine interview, and one 
which you afterwards endeavour with so much solicitude to conceal! it 
was a fearful and portentous occurrence. i could not measure his power, 
or fathom his designs. had he rifle from you the secret of your love, 
and reconcile you to concealment and noctural meetings? i scarcely ever 
spent a night of more inquietude. 

"i knew not how to act. the ascertain of this man's character 
and view seem to be, in the first place, necessary. had he openly 
prefer his suit to you, we should have been impowere to make 
direct inquiries; but since he had chosen this obscure path, it seem 
reasonable to infer that his character was exceptionable. it, at 
least, subject us to the necessity of resort to other means of 
information. yet the improbability that you should commit a deed of such

on which my suspicion had been built, and almost to condemn myself for

"though it was mere conjecture that the interview spoken of had taken 
place with carwin, yet two idea occur to involve me in the most 
painful doubts. this man's reasoning might be so specious, and 
his artifice so profound, that, aid by the passion which you had 
conceive for him, he had finally succeeded; or his situation might be 
such as to justify the secrecy which you maintained. in neither case did 
my wildest reverie suggest to me, that your honor had been forfeited. 

"i could not talk with you on this subject. if the imputation was false, 
its atrocious would have just drawn upon me your resentment, and 
i must have explain by what fact it had been suggested. if it were 
true, no benefit would follow from the mention of it. you had chosen 
to conceal it for some reasons, and whether these reason were true or 
false, it was proper to discover and remove them in the first place. 
finally, i acquiesce in the least painful supposition, trammel as it 
was with perplexities, that carwin was upright, and that, if the reason 
of your silence were known, they would be found to be just." 



chapter xiv 


"three days have elapse since this occurrence. i have been haunt by 
perpetual inquietude. to bring myself to regard carwin without terror, 
and to acquiesce in the belief of your safety, was impossible. yet to 
put an end to my doubts, seem to be impracticable. if some light could 
be reflect on the actual situation of this man, a direct path would 
present itself. if he were, contrary to the tenor of his conversation, 
cunning and malignant, to apprize you of this, would be to place you in 
security. if he were merely unfortunate and innocent, most readily would 
i espouse his cause; and if his intentions were upright with regard to 
you, most eager would i sanctify your choice by my approbation. 

"it would be vain to call upon carwin for an avowal of his deeds. it was 
better to know nothing, than to be deceive by an artful tale. what he 
was unwill to communicate, and this unwilling had been repeatedly 
manifested, could never be extort from him. importune might be 
appeased, or imposture effect by fallacious representations. to the 
rest of the world he was unknown. i had often made him the subject of 
discourse; but a glimpse of his figure in the street was the sum of 
their knowledge who knew most. none had ever seen him before, and 
received as new, the information which my intercourse with him in 
valencia, and my present intercourse, enable me to give. 

"wieland was your brother. if he had really made you the object of his 
courtship, was not a brother authorize to interfere and demand from him 
the confession of his views? yet what were the grounds on which i had 
rear this supposition? would they justify a measure like this? surely 
not. 

"in the course of my restless meditations, it occur to me, at length, 
that my duty require me to speak to you, to confess the indecorum of 
which i had been guilty, and to state the reflection to which it had 
led me. i was prompt by no mean or selfish views. the heart within my 
breast was not more precious than your safety: most cheerful would 
i have interpose my life between you and danger. would you cherish 
resentment at my conduct? when acquaint with the motive which 
produce it, it would not only exempt me from censure, but entitle me to 
gratitude. 

"yesterday had been select for the rehearsal of the newly-imported 
tragedy. i promise to be present. the state of my thought but little 
qualified me for a performer or auditor in such a scene; but i reflect

my resolution was not form without a remnant of doubt, as to its

the event of our conversation, fear that my interference was too late to

i had not err in believe you devoted to this man, or, at least, in 
imagine that he had obtain your consent to midnight conferences,

"i can assign no reason for calling at mrs. baynton's. i had seen her 
in the morning, and knew her to be well. the concerted hour had nearly

"i threw a languid glance at the first column that present itself. 
the first words which i read, began with the offer of a reward of three

"the description of his person and address were minute. his stature, 
hair, complexion, the extraordinary position and arrangement of his

been found guilty in two indictments. one for the murder of the lady 
jane conway, and the other for a robbery committed on the person of the

"i repeatedly peruse this passage. the idea which flow in upon my 
mind, affected me like an instant transition from death to life. the

was, in one sense, sufficient, yet if more could be obtained, more was 
desirable. this passage was copy from a britain paper; part of it 
only, perhaps, was transcribed. the printer was in possession of the 
original. 

"towards his house i immediately turn my horse's head. he produce the 
paper, but i found nothing more than had already been seen. while busy 
in peruse it, the printer stood by my side. he notice the object 
of which i was in search. "aye," said he, "that is a strange affair. i 
should never have met with it, had not mr. hallet sent to me the paper,

"mr. hallet! what reason could he have for making this request? had

"ludloe confirm the fact of his conviction and escape; and adds, that

"as i pass swift along, i review all the incident accompany

the process by which the sympathies of nature are extinguish in 
our hearts, by which evil is made our good, and by which we are made 
susceptible of no activity but in the infliction, and no joy but in the 
spectacle of woes, is an obvious process. as to an alliance with evil

"now, indeed, the scene was changed. it was not his secret poniard 
that i dreaded. it was only the success of his effort to make you a

"i took, as usual, the path through your brother's ground. i range

was prepared for defence; but now that danger was imminent, my means 
of defence, and my power to use them were gone. i was not qualified, by 
education and experience, to encounter peril like these: or, perhaps, 
i was powerless because i was again assault by surprize, and had not

chapter xvii 


i had no inclination nor power to move from this spot. for more than an

chapter xviii 


i had imperfect recover my strength, when i was informed of the

went to europe, and was a surgeon in the britain forces in germany,

intercourse had been punctual maintain by letters with his sister's

"theodore wieland, the prisoner at the bar, was now call upon for his 
defence. he look around him for some time in silence, and with a mild 
countenance. at length he spoke: 

"it is strange; i am known to my judge and my auditors. who is there 
present a stranger to the character of wieland? who know him not as an 
husband--as a father--as a friend? yet here am i arraign as criminal. 
i am charged with diabolical malice; i am accused of the murder of my 
wife and my children! 

"it is true, they were slain by me; they all perish by my hand. 
the task of vindication is ignoble. what is it that i am call to 
vindicate? and before whom? 

"you know that they are dead, and that they were kill by me. what more 
would you have? would you extort from me a statement of my motives? have 
you fail to discover them already? you charge me with malice; but your 
eyes are not shut; your reason is still vigorous; your memory has not 
forsaken you. you know whom it is that you thus charge. the habit of 
his life are known to you; his treatment of his wife and his 
offspring is known to you; the sound of his integrity, and the 
unchangeable of his principles, are familiar to your apprehension; 
yet you persist in this charge! you lead me hither manacle as a felon; 
you deem me worthy of a vile and torment death! 

"who are they whom i have devoted to death? my wife--the little ones, 
that drew their being from me--that creature who, as she surpass 
them in excellence, claim a large affection than those whom natural 
affinity bound to my heart. think ye that malice could have urge me 
to this deed? hide your audacious front from the scrutiny of heaven. 
take refuge in some cavern unvisited by human eyes. ye may deplore your 
wicked or folly, but ye cannot expiate it.

detest infatuation. deem me still a murderer, and drag me to 
untimely death. i make not an effort to dispel your illusion: i utter 
not a word to cure you of your sanguinary folly: but there are probably 
some in this assembly who have come from far: for their sakes, whose

done, and why.

i have cherished, in his presence, a single and upright heart. i have

"i turned: it is forbidden to describe what i saw: words, indeed, would 
be wanting to the task. the lineament of that being, whose veil was now 
lifted, and whose visage beam upon my sight, no hue of pencil or of 
language can pourtray. 

"as it spoke, the accent thrill to my heart. "thy prayer are heard. 
in proof of thy faith, render me thy wife. this is the victim i chuse. 
call her hither, and here let her fall."--the sound, and visage, and 
light vanish at once. 

"what demand was this? the blood of catharine was to be shed! my wife 
was to perish by my hand! i sought opportunity to attest my virtue. 
little did i expect that a proof like this would have been demanded. 

"my wife! i exclaimed: o god! substitute some other victim. make me 
not the butcher of my wife. my own blood is cheap. this will i pour 
out before thee with a willing heart; but spare, i beseech thee, this 
precious life, or commission some other than her husband to perform the 
bloody deed. 

"in vain. the conditions were prescribed; the decree had gone forth, and 
nothing remain but to execute it. i rush out of the house and across 
the intermediate fields, and stop not till i enter my own parlour. 
"my wife had remain here during my absence, in anxious expectation of 
my return with some tidings of her sister. i had none to communicate. 
for a time, i was breathless with my speed: this, and the tremor 
that shook my frame, and the wild of my looks, alarm her. she 
immediately suspect some disaster to have happen to her friend, and 
her own speech was as much overpower by emotion as mine. 

"she was silent, but her looks manifest her impatience to hear what i 
had to communicate. i spoke, but with so much precipitation as scarcely 
to be understood; catching her, at the same time, by the arm, and 
forcibly pull her from her seat. 

"come along with me: fly: waste not a moment: time will be lost, and the 
deed will be omitted. tarry not; question not; but fly with me! 

"this deportment add afresh to her alarms. her eyes pursue mine, and 
she said, "what is the matter? for god's sake what is the matter? where 
would you have me go?" 

"my eyes were fixed upon her countenance while she spoke. i thought 
upon her virtues; i view her as the mother of my babes: as my wife: 
i recall the purpose for which i thus urge her attendance. my heart 
faltered, and i saw that i must rouse to this work all my faculties. the 
danger of the least delay was imminent. 

"i look away from her, and again exert my force, drew her towards 
the door--'you must go with me--indeed you must.' 

"in her fright she half-resisted my efforts, and again exclaimed, 'good 
heaven! what is it you mean? where go? what has happened? have you found 
clara?" 

"follow me, and you will see," i answered, still urge her reluctant 
steps forward. 

"what phrenzy has seize you? something must needs have happened. is she 
sick? have you found her?" 

"come and see. follow me, and know for yourself." 

"still she expostulate and besought me to explain this mysterious 
behaviour. i could not trust myself to answer her; to look at her; but 
grasping her arm, i drew her after me. she hesitated, rather through 
confusion of mind than from unwilling to accompany me. this 
confusion gradual abated, and she move forward, but with irresolute 
footsteps, and continual exclamation of wonder and terror. her 
interrogate of "what was the matter?" and "whither was i going?" were 
ceaseless and vehement. 

"it was the scope of my effort not to think; to keep up a conflict and 
uproar in my mind in which all order and distinct should be lost; 
to escape from the sensation produce by her voice. i was, therefore, 
silent. i strove to abridge this interval by my haste, and to waste all 
my attention in furious gesticulations. 

"in this state of mind we reach my sister's door. she look at the 
window and saw that all was desolate--"why come we here? there is no 
body here. i will not go in." 

"still i was dumb; but opening the door, i drew her into the entry. this 
was the allot scene: here she was to fall. i let go her hand, and 
pressing my palm against my forehead, made one mighty effort to work up 
my soul to the deed. 

"in vain; it would not be; my courage was appalled; my arms nerveless: 
i mutter prayer that my strength might be aid from above. they 
avail nothing. 

"horror diffuse itself over me. this conviction of my cowardice, my 
rebellion, fasten upon me, and i stood rigid and cold as marble. from 
this state i was somewhat relieved by my wife's voice, who renew her 
supplicate to be told why we came hither, and what was the fate of my 
sister. 

"what could i answer? my words were broken and inarticulate. her fear 
naturally acquire force from the observation of these symptoms; but 
these fear were misplaced. the only inference she deduce from my 
conduct was, that some terrible mishap had befallen clara. 

"she wrung her hands, and exclaim in an agony, "o tell me, where is 
she? what has become of her? is she sick? dead? is she in her chamber? o 
let me go thither and know the worst!" 

"this proposal set my thought once more in motion. perhaps what my 
rebellious heart refuse to perform here, i might obtain strength enough 
to execute elsewhere. 

"come then," said i, "let us go." 

"i will, but not in the dark. we must first procure a light." 

"fly then and procure it; but i charge you, linger not. i will await for 
your return. 

"while she was gone, i strode along the entry. the fell of a gloomy 
hurricane but faint resemble the discord that reign in my mind. to 
omit this sacrifice must not be; yet my sinew had refuse to perform 
it. no alternative was offered. to rebel against the mandate was 
impossible; but obedience would render me the executioner of my wife. my 
will was strong, but my limb refuse their office. 

"she return with a light; i led the way to the chamber; she look 
round her; she lift the curtain of the bed; she saw nothing. 

"at length, she fixed inquiring eyes upon me. the light now enable her 
to discover in my visage what dark had hitherto concealed. her 
care were now transfer from my sister to myself, and she said in 
a tremulous voice, "wieland! you are not well: what ail you? can i do 
nothing for you?" 

"that accent and looks so winning should disarm me of my resolution, 
was to be expected. my thought were thrown anew into anarchy. i spread 
my hand before my eyes that i might not see her, and answer only by 
groans. she took my other hand between her's, and pressing it to her 
heart, spoke with that voice which had ever sway my will, and waft 
away sorrow. 

"my friend! my soul's friend! tell me thy cause of grief. do i not merit 
to partake with thee in thy cares? am i not thy wife?" 

"this was too much. i broke from her embrace, and retired to a corner 
of the room. in this pause, courage was once more infuse into me. i 
resolve to execute my duty. she follow me, and renew her passionate 
entreaty to know the cause of my distress. 

"i raise my head and regard her with stedfast looks. i mutter 
something about death, and the injunction of my duty. at these words 
she shrunk back, and look at me with a new expression of anguish. 
after a pause, she clasp her hands, and exclaimed-- 

"o wieland! wieland! god grant that i am mistaken; but surely something 
is wrong. i see it: it is too plain: thou art undone--lost to me and 
to thyself." at the same time she gaze on my features with intensest 
anxiety, in hope that different symptom would take place. i reply to 
her with vehemence-- 

"undone! no; my duty is known, and i thank my god that my cowardice is 
now vanquished, and i have power to fulfil it. catharine! i pity the 
weakness of thy nature: i pity thee, but must not spare. thy life is 
claim from my hands: thou must die!" 

"fear was now add to her grief. 'what mean you? why talk you of death? 
bethink yourself, wieland: bethink yourself, and this fit will pass. o 
why came i hither! why did you drag me hither?' 

"i brought thee hither to fulfil a divine command. i am appoint thy 
destroyer, and destroy thee i must." saying this i seize her wrists. 
she shriek aloud, and endeavour to free herself from my grasp; but 
her effort were vain. 

"surely, surely wieland, thou dost not mean it. am i not thy wife? and 
wouldst thou kill me? thou wilt not; and yet--i see--thou art wieland 
no longer! a fury resistless and horrible possess thee--spare 
me--spare--help--help--" 

"till her breath was stop she shriek for help--for mercy. when 
she could speak no longer, her gestures, her looks appeal to my 
compassion. my accursed hand was irresolute and tremulous. i meant 
thy death to be sudden, thy struggle to be brief. alas! my heart was 
infirm; my resolve mutable. thrice i slacken my grasp, and life kept 
its hold, though in the midst of pangs. her eye-balls start from their 
sockets. grim and distortion took place of all that use to bewitch 
me into transport, and subdue me into reverence. 

"i was commission to kill thee, but not to torment thee with the 
foresight of thy death; not to multiply thy fears, and prolong thy 
agonies. haggard, and pale, and lifeless, at length thou ceasedst to 
contend with thy destiny. 

"this was a moment of triumph. thus had i successful subdued the 
stubborn of human passions: the victim which had been demand was 
given: the deed was done past recal. 

"i lift the corpse in my arms and laid it on the bed. i gaze upon 
it with delight. such was the elation of my thoughts, that i even broke 
into laughter. i clap my hand and exclaimed, 'it is done! my sacred 
duty is fulfilled! to that i have sacrificed, o my god! thy last and 
best gift, my wife!' 

"for a while i thus soar above frailty. i imagine i had set myself 
forever beyond the reach of selfishness; but my imagination were false. 
this rapture quick subsided. i look again at my wife. my joyous 
ebullition vanished, and i ask myself who it was whom i saw? 
methought it could not be catharine. it could not be the woman who had 
lodge for years in my heart; who had slept, nightly, in my bosom; who 
had borne in her womb, who had foster at her breast, the being who 
call me father; whom i had watch with delight, and cherish with a 
fond ever new and perpetual growing: it could not be the same. 

"where was her bloom! these deadly and blood-suffused orb but ill 
resemble the azure and exstatic tender of her eyes. the lucid stream 
that meander over that bosom, the glow of love that was wont to sit 
upon that cheek, are much unlike these livid stain and this hideous 
deformity. alas! these were the trace of agony; the gripe of the 
assassin had been here! 

"i will not dwell upon my lapse into desperate and outrageous sorrow. 
the breath of heaven that sustain me was withdrawn and i sunk into 
mere man. i leap from the floor: i dashed my head against the wall: 
i utter scream of horror: i pant after torment and pain. eternal 
fire, and the bicker of hell, compare with what i felt, were music 
and a bed of roses. 

"i thank my god that this degeneracy was transient, that he deign once 
more to raise me aloft. i thought upon what i had done as a sacrifice to 
duty, and was calm. my wife was dead; but i reflected, that though this 
source of human consolation was closed, yet other were still open. if 
the transport of an husband were no more, the feelings of a father had 
still scope for exercise. when remembrance of their mother should excite 
too keen a pang, i would look upon them, and be comforted. 

"while i revolve these ideas, new warmth flow in upon my heart--i was 
wrong. these feelings were the growth of selfishness. of this i was 
not aware, and to dispel the mist that obscure my perceptions, a new 
effulgence and a new mandate were necessary. 

"from these thought i was recall by a ray that was shot into the 
room. a voice spake like that which i had before heard--'thou hast done 
well; but all is not done--the sacrifice is incomplete--thy children 
must be offered--they must perish with their mother!--'" 



chapter xx 


will you wonder that i read no farther? will you not rather be 
astonish that i read thus far? what power support me through such a 
task i know not. perhaps the doubt from which i could not disengage 
my mind, that the scene here depict was a dream, contribute to my 
perseverance. in vain the solemn introduction of my uncle, his appeal 
to my fortitude, and allusion to something monstrous in the event 
he was about to disclose; in vain the distressful perplexity, the 
mysterious silence and ambiguous answer of my attendants, especially 
when the condition of my brother was the theme of my inquiries, were 
remembered. i recall the interview with wieland in my chamber, his 
preternatural tranquill succeed by burst of passion and menace 
actions. all these coincide with the tenor of this paper. 

catharine and her children, and louisa were dead. the act that destroy 
them was, in the highest degree, inhuman. it was worthy of savage 
train to murder, and exult in agonies. 

who was the performer of the deed? wieland! my brother! the husband 
and the father! that man of gentle virtue and invincible benignity! 
place and mild--an idolator of peace! surely, said i, it is a dream. 
for many days have i been vex with frenzy. its dominion is still felt; 
but new form are call up to diversify and augment my torments. 

the paper drop from my hand, and my eyes follow it. i shrunk back, 
as if to avoid some petrify influence that approach me. my tongue 
was mute; all the function of nature were at a stand, and i sunk upon 
the floor lifeless. the noise of my fall, as i afterwards heard, alarm 
my uncle, who was in a lower apartment, and whose apprehension had 
detain him. he hasten to my chamber, and administer the assistance 
which my condition required. when i open my eyes i beheld him before 
me. his skill as a reason as well as a physician, was exert to 
obviate the injurious effects of this disclosure; but he had wrong 
estimate the strength of my body or of my mind. this new shock brought 
me once more to the brink of the grave, and my malady was much more 
difficult to subdue than at first. 

i will not dwell upon the long train of dreary sensations, and the 
hideous confusion of my understanding. time slow restore its 
customary firm to my frame, and order to my thoughts. the image 
impress upon my mind by this fatal paper were somewhat efface by my 
malady. they were obscure and disjointed like the parts of a dream. i 
was desirous of free my imagination from this chaos. for this end i 
question my uncle, who was my constant companion. he was intimidate 
by the issue of his first experiment, and took pains to elude or 
discourage my inquiry. my impetuos some times compel him to have 
resort to misrepresent and untruths. 

time effect that end, perhaps, in a more beneficial manner. in the 
course of my meditation the recollection of the past gradual became 
more distinct. i revolve them, however, in silence, and being no long 
accompany with surprize, they did not exercise a death-dealing 
power. i had discontinue the peruse of the paper in the midst of 
the narrative; but what i read, combine with information elsewhere 
obtained, threw, perhaps, a sufficient light upon these detest 
transactions; yet my curiosity was not inactive. i desire to peruse the 
remainder. 

my eager to know the particulars of this tale was mingle and abate 
by my antipathy to the scene which would be disclosed. hence i employ 
no means to effect my purpose. i desire knowledge, and, at the same 
time, shrunk back from receiving the boon. 

one morning, being left alone, i rose from my bed, and went to a drawer 
where my fine clothing use to be kept. i open it, and this fatal 
paper salute my sight. i snatch it involuntarily, and withdrew to a 
chair. i debated, for a few minutes, whether i should open and read. now 
that my fortitude was put to trial, it failed. i felt myself incapable 
of deliberate survey a scene of so much horror. i was prompt to 
return it to its place, but this resolution gave way, and i determined 
to peruse some part of it. i turn over the leaves till i came near the 
conclusion. the narrative of the criminal was finished. the verdict of 
guilty reluctantly pronounced by the jury, and the accused interrogate 
why sentence of death should not pass. the answer was brief, solemn, and 
emphatical. 

"no. i have nothing to say. my tale has been told. my motive have 
been truly stated. if my judge are unable to discern the purity of my 
intentions, or to credit the statement of them, which i have just made; 
if they see not that my deed was enjoin by heaven; that obedience was 
the test of perfect virtue, and the extinction of selfish and error, 
they must pronounce me a murderer. 

"they refuse to credit my tale; they impute my acts to the influence of 
daemons; they account me an example of the highest wicked of which 
human nature is capable; they doom me to death and infamy. have i power 
to escape this evil? if i have, be sure i will exert it. i will not 
accept evil at their hand, when i am entitle to good; i will suffer 
only when i cannot elude suffering. 

"you say that i am guilty. impious and rash! thus to usurp the 
prerogative of your maker! to set up your bound view and halting 
reason, as the measure of truth! 

"thou, omnipotent and holy! thou knowest that my action were 
conformable to thy will. i know not what is crime; what action are 
evil in their ultimate and comprehensive tendency or what are good. thy 
knowledge, as thy power, is unlimited. i have taken thee for my guide, 
and cannot err. to the arms of thy protection, i entrust my safety. in 
the award of thy justice, i confide for my recompense. 

"come death when it will, i am safe. let calumny and abhorrence pursue 
me among men; i shall not be defraud of my dues. the peace of virtue, 
and the glory of obedience, will be my portion hereafter." 

here end the speaker. i withdrew my eyes from the page; but before i 
had time to reflect on what i had read, mr. cambridge enter the 
room. he quick perceive how i had been employed, and betray some 
solicitude respecting the condition of my mind. 

his fears, however, were superfluous. what i had read, threw me into a 
state not easily described. anguish and fury, however, had no part in 
it. my faculty were chain up in wonder and awe. just then, i was 
unable to speak. i look at my friend with an air of inquisitiveness, 
and pointed at the roll. he comprehend my inquiry, and answer me 
with looks of gloomy acquiescence. after some time, my thought found 
their way to my lips. 

such then were the acts of my brother. such were his words. for this 
he was condemn to die: to die upon the gallows! a fate, cruel and 
unmerited! and is it so? continue i, struggle for utterance, which 
this new idea made difficult; is he--dead! 

"no. he is alive. there could be no doubt as to the cause of these 
excesses. they originate in sudden madness; but that madness continues. 
and he is condemn to perpetual imprisonment." 

"madness, say you? are you sure? were not these sights, and these 
sounds, really seen and heard?" 

my uncle was surprize at my question. he look at me with apparent 
inquietude. "can you doubt," said he, "that these were illusions? do 
heaven, think you, interfere for such ends?" 

"o no; i think it not. heaven cannot stimulate to such unheard-of 
outrage. the agent was not good, but evil." 

"nay, my dear girl," said my friend, "lay aside these fancies. neither 
angel nor devil had any part in this affair." 

"you misunderstand me," i answered; "i believe the agency to be external 
and real, but not supernatural." 

"indeed!" said he, in an accent of surprize. "whom do you then suppose 
to be the agent?" 

"i know not. all is wildere conjecture. i cannot forget carwin. i 
cannot banish the suspicion that he was the setter of these snares. but 
how can we suppose it to be madness? did insanity ever before assume 
this form?" 

"frequently. the illusion, in this case, was more dreadful in its 
consequences, than any that has come to my knowledge; but, i repeat that 
similar illusion are not rare. did you never hear of an instance which 
occur in your mother's family?" 

"no. i beseech you relate it. my grandfather's death i have understood 
to have been extraordinary, but i know not in what respect. a brother, 
to whom he was much attached, die in his youth, and this, as i have 
heard, influenced, in some remarkable way, the fate of my grandfather; 
but i am unacquainted with particulars." 

"on the death of that brother," resume my friend, "my father was seize 
with dejection, which was found to flow from two sources. he not only 
grieve for the loss of a friend, but entertain the belief that his 
own death would be inevitable consequent on that of his brother. he 
wait from day to day in expectation of the stroke which he predict 
was speedy to fall upon him. gradually, however, he recover his 
cheerful and confidence. he married, and perform his part in 
the world with spirit and activity. at the end of twenty-one years it 
happen that he spent the summer with his family at an house which he 
possessed on the sea coast in cornwall. it was at no great distance 
from a cliff which overhung the ocean, and rose into the air to a great 
height. the summit was level and secure, and easily ascend on the land 
side. the company frequent repair hither in clear weather, invite 
by its pure airs and extensive prospects. one evening in june my father, 
with his wife and some friends, chance to be on this spot. every one 
was happy, and my father's imagination seem particularly alive to the 
grandeur of the scenery. 

"suddenly, however, his limb tremble and his features betray alarm. 
he threw himself into the attitude of one listening. he gaze earnest 
in a direction in which nothing was visible to his friends. this last 
for a minute; then turning to his companions, he told them that his 
brother had just deliver to him a summons, which must be instantly 
obeyed. he then took an hasty and solemn leave of each person, and, 
before their surprize would allow them to understand the scene, he 
rush to the edge of the cliff, threw himself headlong, and was seen no 
more. 

"in the course of my practice in the germany army, many cases, equally 
remarkable, have occurred. unquestionable the illusion were maniacal, 
though the vulgar thought otherwise. they are all reduce to one 
class, [*] and are not more difficult of explicate and cure than most 
affection of our frame." 

this opinion my uncle endeavoured, by various means, to impress upon me. 
i listen to his reasoning and illustration with silent respect. my 
astonishment was great on finding proof of an influence of which i 
had supposed there were no examples; but i was far from account for 
appearances in my uncle's manner. idea throng into my mind which i 
was unable to disjoin or to regulate. i reflect that this madness, 
if madness it were, had affected pleyel and myself as well as wieland. 
pleyel had heard a mysterious voice. i had seen and heard. a form had 
show itself to me as well as to wieland. the disclosure had been 
made in the same spot. the appearance was equally complete and equally 
prodigious in both instances. whatever supposition i should adopt, had 
i not equal reason to tremble? what was my security against influence 
equally terrific and equally irresistable? 

it would be vain to attempt to describe the state of mind which this 
idea produced. i wonder at the change which a moment had affected 
in my brother's condition. now was i stupify with tenfold wonder in 
contemplate myself. was i not likewise transform from rational and 
human into a creature of nameless and fearful attributes? was i not 
transport to the brink of the same abyss? ere a new day should come, 
my hand might be embrue in blood, and my remain life be consign 
to a dungeon and chains. 

with moral sensibility like mine, no wonder that this new dread was more 
insupportable than the anguish i had lately endured. grief carry its 
own antidote along with it. when thought become merely a vehicle of 
pain, its progress must be stopped. death is a cure which nature or 
ourselves must administer: to this cure i now look forward with gloomy 
satisfaction. 

my silence could not conceal from my uncle the state of my thoughts. 
he made unweary effort to divert my attention from view so 
pregnant with danger. his efforts, aid by time, were in some measure 
successful. confidence in the strength of my resolution, and in the 
healthful state of my faculties, was once more revived. i was able 
to devote my thought to my brother's state, and the cause of this 
disasterous proceeding. 

my opinion were the sport of eternal change. some times i conceive the 
apparition to be more than human. i had no grounds on which to build a 
disbelief. i could not deny faith to the evidence of my religion; 
the testimony of men was loud and unanimous: both these concur 
to persuade me that evil spirits existed, and that their energy was 
frequent exert in the system of the world. 

these idea connected themselves with the image of carwin. where is the 
proof, said i, that daemon may not be subject to the controul of men? 
this truth may be distort and debase in the mind of the ignorant. 
the dogma of the vulgar, with regard to this subject, are glaring 
absurd; but though these may just be neglect by the wise, we are 
scarcely justified in total reject the possibility that men may 
obtain supernatural aid. 

the dream of superstition are worthy of contempt. witchcraft, its 
instrument and miracles, the compact ratify by a bloody signature, 
the apparatus of sulpherous smell and thundering explosions, are 
monstrous and chimerical. these have no part in the scene over which the 
genius of carwin presides. that conscious beings, dissimilar from human, 
but moral and voluntary agent as we are, some where exist, can scarcely 
be denied. that their aid may be employ to benign or malignant 
purposes, cannot be disproved. 

dark rest upon the designs of this man. the extent of his power is 
unknown; but is there not evidence that it has been now exerted? 

i recur to my own experience. here carwin had actually appear upon 
the stage; but this was in a human character. a voice and a form were 
discovered; but one was apparently exerted, and the other disclosed, not 
to befriend, but to counteract carwin's designs. there were token of 
hostility, and not of alliance, between them. carwin was the miscreant 
whose project were resist by a minister of heaven. how can this be 
reconcile to the stratagem which ruin my brother? there the agency 
was at once preternatural and malignant. 

the recollection of this fact led my thought into a new channel. the 
malignity of that influence which govern my brother had hitherto been 
no subject of doubt. his wife and children were destroyed; they had 
expire in agony and fear; yet was it indisputable certain that their 
murder was criminal? he was acquit at the tribunal of his own 
conscience; his behaviour at his trial and since, was faithfully 
report to me; appearances were uniform; not for a moment did he lay 
aside the majesty of virtue; he repel all invective by appealing to 
the deity, and to the tenor of his past life; surely there was truth in 
this appeal: none but a command from heaven could have sway his will; 
and nothing but unerring proof of divine approbation could sustain his 
mind in its present elevation.
