chapter xxi 


such, for some time, was the course of my meditations. my weakness, and 
my aversion to be pointed at as an object of surprize or compassion, 
prevent me from go into public. i studious avoid the visit of 
those who came to express their sympathy, or gratify their curiosity. 
my uncle was my principal companion. nothing more powerful tend to 
console me than his conversation. 

with regard to pleyel, my feelings seem to have undergone a total 
revolution. it often happen that one passion supplant another. late 
disaster had rent my heart, and now that the wound was in some degree 
closed, the love which i had cherish for this man seem likewise to 
have vanished. 

hitherto, indeed, i had had no cause for despair. i was innocent of that 
offence which had estrange him from my presence. i might reasonably 
expect that my innocence would at some time be irresistable 
demonstrated, and his affection for me be revive with his esteem. now 
my aversion to be thought culpable by him continued, but was unattended 
with the same impatience. i desire the removal of his suspicions, 
not for the sake of regain his love, but because i delight in the 
venerate of so excellent a man, and because he himself would derive 
pleasure from conviction of my integrity. 

my uncle had early informed me that pleyel and he had seen each other, 
since the return of the latter from europe. amidst the topic of their 
conversation, i discover that pleyel had careful omit the mention 
of those event which had drawn upon me so much abhorrence. i could 
not account for his silence on this subject. perhaps time or some new 
discovery had alter or shaken his opinion. perhaps he was unwilling, 
though i were guilty, to injure me in the opinion of my venerable 
kinsman. i understood that he had frequent visit me during 
my disease, had watch many successive nights by my bedside, and 
manifest the utmost anxiety on my account. 

the journey which he was prepare to take, at the termination of our 
last interview, the catastrophe of the ensue night induce him to 
delay. the motive of this journey i had, till now, total mistaken. 
they were explain to me by my uncle, whose tale excited my 
astonishment without awakening my regret. in a different state of mind, 
it would have add unspeakable to my distress, but now it was more 
a source of pleasure than pain. this, perhaps, is not the least 
extraordinary of the fact contained in this narrative. it will excite 
less wonder when i add, that my indifference was temporary, and that the 
lapse of a few days shew me that my feelings were deaden for a time, 
rather than finally extinguished. 

theresa de stolberg was alive. she had conceive the resolution of 
seek her lover in america. to conceal her flight, she had cause the 
report of her death to be propagated. she put herself under the conduct 
of bertrand, the faithful servant of pleyel. the pacquet which the 
latter received from the hand of his servant, contained the tidings of 
her safe arrival at boston, and to meet her there was the purpose of his 
journey. 

this discovery had set this man's character in a new light. i had 
mistaken the heroism of friendship for the phrenzy of love. he who 
had gain my affections, may be supposed to have previous entitle 
himself to my reverence; but the levity which had formerly characterize 
the behaviour of this man, tend to obscure the great of his 
sentiments. i did not fail to remark, that since this lady was still 
alive, the voice in the temple which assert her death, must either 
have been intended to deceive, or have been itself deceived. the latter 
supposition was inconsistent with the notion of a spiritual, and the 
former with that of a benevolent being. 

when my disease abated, pleyel had forborne his visits, and had lately 
set out upon this journey. this amount to a proof that my guilt was 
still believe by him. i was grieve for his errors, but trust that my 
vindication would, soon or later, be made. 

meanwhile, tumultuous thought were again set afloat by a proposal 
made to me by my uncle. he imagine that new airs would restore my 
languish constitution, and a varied succession of object tend to 
repair the shock which my mind had received. for this end, he propose 
to me to take up my abode with him in france or italy. 

at a more prosperous period, this scheme would have pleased for its own 
sake. now my heart sicken at the prospect of nature. the world of man 
was shrowde in misery and blood, and constitute a loathsome spectacle. 
i willing closed my eyes in sleep, and regret that the respite it 
afford me was so short. i marked with satisfaction the progress of 
decay in my frame, and consent to live, merely in the hope that 
the course of nature would speedy relieve me from the burthen. 
nevertheless, as he persist in his scheme, i concur in it merely 
because he was entitle to my gratitude, and because my refusal gave him 
pain. 

no soon was he informed of my consent, than he told me i must make 
immediate preparation to embark, as the ship in which he had engaged 
a passage would be ready to depart in three days. this expedition was 
unexpected. there was an impatience in his manner when he urge the 
necessity of dispatch that excited my surprize. when i question him as 
to the cause of this haste, he generally state reason which, at 
that time, i could not deny to be plausible; but which, on the review, 
appear insufficient. i suspect that the true motive were concealed, 
and believe that these motive had some connection with my brother's 
destiny. 

i now recollect that the information respecting wieland which had, 
from time to time, been impart to me, was always accompany with airs 
of reserve and mysteriousness. what had appear sufficient explicit 
at the time it was uttered, i now remember to have been falter 
and ambiguous. i was resolve to remove my doubts, by visiting the 
unfortunate man in his dungeon. 

heretofore the idea of this visit had occur to me; but the horrors 
of his dwelling-place, his wild yet placid physiognomy, his neglect 
locks, the fetter which constrained his limbs, terrible as they were in 
description, how could i endure to behold! 

now, however, that i was prepare to take an everlasting farewell of my 
country, now that an ocean was henceforth to separate me from him, how 
could i part without an interview? i would examine his situation with my 
own eyes. i would know whether the representations which had been made 
to me were true. perhaps the sight of the sister whom he was wont 
to love with a passion more than fraternal, might have an auspicious 
influence on his malady. 

have form this resolution, i wait to communicate it to mr. 
cambridge. i was aware that, without his concurrence, i could not hope 
to carry it into execution, and could discover no objection to which 
it was liable. if i had not been deceive as to his condition, no 
inconvenience could arise from this proceeding. his consent, therefore, 
would be the test of his sincerity. 

i seize this opportunity to state my wish on this head. my suspicion 
were confirmed by the manner in which my request affected him. after 
some pause, in which his countenance betray every mark of perplexity, 
he said to me, "why would you pay this visit? what useful purpose can it 
serve?" 

"we are preparing," said i, "to leave the country forever: what kind of 
being should i be to leave behind me a brother in calamity without even 
a parting interview? indulge me for three minutes in the sight of him. 
my heart will be much easy after i have look at him, and shed a few 
tear in his presence." 

"i believe otherwise. the sight of him would only augment your distress, 
without contributing, in any degree, to his benefit." 

"i know not that," return i. "surely the sympathy of his sister, 
proof that her tender is as lively as ever, must be a source 
of satisfaction to him. at present he must regard all mankind as his 
enemy and calumniators. his sister he, probably, conceive to partake 
in the general infatuation, and to join in the cry of abhorrence that 
is raise against him. to be undeceive in this respect, to be assured 
that, however i may impute his conduct to delusion, i still retain all 
my former affection for his person, and venerate for the purity of his 
motives, cannot but afford him pleasure. when he hear that i have left 
the country, without even the ceremonious attention of a visit, what 
will he think of me? his magnanimity may hinder him from repining, but 
he will surely consider my behaviour as savage and unfeeling. indeed, 
dear sir, i must pay this visit. to embark with you without pay it, 
will be impossible. it may be of no service to him, but will enable me 
to acquit myself of what i cannot but esteem a duty. besides," continue 
i, "if it be a mere fit of insanity that has seize him, may not my 
presence chance to have a salutary influence? the mere sight of me, it 
is not impossible, may rectify his perceptions." 

"ay," said my uncle, with some eagerness; "it is by no means impossible 
that your interview may have that effect; and for that reason, beyond 
all others, would i dissuade you from it." 

i express my surprize at this declaration. "is it not to be desire 
that an error so fatal as this should be rectified?" 

"i wonder at your question. reflect on the consequence of this error. 
has he not destroy the wife whom he loved, the children whom he 
idolized? what is it that enable him to bear the remembrance, but the 
belief that he act as his duty enjoined? would you rash bereave him 
of this belief? would you restore him to himself, and convince him 
that he was instigate to this dreadful outrage by a perversion of his 
organs, or a delusion from hell? 

"now his vision are joyous and elate. he conceive himself to have 
reach a lofty degree of virtue, than any other human being. the 
merit of his sacrifice is only enhance in the eyes of superior beings, 
by the detest that pursue him here, and the suffering to which he 
is condemned. the belief that even his sister has desert him, and 
gone over to his enemies, add to his sublime of feelings, and his 
confidence in divine approbation and future recompense. 

"let him be undeceive in this respect, and what flood of despair and 
of horror will overwhelm him! instead of glowing approbation and serene 
hope, will he not hate and torture himself? self-violence, or a phrenzy 
far more savage and destructive than this, may be expect to succeed. i 
beseech you, therefore, to relinquish this scheme. if you calm reflect 
upon it, you will discover that your duty lie in careful shun 
him." 

mr. cambridge's reasoning suggest view to my understanding, that had 
not hitherto occurred. i could not but admit their validity, but they 
shewed, in a new light, the depth of that misfortune in which my brother 
was plunged. i was silent and irresolute. 

presently, i considered, that whether wieland was a maniac, a faithful 
servant of his god, the victim of hellish illusions, or the dupe of 
human imposture, was by no means certain. in this state of my mind it 
became me to be silent during the visit that i projected. this visit 
should be brief: i should be satisfy merely to snatch a look at him. 
admit that a change in his opinion were not to be desired, there 
was no danger from the conduct which i should pursue, that this change 
should be wrought. 

but i could not conquer my uncle's aversion to this scheme. yet i 
persisted, and he found that to make me voluntary relinquish it, it 
was necessary to be more explicit than he had hitherto been. he took 
both my hands, and anxious examine my countenance as he spoke, 
"clara," said he, "this visit must not be paid. we must hasten with the 
utmost expedition from this shore. it is folly to conceal the truth 
from you, and, since it is only by disclose the truth that you can be 
prevail upon to lay aside this project, the truth shall be told. 

"o my dear girl!" continue he with increase energy in his accent, 
"your brother's phrenzy is, indeed, stupendous and frightful. the soul 
that formerly actuate his frame has disappeared. the same form remains; 
but the wise and benevolent wieland is no more. a fury that is rapacious 
of blood, that lift his strength almost above that of mortals, that 
bends all his energy to the destruction of whatever was once dear to 
him, possess him wholly. 

"you must not enter his dungeon; his eyes will no soon be fixed upon 
you, than an exertion of his force will be made. he will shake off his 
fetter in a moment, and rush upon you. no interposition will then be 
strong or quick enough to save you. 

"the phantom that has urge him to the murder of catharine and her 
children is not yet appeased. your life, and that of pleyel, are exact 
from him by this imaginary being. he is eager to comply with this 
demand. twice he has escape from his prison. the first time, he no 
soon found himself at liberty, than he haste to pleyel's house. it 
being midnight, the latter was in bed. wieland penetrate unobserved 
to his chamber, and open his curtain. happily, pleyel awoke at the 
critical moment, and escape the fury of his kinsman, by leap from 
his chamber-window into the court. happily, he reach the ground 
without injury. alarm were given, and after diligent search, your 
brother was found in a chamber of your house, whither, no doubt, he 
had sought you. his chains, and the watchful of his guards, were 
redoubled; but again, by some miracle, he restore himself to liberty. 
he was now incautious apprize of the place of your abode: and had not 
information of his escape been instantly given, your death would have 
been add to the number of his atrocious acts. 

"you now see the danger of your project. you must not only forbear to 
visit him, but if you would save him from the crime of embrue his 
hand in your blood, you must leave the country. there is no hope that 
his malady will end but with his life, and no precaution will ensure 
your safety, but that of place the ocean between you. 

"i confess i came over with an intention to reside among you, but 
these disaster have change my views. your own safety and my happiness 
require that you should accompany me in my return, and i entreat you to 
give your cheerful concurrence to this measure." 

after these representations from my uncle, it was impossible to retain 
my purpose. i readily consent to seclude myself from wieland's 
presence. i likewise acquiesce in the proposal to go to europe; not 
that i ever expect to arrive there, but because, since my principles 
forbad me to assail my own life, change had some tendency to make 
supportable the few days which disease should spare to me. 

what a tale had thus been unfolded! i was hunt to death, not by 
one whom my misconduct had exasperated, who was conscious of illicit 
motives, and who sought his end by circumvent and surprize; but by 
one who deem himself commission for this act by heaven; who 
regard this career of horror as the last refinement of virtue; whose 
implacable was proportion to the reverence and love which he felt 
for me, and who was inaccessible to the fear of punishment and ignominy! 

in vain should i endeavour to stay his hand by urge the claim of 
a sister or friend: these were his only reason for pursue my 
destruction. had i been a stranger to his blood; had i been the most 
worthless of human kind; my safety had not been endangered. 

surely, said i, my fate is without example. the phrenzy which is charged 
upon my brother, must belong to myself. my foe is manacle and guarded; 
but i derive no security from these restraints. i live not in a 
community of savages; yet, whether i sit or walk, go into crouds, 
or hide myself in solitude, my life is marked for a prey to inhuman 
violence; i am in perpetual danger of perishing; of perishing under the 
grasp of a brother! 

i recollect the omen of this destiny; i remember the gulf to which 
my brother's invitation had conduct me; i remember that, when on the 
brink of danger, the author of my peril was depict by my fear in 
his form: thus realized, were the creature of prophetic sleep, and of 
wakeful terror! 

these image were unavoidable connected with that of carwin. in 
this paroxysm of distress, my attention fasten on him as the grand 
deceiver; the author of this black conspiracy; the intelligence that 
govern in this storm. 

some relief is afford in the midst of suffering, when its author is 
discover or imagined; and an object found on which we may pour out 
our indignation and our vengeance. i ran over the event that had taken 
place since the origin of our intercourse with him, and reflect on the 
tenor of that description which was received from ludloe. mixed up with 
notions of supernatural agency, were the vehement suspicion which i 
entertained, that carwin was the enemy whose machination had destroy 
us. 

i thirst for knowledge and for vengeance. i regard my hasty 
departure with reluctance, since it would remove me from the means by 
which this knowledge might be obtained, and this vengeance gratified. 
this departure was to take place in two days. at the end of two days 
i was to bid an eternal adieu to my native country. should i not pay a 
parting visit to the scene of these disasters? should i not bedew with 
my tear the grave of my sister and her children? should i not explore 
their desolate habitation, and gather from the sight of its wall and 
furniture food for my eternal melancholy? 

this suggestion was succeed by a secret shuddering. some disastrous 
influence appear to overhang the scene. how many memorial should i 
meet with serving to recall the image of those i had lost! 

i was tempt to relinquish my design, when it occur to me that i 
had left among my papers a journal of transactions in shorthand. i 
was employ in this manuscript on that night when pleyel's incautious 
curiosity tempt him to look over my shoulder. i was then recording my 
adventure in the recess, an imperfect sight of which led him into such 
fatal errors. 

i had regulate the disposition of all my property. this manuscript, 
however, which contained the most secret transactions of my life, i was 
desirous of destroying. for this end i must return to my house, and this 
i immediately determined to do. 

i was not willing to expose myself to opposition from my friends, 
by mention my design; i therefore bespoke the use of mr. hallet's 
chaise, under pretence of enjoy an airing, as the day was remarkably 
bright. 

this request was gladly comply with, and i direct the servant to 
conduct me to mettingen. i dismiss him at the gate, intend to use, 
in returning, a carriage belong to my brother. 



chapter xxii 


the inhabitant of the hut received me with a mixture of joy and 
surprize. their homely welcome, and their artless sympathy, were 
grateful to my feelings. in the midst of their inquiries, as to my 
health, they avoid all allusion to the source of my malady. they were 
honest creatures, and i love them well. i participate in the tear 
which they shed when i mention to them my speedy departure for europe, 
and promise to acquaint them with my welfare during my long absence. 

they express great surprize when i informed them of my intention to 
visit my cottage. alarm and foreboding overspread their features, and 
they attempt to dissuade me from visiting an house which they firm 
believe to be haunt by a thousand ghastly apparitions. 

these apprehensions, however, had no power over my conduct. i took an 
irregular path which led me to my own house. all was vacant and forlorn. 
a small enclosure, near which the path led, was the burying-ground 
belong to the family. this i was oblige to pass. once i had intended 
to enter it, and ponder on the emblem and inscription which my uncle 
had cause to be made on the tomb of catharine and her children; but 
now my heart falter as i approached, and i hasten forward, that 
distance might conceal it from my view. 

when i approach the recess, my heart again sunk. i avert my eyes, 
and left it behind me as quick as possible. silence reign through 
my habitation, and a dark which closed door and shutter produced. 
every object was connected with mine or my brother's history. i pass 
the entry, mount the stair, and unlock the door of my chamber. 
it was with difficulty that i curb my fancy and smother my fears. 
slight movements and casual sound were transform into beckon 
shadow and calling shapes. 

i proceed to the closet. i open and look round it with 
fearfulness. all things were in their accustomed order. i sought and 
found the manuscript where i was use to deposit it. this being secured, 
there was nothing to detain me; yet i stood and contemplate awhile the 
furniture and wall of my chamber. i remember how long this apartment 
had been a sweet and tranquil asylum; i compare its former state with 
its present dreariness, and reflect that i now beheld it for the last 
time. 

here it was that the incomprehensible behaviour of carwin was witnessed: 
this the stage on which that enemy of man shew himself for a moment 
unmasked. here the menace of murder were waft to my ear; and here 
these menace were executed. 

these thought had a tendency to take from me my self-command. my feeble 
limb refuse to support me, and i sunk upon a chair. incoherent and 
half-articulate exclamation escape my lips. the name of carwin was 
uttered, and eternal woes, woe like that which his malice had entail 
upon us, were heap upon him. i invoke all-seeing heaven to drag to 
light and to punish this betrayer, and accused its providence for have 
thus long delay the retribution that was due to so enormous a guilt. 

i have said that the window shutter were closed. a feeble light, 
however, found entrance through the crevices. a small window illuminate 
the closet, and the door being closed, a dim ray stream through 
the key-hole. a kind of twilight was thus created, sufficient for the 
purpose of vision; but, at the same time, involve all minute object 
in obscurity. 

this dark suit the colour of my thoughts. i sicken at the 
remembrance of the past. the prospect of the future excited my loathing. 
i mutter in a low voice, why should i live longer? why should i drag a 
miserable being? all, for whom i ought to live, have perished. am i not 
myself hunt to death? 

at that moment, my despair sudden became vigorous. my nerves were no 
long unstrung. my powers, that had long been deadened, were revived. 
my bosom swell with a sudden energy, and the conviction dart through 
my mind, that to end my torment was, at once, practicable and wise. 

i knew how to find way to the recess of life. i could use a lancet 
with some skill, and could distinguish between vein and artery. by 
piercing deep into the latter, i should shun the evil which the future 
had in store for me, and take refuge from my woe in quiet death. 

i start on my feet, for my feeble was gone, and haste to the 
closet. a lancet and other small instrument were preserve in a 
case which i had deposit here. inattentive as i was to foreign 
considerations, my ear were still open to any sound of mysterious 
import that should occur. i thought i heard a step in the entry. my 
purpose was suspended, and i cast an eager glance at my chamber door, 
which was open. no one appeared, unless the shadow which i discern 
upon the floor, was the outline of a man. if it were, i was authorize 
to suspect that some one was post close to the entrance, who possibly 
had overheard my exclamations. 

my teeth chattered, and a wild confusion took place of my momentary 
calm. thus it was when a terrific visage had disclose itself on a 
former night. thus it was when the evil destiny of wieland assume the 
lineament of something human. what horrid apparition was prepare to 
blast my sight? 

still i listen and gazed. not long, for the shadow moved; a foot, 
unshape and huge, was thrust forward; a form advanced from its 
concealment, and stalk into the room. it was carwin! while i had 
breath i shrieked. while i had power over my muscles, i motion with 
my hand that he should vanish. my exertion could not last long; i sunk 
into a fit. 

o that this grateful oblivion had last for ever! too quick i 
recover my senses. the power of distinct vision was no soon restore 
to me, than this hateful form again present itself, and i once more 
relapsed. 

a second time, untoward nature recall me from the sleep of death. 
i found myself stretch upon the bed. when i had power to look up, i 
remember only that i had cause to fear. my distempered fancy fashion 
to itself no distinguishable image. i threw a languid glance round me; 
once more my eyes light upon carwin. 

he was seat on the floor, his back rest against the wall, his knee 
were drawn up, and his face was bury in his hands. that his station 
was at some distance, that his attitude was not menacing, that his 
ominous visage was concealed, may account for my now escape a shock, 
violent as those which were past. i withdrew my eyes, but was not again 
desert by my senses. 

on perceive that i had recover my sensibility, he lift his head. 
this motion attract my attention. his countenance was mild, but sorrow 
and astonishment sat upon his features. i avert my eyes and feeble 
exclaimed--"o! fly--fly far and for ever!--i cannot behold you and 
live!" 

he did not rise upon his feet, but clasp his hands, and said in a 
tone of deprecation--"i will fly. i am become a fiend, the sight of whom 
destroys. yet tell me my offence! you have link curse with my name; 
you ascribe to me a malice monstrous and infernal. i look around; all 
is lonely and desert! this house and your brother's are solitary and 
dismantled! you die away at the sight of me! my fear whisper that some 
deed of horror has been perpetrated; that i am the undesign cause." 

what language was this? had he not avowed himself a ravisher? had not 
this chamber witness his atrocious purposes? i besought him with new 
vehemence to go. 

he lift his eyes--"great heaven! what have i done? i think i know 
the extent of my offences. i have acted, but my action have possibly 
effect more than i designed. this fear has brought me back from my 
retreat. i come to repair the evil of which my rash was the cause, 
and to prevent more evil. i come to confess my errors." 

"wretch!" i cry when my suffocate emotion would permit me to speak, 
"the ghost of my sister and her children, do they not rise to accuse 
thee? who was it that blasted the intellect of wieland? who was it 
that urge him to fury, and guide him to murder? who, but thou and the 
devil, with whom thou art confederated?" 

at these words a new spirit pervade his countenance. his eyes once more 
appeal to heaven. "if i have memory, if i have being, i am innocent. i 
intended no ill; but my folly, indirect and remotely, may have cause 
it; but what words are these! your brother lunatic! his children dead!" 

what should i infer from this deportment? was the ignorance which these 
words imply real or pretended?--yet how could i imagine a mere human 
agency in these events? but if the influence was preternatural or 
maniacal in my brother's case, they must be equally so in my own. then 
i remember that the voice exerted, was to save me from carwin's 
attempts. these idea tend to abate my abhorrence of this man, and to 
detect the absurd of my accusations. 

"alas!" said i, "i have no one to accuse. leave me to my fate. fly from 
a scene stain with cruelty; devoted to despair." 

carwin stood for a time muse and mournful. at length he said, "what 
has happened? i came to expiate my crimes: let me know them in their 
full extent. i have horrible forebodings! what has happened?" 

i was silent; but recollect the intimate given by this man when he 
was detect in my closet, which imply some knowledge of that power 
which interfere in my favor, i eager inquired, "what was that voice 
which call upon me to hold when i attempt to open the closet? what 
face was that which i saw at the bottom of the stairs? answer me truly." 

"i came to confess the truth. your allusion are horrible and strange. 
perhaps i have but faint conception of the evil which my infatuation 
has produced; but what remains i will perform. it was my voice that you 
heard! it was my face that you saw!" 

for a moment i doubt whether my remembrance of event were not 
confused. how could he be at once station at my shoulder and shut up 
in my closet? how could he stand near me and yet be invisible? but if 
carwin's were the thrill voice and the fiery visage which i had heard 
and seen, then was he the prompter of my brother, and the author of 
these dismal outrages. 

once more i avert my eyes and struggle for speech. "begone! thou man 
of mischief! remorseless and implacable miscreant! begone!" 

"i will obey," said he in a disconsolate voice; "yet, wretch as i am, 
am i unworthy to repair the evil that i have committed? i came as a 
repentant criminal. it is you whom i have injured, and at your bar am 
i willing to appear, and confess and expiate my crimes. i have deceive 
you: i have sport with your terrors: i have plot to destroy your 
reputation. i come now to remove your errors; to set you beyond the 
reach of similar fears; to rebuild your fame as far as i am able. 

"this is the amount of my guilt, and this the fruit of my remorse. will 
you not hear me? listen to my confession, and then denounce punishment. 
all i ask is a patient audience." 

"what!" i replied, "was not thine the voice that command my brother to 
imbrue his hand in the blood of his children--to strangle that angel of 
sweet his wife? has he not vow my death, and the death of pleyel, 
at thy bidding? hast thou not made him the butcher of his family; 
change him who was the glory of his species into worse than brute; 
rob him of reason, and consign the rest of his days to fetter and 
stripes?" 

carwin's eyes glared, and his limb were petrify at this intelligence. 
no words were requisite to prove him guiltless of these enormities: at 
the time, however, i was nearly insensible to these exculpatory tokens. 
he walk to the farther end of the room, and have recover some 
degree of composure, he spoke-- 

"i am not this villain; i have slain no one; i have prompt none to 
slay; i have handle a tool of wonderful efficacy without malignant 
intentions, but without caution; ample will be the punishment of my 
temerity, if my conduct has contribute to this evil." he paused.-- 

i likewise was silent. i struggle to command myself so far as to listen 
to the tale which he should tell. observing this, he continued-- 

"you are not apprize of the existence of a power which i possess. i 
know not by what name to call it. [*] it enable me to mimic exactly the 
voice of another, and to modify the sound so that it shall appear to 
come from what quarter, and be utter at what distance i please. 

"i know not that every one possess this power. perhaps, though a 
casual position of my organ in my youth shew me that i possessed 
it, it is an art which may be taught to all. would to god i had die 
unknowing of the secret! it has produce nothing but degrade and 
calamity. 

"for a time the possession of so potent and stupendous an endowment 
elated me with pride. unfortify by principle, subject to poverty, 
stimulate by headlong passions, i made this powerful engine subservient 
to the supply of my wants, and the gratification of my vanity. i shall 
not mention how diligent i cultivated this gift, which seem capable 
of unlimited improvement; nor detail the various occasion on which 
it was successful exert to lead superstition, conquer avarice, or 
excite awe. 

"i left america, which is my native soil, in my youth. i have been 
engaged in various scene of life, in which my peculiar talent has been 
exercise with more or less success. i was finally betray by one who 
call himself my friend, into acts which cannot be justified, though 
they are susceptible of apology. 

"the perfidy of this man compel me to withdraw from europe. i 
return to my native country, uncertain whether silence and obscurity 
would save me from his malice. i reside in the purlieus of the city. i 
put on the garb and assume the manners of a clown. 

"my chief recreation was walking. my principal haunt were the lawn 
and garden of mettingen. in this delightful region the luxuriance 
of nature had been chasten by judicious art, and each successive 
contemplation unfold new enchantments. 

"i was studious of seclusion: i was satiate with the intercourse of 
mankind, and discretion require me to shun their intercourse. for 
these reason i long avoid the observation of your family, and chiefly 
visit these precincts at night. 

"i was never weary of admire the position and ornament of the 
temple. many a night have i pass under its roof, revolving no pleasing 
meditations. when, in my frequent rambles, i perceive this apartment 
was occupied, i gave a different direction to my steps. one evening, 
when a shower had just passed, judge by the silence that no one 
was within, i ascend to this building. glancing careless round, i 
perceive an open letter on the pedestal. to read it was doubtless an 
offence against politeness. of this offence, however, i was guilty. 

"scarcely had i gone half through when i was alarm by the approach 
of your brother. to scramble down the cliff on the opposite side 
was impracticable. i was unprepared to meet a stranger. besides 
the aukward attend such an interview in these circumstances, 
concealment was necessary to my safety. a thousand times had i vow 
never again to employ the dangerous talent which i possessed; but such 
was the force of habit and the influence of present convenience, that i 
use this method of arrest his progress and leading him back to the 
house, with his errand, whatever it was, unperformed. i had often caught 
parts, from my station below, of your conversation in this place, and 
was well acquaint with the voice of your sister. 

"some week after this i was again quiet seat in this recess. the 
late of the hour secure me, as i thought, from all interruption. 
in this, however, i was mistaken, for wieland and pleyel, as i judge by 
their voices, earnest in dispute, ascend the hill. 

"i was not sensible that any inconvenience could possibly have flow 
from my former exertion; yet it was follow with compunction, because 
it was a deviation from a path which i had assign to myself. now 
my aversion to this means of escape was enforce by an unauthorized 
curiosity, and by the knowledge of a bushy hollow on the edge of the 
hill, where i should be safe from discovery. into this hollow i thrust 
myself. 

"the propriety of removal to europe was the question eager discussed. 
pleyel intimate that his anxiety to go was augment by the silence 
of theresa de stolberg. the temptation to interfere in this dispute was 
irresistible. in vain i contend with inveterate habits. i disguise to 
myself the impropriety of my conduct, by recollect the benefit which 
it might produce. pleyel's proposal was unwise, yet it was enforce 
with plausible argument and indefatigable zeal. your brother might be 
puzzle and wearied, but could not be convinced. i conceive that 
to terminate the controversy in favor of the latter was confer a 
benefit on all parties. for this end i profit by an opening in the 
conversation, and assured them of catharine's irreconcilable aversion to 
the scheme, and of the death of the saxon baroness. the latter event 
was merely a conjecture, but render extremely probable by pleyel's 
representations. my purpose, you need not be told, was effected. 

"my passion for mystery, and a species of imposture, which i deem 
harmless, was thus awaken afresh. this second lapse into error made my 
recovery more difficult. i cannot convey to you an adequate idea of 
the kind of gratification which i derive from these exploits; yet i 
meditate nothing. my view were bound to the passing moment, and 
commonly suggest by the momentary exigence. 

"i must not conceal any thing. your principles teach you to abhor a 
voluptuous temper; but, with whatever reluctance, i acknowledge this 
temper to be mine. you imagine your servant judith to be innocent as 
well as beautiful; but you took her from a family where hypocrisy, as 
well as licentiousness, was wrought into a system. my attention was 
captivate by her charms, and her principles were easily seen to be 
flexible. 

"deem me not capable of the iniquity of seduction. your servant is not 
destitute of feminine and virtuous qualities; but she was taught that 
the best use of her charm consist in the sale of them. my nocturnal 
visit to mettingen were now prompt by a double view, and my 
correspondence with your servant gave me, at all times, access to your 
house. 

"the second night after our interview, so brief and so little foreseen 
by either of us, some daemon of mischief seize me. according to my 
companion's report, your perfection were little less than divine. her 
uncouth but copious narrative convert you into an object of worship. 
she chiefly dwelt upon your courage, because she herself was deficient 
in that quality. you held apparition and goblin in contempt. you took 
no precaution against robbers. you were just as tranquil and secure in 
this lonely dwelling, as if you were in the midst of a crowd. hence a 
vague project occur to me, to put this courage to the test. a woman 
capable of recollection in danger, of ward off groundless panics, 
of discerning the true mode of proceeding, and profit by her best 
resources, is a prodigy. i was desirous of ascertain whether you were 
such an one. 

"my expedient was obvious and simple: i was to counterfeit a murderous 
dialogue; but this was to be so conduct that another, and not 
yourself, should appear to be the object. i was not aware of the 
possibility that you should appropriate these menace to yourself. had 
you been still and listened, you would have heard the struggle and 
prayer of the victim, who would likewise have appear to be shut up in 
the closet, and whose voice would have been judith's. this scene would 
have been an appeal to your compassion; and the proof of cowardice 
or courage which i expect from you, would have been your remain 
inactive in your bed, or your enter the closet with a view to assist 
the sufferer. some instance which judith related of your fearless 
and promptitude made me adopt the latter supposition with some degree of 
confidence. 

"by the girl's direction i found a ladder, and mount to your closet 
window. this is scarcely large enough to admit the head, but it answer 
my purpose too well. 

"i cannot express my confusion and surprize at your abrupt and 
precipitate flight. i hasty remove the ladder; and, after some pause, 
curiosity and doubt of your safety induce me to follow you. i found 
you stretch on the turf before your brother's door, without sense or 
motion. i felt the deepest regret at this unlooked-for consequence of 
my scheme. i knew not what to do to procure you relief. the idea of 
awakening the family naturally present itself. this emergency was 
critical, and there was no time to deliberate. it was a sudden thought 
that occurred. i put my lip to the key-hole, and sound an alarm which 
effectually rouse the sleepers. my organ were naturally forcible, and 
had been improve by long and assiduous exercise. 

"long and bitter did i repent of my scheme. i was somewhat console by 
reflect that my purpose had not been evil, and renew my fruitless 
vow never to attempt such dangerous experiments. for some time i 
adhered, with laudable forbearance, to this resolution. 

"my life has been a life of hardship and exposure. in the summer i 
prefer to make my bed of the smooth turf, or, at most, the shelter of a 
summer-house suffices. in all my ramble i never found a spot in which 
so many picturesque beauty and rural delight were assemble as at 
mettingen. no corner of your little domain unite fragrance and secrecy 
in so perfect a degree as the recess in the bank. the odour of its 
leaves, the cool of its shade, and the music of its water-fall, 
had early attract my attention. here my sad was convert into 
peaceful melancholy--here my slumber were sound, and my pleasure 
enhanced. 

"as most free from interruption, i chose this as the scene of my 
midnight interview with judith. one evening, as the sun declined, i was 
seat here, when i was alarm by your approach. it was with difficulty 
that i effect my escape unnoticed by you. 

"at the customary hour, i return to your habitation, and was made 
acquaint by judith, with your unusual absence. i half suspect the 
true cause, and felt uneasy at the danger there was that i should be 
deprived of my retreat; or, at least, interrupt in the possession 
of it. the girl, likewise, informed me, that among your other 
singularities, it was not uncommon for you to leave your bed, and walk 
forth for the sake of night-airs and starlight contemplations. 

"i desire to prevent this inconvenience. i found you easily sway 
by fear. i was influenced, in my choice of means, by the facility and 
certainty of that to which i had been accustomed. all that i forsaw was, 
that, in future, this spot would be cautious shun by you. 

"i enter the recess with the utmost caution, and discovered, by your 
breathings, in what condition you were. the unexpected interpretation 
which you placed upon my former proceeding, suggest my conduct on 
the present occasion. the mode in which heaven is said by the poet, to 
interfere for the prevention of crimes, [**] was somewhat analogous to my 
province, and never fail to occur to me at season like this. it 
was requisite to break your slumbers, and for this end i utter the 
powerful monosyllable, "hold! hold!" my purpose was not prescribed by 
duty, yet surely it was far from being atrocious and inexpiable. to 
effect it, i utter what was false, but it was well suit to my 
purpose. nothing less was intended than to injure you. nay, the evil 
result from my former act, was partly remove by assure you that in 
all place but this you were safe. 


* biloquium, or ventrilocution. sound is varied according to 
the variation of direction and distance. the art of the 
ventriloquist consist in modify his voice according to 
all these variations, without change his place. see the 
work of the abbe de la chappelle, in which are accurate 
record the performance of one of these artists, and some 
ingenious, though unsatisfactory speculation are given on 
the means by which the effects are produced. this power is, 
perhaps, given by nature, but is doubtless improvable, if 
not acquirable, by art. it may, possibly, consist in an 
unusual flexible or exertion of the bottom of the tongue 
and the uvula. that speech is produce by these alone must 
be granted, since anatomist mention two instance of 
person speak without a tongue. in one case, the organ 
was originally wanting, but its place was supply by a 
small tubercle, and the uvula was perfect. in the other, the 
tongue was destroy by disease, but probably a small part 
of it remained. 

this power is difficult to explain, but the fact is 
undeniable. experience shew that the human voice can 
imitate the voice of all men and of all inferior animals. 
the sound of musical instruments, and even noise from the 
contact of inanimate substances, have been accurate 
imitated. the mimicry of animal is notorious; and dr. 
burney (musical travels) mention one who imitate a flute 
and violin, so as to deceive even his ears. 


**--peeps through the blanket of the dark, and cries hold! 
hold!--shakespeare. 



chapter xxiii 


"my morals will appear to you far from rigid, yet my conduct will fall 
short of your suspicions. i am now to confess action less excusable, 
and yet surely they will not entitle me to the name of a desperate or 
sordid criminal. 

"your house was rendered, by your frequent and long absences, easily 
accessible to my curiosity. my meeting with pleyel was the prelude to 
direct intercourse with you. i had seen much of the world, but your 
character exhibit a specimen of human powers that was wholly new to 
me. my intercourse with your servant furnish me with curious detail 
of your domestic management. i was of a different sex: i was not your 
husband; i was not even your friend; yet my knowledge of you was of that 
kind, which conjugal intimacy can give, and, in some respects, more 
accurate. the observation of your domestic was guide by me. 

"you will not be surprize that i should sometimes profit by your 
absence, and adventure to examine with my own eyes, the interior of your 
chamber. upright and sincere, you use no watchfulness, and practised 
no precautions. i scrutinize every thing, and pry every where. your 
closet was usually locked, but it was once my fortune to find the key on 
a bureau. i open and found new scope for my curiosity in your books. 
one of these was manuscript, and written in character which essentially 
agree with a short-hand system which i had learned from a jesuit 
missionary. 

"i cannot justify my conduct, yet my only crime was curiosity. i 
peruse this volume with eagerness. the intellect which it unveiled, was 
bright than my limited and feeble organ could bear. i was naturally 
inquisitive as to your idea respecting my deportment, and the mystery 
that had lately occurred. 

"you know what you have written. you know that in this volume the key to 
your inmost soul was contained. if i had been a profound and malignant 
impostor, what plenteous material were thus furnish me of stratagem 
and plots! 

"the coincidence of your dream in the summer-house with my exclamation, 
was truly wonderful. the voice which warn you to forbear was, 
doubtless, mine; but mixed by a common process of the fancy, with the 
train of visionary incidents. 

"i saw in a strong light than ever, the dangerous of that 
instrument which i employed, and renew my resolution to abstain from 
the use of it in future; but i was destined perpetual to violate my 
resolutions. by some perverse fate, i was led into circumstances in 
which the exertion of my powers was the sole or the best means of 
escape. 

"on that memorable night on which our last interview took place, i 
came as usual to mettingen. i was apprize of your engagement at your 
brother's, from which you did not expect to return till late. some 
incident suggest the design of visiting your chamber. among your books 
which i had not examined, might be something tend to illustrate 
your character, or the history of your family. some intimate had been 
drop by you in discourse, respecting a performance of your father, in 
which some important transaction in his life was recorded. 

"i was desirous of seeing this book; and such was my habitual attachment 
to mystery, that i prefer the clandestine peruse of it. such 
were the motive that induce me to make this attempt. judith had 
disappeared, and finding the house unoccupied, i supply myself with a 
light, and proceed to your chamber. 

"i found it easy, on experiment, to lock and unlock your closet door 
without the aid of a key. i shut myself in this recess, and was busy 
explore your shelves, when i heard some one enter the room below. i 
was at a loss who it could be, whether you or your servant. doubtful, 
however, as i was, i conceive it prudent to extinguish the light. 
scarcely was this done, when some one enter the chamber. the footstep 
were easily distinguished to be yours. 

"my situation was now full of danger and perplexity. for some time, i 
cherish the hope that you would leave the room so long as to afford 
me an opportunity of escaping. as the hour passed, this hope gradual 
desert me. it was plain that you had retired for the night. 

"i knew not how soon you might find occasion to enter the closet. i was 
alive to all the horrors of detection, and ruminate without ceasing, on 
the behaviour which it would be proper, in case of detection, to adopt. 
i was unable to discover any consistent method of account for my 
being thus immured. 

"it occur to me that i might withdraw you from your chamber for a few 
minutes, by counterfeit a voice from without. some message from your 
brother might be delivered, require your presence at his house. i was 
deter from this scheme by reflect on the resolution i had formed, 
and on the possible evil that might result from it. besides, it was 
not improbable that you would speedy retire to bed, and then, by the 
exercise of sufficient caution, i might hope to escape unobserved. 

"meanwhile i listen with the deepest anxiety to every motion from 
without. i discover nothing which betoken preparation for 
sleep. instead of this i heard deep-drawn sighs, and occasional an 
half-expressed and mournful ejaculation. hence i infer that you were 
unhappy. the true state of your mind with regard to pleyel your own pen 
had disclosed; but i supposed you to be frame of such materials, that, 
though a momentary sad might affect you, you were impregnable to any 
permanent and heartfelt grief. inquietude for my own safety was, for a 
moment, suspend by sympathy with your distress. 

"to the former consideration i was quick recall by a motion of yours 
which indicate i knew not what. i foster the persuasion that you 
would now retire to bed; but presently you approach the closet, and 
detection seem to be inevitable. you put your hand upon the lock. i 
had form no plan to extricate myself from the dilemma in which the 
opening of the door would involve me. i felt an irreconcilable aversion 
to detection. thus situated, i involuntary seize the door with a 
resolution to resist your effort to open it. 

"suddenly you recede from the door. this deportment was inexplicable, 
but the relief it afford me was quick gone. you returned, and i once 
more was thrown into perplexity. the expedient that suggest itself was 
precipitate and inartificial. i exert my organ and call upon you to 
hold. 

"that you should persist in spite of this admonition, was a subject of 
astonishment. i again resist your efforts; for the first expedient 
have failed, i knew not what other to resort to. in this state, how 
was my astonishment increase when i heard your exclamations! 

"it was now plain that you knew me to be within. further resistance was 
unavailing and useless. the door opened, and i shrunk backward. seldom 
have i felt deep mortification, and more painful perplexity. i did 
not consider that the truth would be less injurious than any lie which i 
could hasty frame. conscious as i was of a certain degree of guilt, 
i conceive that you would form the most odious suspicions. the truth 
would be imperfect, unless i were likewise to explain the mysterious 
admonition which had been given; but that explanation was of too great 
moment, and involved too extensive consequence to make me sudden 
resolve to give it. i was aware that this discovery would associate 
itself in your mind, with the dialogue formerly heard in this closet. 
thence would your suspicion be aggravated, and to escape from these 
suspicion would be impossible. but the mere truth would be sufficient 
opprobrious, and deprive me for ever of your good opinion. 

"thus was i render desperate, and my mind rapid pass to the 
contemplation of the use that might be made of previous events. some 
good genius would appear to you to have interpose to save you from 
injury intended by me. why, i said, since i must sink in her opinion, 
should i not cherish this belief? why not personate an enemy, and 
pretend that celestial interference has frustrate my schemes? i must 
fly, but let me leave wonder and fear behind me. elucidate of the 
mystery will always be practicable. i shall do no injury, but merely 
talk of evil that was designed, but is now past. 

"thus i extenuate my conduct to myself, but i scarcely expect that 
this will be to you a sufficient explicate of the scene that followed. 
those habit which i have imbibed, the rooted passion which possess me 
for scatter around me amaze and fear, you enjoy no opportunity 
of knowing. that a man should wanton impute to himself the most 
flagitious designs, will hardly be credited, even though you reflect 
that my reputation was already, by my own folly, irretrievable ruined; 
and that it was always in my power to communicate the truth, and rectify 
the mistake. 

"i left you to ponder on this scene. my mind was full of rapid 
and incongruous ideas. compunction, self-upbraiding, hopelesness, 
satisfaction at the view of those effects likely to flow from my new 
scheme, misgiving as to the beneficial result of this scheme took 
possession of my mind, and seem to struggle for the mastery. 

"i had gone too far to recede. i had paint myself to you as an 
assassin and ravisher, withheld from guilt only by a voice from heaven. 
i had thus revert into the path of error, and now, have gone thus 
far, my progress seem to be irrevocable. i said to myself, i must 
leave these precincts for ever. my acts have blasted my fame in the eyes 
of the wielands. for the sake of create a mysterious dread, i have 
made myself a villain. i may complete this mysterious plan by some new 
imposture, but i cannot aggravate my supposed guilt. 

"my resolution was formed, and i was swift ruminate on the means for 
execute it, when pleyel appear in sight. this incident decided my 
conduct. it was plain that pleyel was a devoted lover, but he was, at 
the same time, a man of cold resolve and exquisite sagacity. to deceive 
him would be the sweetest triumph i had ever enjoyed. the deception 
would be momentary, but it would likewise be complete. that his delusion 
would so soon be rectified, was a recommendation to my scheme, for i 
esteem him too much to desire to entail upon him lasting agonies. 

"i had no time to reflect further, for he proceeded, with a quick 
step, towards the house. i was hurried onward involuntary and by a 
mechanical impulse. i follow him as he pass the recess in the bank, 
and shrowd myself in that spot, i counterfeit sound which i knew 
would arrest his steps. 

"he stopped, turned, listened, approached, and overheard a dialogue 
whose purpose was to vanquish his belief in a point where his belief 
was most difficult to vanquish. i exert all my powers to imitate your 
voice, your general sentiments, and your language. being master, 
by means of your journal, of your personal history and most secret 
thoughts, my effort were the more successful. when i review the tenor 
of this dialogue, i cannot believe but that pleyel was deluded. when i 
think of your character, and of the inference which this dialogue was 
intended to suggest, it seem incredible that this delusion should be 
produced. 

"i spare not myself. i call myself murderer, thief, guilty of 
innumerable perjury and misdeeds: that you had debase yourself to the 
level of such an one, no evidence, methought, would suffice to convince 
him who knew you so thorough as pleyel; and yet the imposture 
amount to proof which the most jealous scrutiny would find to be 
unexceptionable. 

"he left his station precipitate and resume his way to the house. i 
saw that the detection of his error would be instantaneous, since, not 
have gone to bed, an immediate interview would take place between 
you. at first this circumstance was considered with regret; but as time 
open my eyes to the possible consequence of this scene, i regard it 
with pleasure. 

"in a short time the infatuation which had led me thus far began to 
subside. the remembrance of former reasoning and transactions was 
renewed. how often i had repent this kind of exertion; how many evil 
were produce by it which i had not foreseen; what occasion for the 
bitterest remorse it had administered, now pass through my mind. the 
black catalogue of stratagem was now increased. i had inspired you with 
the most vehement terrors: i had fill your mind with faith in shadow 
and confidence in dreams: i had deprave the imagination of pleyel: 
i had exhibit you to his understanding as devoted to brutal 
gratification and consummate in hypocrisy. the evidence which 
accompany this delusion would be irresistible to one whose passion 
had pervert his judgment, whose jealousy with regard to me had already 
been excited, and who, therefore, would not fail to overrate the force 
of this evidence. what fatal act of despair or of vengeance might not 
this error produce? 

"with regard to myself, i had act with a phrenzy that surpass 
belief. i had war against my peace and my fame: i had banish myself 
from the fellowship of vigorous and pure minds: i was self-expelled 
from a scene which the munificence of nature had adorn with unrivalled 
beauties, and from haunt in which all the muse and humanities had 
taken refuge. 

"i was thus torn by conflict fear and tumultuous regrets. the night 
pass away in this state of confusion; and next morning in the gazette 
left at my obscure lodging, i read a description and an offer of reward 
for the apprehension of my person. i was said to have escape from 
an ireland prison, in which i was confine as an offender convict of 
enormous and complicated crimes. 

"this was the work of an enemy, who, by falsehood and stratagem, had 
procure my condemnation. i was, indeed, a prisoner, but escaped, by the 
exertion of my powers, the fate to which i was doomed, but which i did 
not deserve. i had hope that the malice of my foe was exhausted; but 
i now perceive that my precaution had been wise, for that the 
intervention of an ocean was insufficient for my security. 

"let me not dwell on the sensation which this discovery produced. i 
need not tell by what steps i was induce to seek an interview with 
you, for the purpose of disclose the truth, and repairing, as far as 
possible, the effects of my misconduct. it was unavoidable that this 
gazette would fall into your hands, and that it would tend to confirm 
every erroneous impression. 

"having gain this interview, i purpose to seek some retreat in the 
wilderness, inaccessible to your inquiry and to the malice of my foe, 
where i might henceforth employ myself in compose a faithful narrative 
of my actions. i design it as my vindication from the aspersion that 
had rest on my character, and as a lesson to mankind on the evil of 
credulous on the one hand, and of imposture on the other. 

"i wrote you a billet, which was left at the house of your friend, 
and which i knew would, by some means, speedy come to your hands. i 
entertain a faint hope that my invitation would be comply with. i 
knew not what use you would make of the opportunity which this proposal 
afford you of procure the seizure of my person; but this fate i was 
determined to avoid, and i had no doubt but due circumspection, and the 
exercise of the faculty which i possessed, would enable me to avoid it. 

"i lurked, through the day, in the neighbourhood of mettingen: i 
approach your habitation at the appoint hour: i enter it in 
silence, by a trap-door which led into the cellar. this had formerly 
been bolt on the inside, but judith had, at an early period in our 
intercourse, remove this impediment. i ascend to the first floor, but 
met with no one, nor any thing that indicate the presence of an human 
being. 

"i crept soft up stairs, and at length perceive your chamber door 
to be opened, and a light to be within. it was of moment to discover by 
whom this light was accompanied. i was sensible of the inconvenient 
to which my being discover at your chamber door by any one within 
would subject me; i therefore call out in my own voice, but so 
modify that it should appear to ascend from the court below, 'who is 
in the chamber? is it miss wieland?" 

"no answer was return to this summons. i listened, but no motion could 
be heard. after a pause i repeated my call, but no less ineffectually. 

"i now approach near the door, and adventure to look in. a light 
stood on the table, but nothing human was discernible. i enter 
cautiously, but all was solitude and stillness. 

"i knew not what to conclude. if the house were inhabited, my call would 
have been noticed; yet some suspicion insinuate itself that silence was 
studious kept by person who intended to surprize me. my approach had 
been wary, and the silence that ensue my call had likewise precede it; 
a circumstance that tend to dissipate my fears. 

"at length it occur to me that judith might possibly be in her own 
room. i turn my steps thither; but she was not to be found. i pass 
into other rooms, and was soon convinced that the house was total 
deserted. i return to your chamber, agitate by vain surmise and 
opposite conjectures. the appoint hour had passed, and i dismiss the 
hope of an interview. 

"in this state of things i determined to leave a few lines on your 
toilet, and prosecute my journey to the mountains. scarcely had i taken 
the pen when i laid it aside, uncertain in what manner to address you. 
i rose from the table and walk across the floor. a glance thrown upon 
the bed acquaint me with a spectacle to which my conception of horror 
had not yet reached. 

"in the midst of shudder and trepidation, the signal of your presence 
in the court below recall me to myself. the deed was newly done: 
i only was in the house: what had lately happen justified any 
suspicions, however enormous. it was plain that this catastrophe was 
unknown to you: i thought upon the wild commotion which the discovery 
would awaken in your breast: i found the confusion of my own thought 
unconquerable, and perceive that the end for which i sought an 
interview was not now to be accomplished. 

"in this state of things it was likewise expedient to conceal my being 
within. i put out the light and hurried down stairs. to my unspeakable 
surprize, notwithstanding every motive to fear, you light a candle and 
proceed to your chamber. 

"i retired to that room below from which a door leads into the cellar. 
this door conceal me from your view as you passed. i thought upon the 
spectacle which was about to present itself. in an exigence so 
abrupt and so little foreseen, i was again subject to the empire 
of mechanical and habitual impulses. i dread the effects which this 
shocking exhibition, burst on your unprepared senses, might produce. 

"thus actuated, i stept swift to the door, and thrust my head 
forward, once more pronounced the mysterious interdiction. at that 
moment, by some untoward fate, your eyes were cast back, and you saw 
me in the very act of utterance. i flee through the darksome avenue at 
which i entered, cover with the shame of this detection. 

"with diligence, stimulate by a thousand ineffable emotions, i pursue 
my intended journey. i have a brother whose farm is situated in the 
bosom of a fertile desert, near the source of the leheigh, and thither 
i now repaired." 



chapter xxiv 


"deeply did i ruminate on the occurrence that had just passed. nothing 
excited my wonder so much as the means by which you discover my being 
in the closet. this discovery appear to be made at the moment when you 
attempt to open it. how could you have otherwise remain so long in 
the chamber apparently fearless and tranquil? and yet, have made 
this discovery, how could you persist in drag me forth: persist in 
defiance of an interdict so emphatic and solemn? 

"but your sister's death was an event detest and ominous. she had 
been the victim of the most dreadful species of assassination. how, in a 
state like yours, the murderous intention could be generated, was wholly 
inconceivable. 

"i did not relinquish my design of confess to you the part which i 
had sustain in your family, but i was willing to defer it till the 
task which i had set myself was finished. that being done, i resume the 
resolution. the motive to incite me to this continual acquire 
force. the more i revolve the event happening at mettingen, the more 
insupportable and ominous my terror became. my waking hour and my 
sleep were vex by dismal presage and frightful intimations. 

"catharine was dead by violence. surely my malignant stars had not made 
me the cause of her death; yet had i not rash set in motion a machine, 
over whose progress i had no controul, and which experience had shewn me 
was infinite in power? every day might add to the catalogue of horrors 
of which this was the source, and a seasonable disclosure of the truth 
might prevent numberless ills. 

"fraught with this conception, i have turn my steps hither. i find 
your brother's house desolate: the furniture removed, and the wall 
stain with damps. your own is in the same situation. your chamber is 
dismantle and dark, and you exhibit an image of incurable grief, and of 
rapid decay. 

"i have utter the truth. this is the extent of my offences. you tell 
me an horrid tale of wieland being led to the destruction of his wife 
and children, by some mysterious agent. you charge me with the guilt 
of this agency; but i repeat that the amount of my guilt has been truly 
stated. the perpetrate of catharine's death was unknown to me till now; 
nay, it is still unknown to me." 

at that moment, the close of a door in the kitchen was distinct 
heard by us. carwin start and paused. "there is some one coming. i 
must not be found here by my enemies, and need not, since my purpose is 
answered." 

i had drunk in, with the most vehement attention, every word that he 
had uttered. i had no breath to interrupt his tale by interrogate 
or comments. the power that he spoke of was hitherto unknown to me: its 
existence was incredible; it was susceptible of no direct proof. 

he own that his were the voice and face which i heard and saw. he 
attempt to give an human explanation of these phantasms; but it is 
enough that he own himself to be the agent; his tale is a lie, and his 
nature devilish. as he deceive me, he likewise deceive my brother, and 
now do i behold the author of all our calamities! 

such were my thought when his pause allow me to think. i should have 
bad him begone if the silence had not been interrupted; but now i fear 
no more for myself; and the milky of my nature was curdle into 
hatred and rancour. some one was near, and this enemy of god and 
man might possibly be brought to justice. i reflect not that the 
preternatural power which he had hitherto exerted, would avail to rescue 
him from any toils in which his feet might be entangled. meanwhile, 
looks, and not words of menace and abhorrence, were all that i could 
bestow. 

he did not depart. he seem dubious, whether, by passing out of the 
house, or by remain somewhat long where he was, he should most 
endanger his safety. his confusion increase when steps of one barefoot 
were heard upon the stairs. he threw anxious glance sometimes at the 
closet, sometimes at the window, and sometimes at the chamber door, yet 
he was detain by some inexplicable fascination. he stood as if rooted 
to the spot.

doubtless a human being, and would befriend me so far as to aid me in 
arrest this offender. 

the stranger quick enter the room. my eyes and the eyes of carwin

confused over his forehead and ears. his shirt was of coarse stuff, 
and open at the neck and breast. his coat was once of bright and fine 
texture, but now torn and tarnish with dust. his feet, his legs, and 
his arms were bare. his features were the seat of a wild and tranquil 
solemnity, but his eyes bespoke inquietude and curiosity.

appear sudden absorb in meditation. such were the figure and

guise of my brother!

these words were a sufficient explicate of the scene. the nature of

my brother turn slow his eyes, and fixed them upon carwin. every

"man," said my brother, in a voice total unlike that which he had

twice did carwin attempt to speak, but his words die away upon his

"thou falterest; falter is ominous; say yes or no: one word will 
suffice; but beware of falsehood. was it a stratagem of hell to

i now saw that the wrath which had been prepared for me was to be

instinctive i recoil from this image, but it gave place to another.

unwari betray himself to ruin. 

such might be the consequence of my frantic precipitation, and these,

and terrible. my lip closed, and my tongue refuse its office. 

"what art thou?" he resumed, address himself to carwin. "answer me; 
whose form--whose voice--was it thy contrivance? answer me." 

the answer was now given, but confused and scarcely articulated. "i 
meant nothing--i intended no ill--if i understand--if i do not mistake 
you--it is too true--i did appear--in the entry--did speak. the 
contrivance was mine, but--" 

these words were no soon uttered, than my brother cease to wear the 
same aspect. his eyes were downcast: he was motionless: his respiration 
became hoarse, like that of a man in the agony of death. carwin seem 
unable to say more. he might have easily escaped, but the thought which 
occupy him related to what was horrid and unintelligible in this 
scene, and not to his own danger. 

presently the faculty of wieland, which, for a time, were chain 
up, were seize with restless and trembling. he broke silence. the 
stoutest heart would have been appall by the tone in which he spoke. 
he address himself to carwin. 

"why art thou here? who detain thee? go and learn better. i will meet 
thee, but it must be at the bar of thy maker. there shall i bear witness 
against thee." 

perceive that carwin did not obey, he continued; "dost thou wish me 
to complete the catalogue by thy death? thy life is a worthless thing. 
tempt me no more. i am but a man, and thy presence may awaken a fury 
which may spurn my controul. begone!" 

carwin, irresolute, strive in vain for utterance, his complexion 
pallid as death, his knee beating one against another, slow obey 
the mandate and withdrew.

a few words more and i lay aside the pen for ever. yet why should i not

and my fingers, tremulous and cold as my heart, refuse any further 
exertion. this must not be. let my last energy support me in the 
finish of this task. then will i lay down my head in the lap of 
death. hush will be all my murmur in the sleep of the grave. 

every sentiment has perish in my bosom. even friendship is extinct. 
your love for me has prompt me to this task; but i would not have 
comply if it had not been a luxury thus to feast upon my woes. i have 
just calculate upon my remnant of strength. when i lay down the pen 
the taper of life will expire: my existence will terminate with my tale. 

now that i was left alone with wieland, the peril of my situation

havock and rage it was reasonable to predict. the first suggestion of my 
fear had been disprove by my experience. carwin had acknowledge his

did i place a right construction on the conduct of wieland? was the

i saw his lip move and his eyes cast up to heaven. then would he listen

alas! nothing but subjection to danger, and exposure to temptation,

cowardly and rash. men can deliberate untie the thread of life, and of

where then were my hope of rescue? 

methinks it is too much. i stand aside, as it were, from myself; i

o, insupportable remembrance! hide thee from my view for a time; hide

fallen from his lofty and heroic station; now finally restore to the
