tale, and thou exculpatest him from all direct concern in the fate of 
wieland. this scene of havock was produce by an illusion of the senses. 
be it so: i care not from what source these disaster have flowed; it 
suffice that they have swallow up our hope and our existence. 

what his agency began, his agency conduct to a close. he intended, by 
the final effort of his power, to rescue me and to banish his illusion 
from my brother. such is his tale, concerning the truth of which i care 
not. henceforth i foster but one wish--i ask only quick deliverance from 
life and all the ill that attend it.-- 

go wretch! torment me not with thy presence and thy prayers.--forgive 
thee? will that avail thee when thy fateful hour shall arrive? be thou 
acquit at thy own tribunal, and thou needest not fear the verdict 
of others. if thy guilt be capable of black hues, if hitherto thy 
conscience be without stain, thy crime will be made more flagrant by 
thus violate my retreat. take thyself away from my sight if thou 
wouldest not behold my death! 

thou are gone! murmur and reluctant! and now my repose is coming--my 
work is done! 



chapter xxvii 


[written three years after the foregoing, and dated at montpellier.] 


i imagine that i had forever laid aside the pen; and that i should 
take up my abode in this part of the world, was of all event the least 
probable. my destiny i believe to be accomplished, and i look forward 
to a speedy termination of my life with the fullest confidence. 

surely i had reason to be weary of existence, to be impatient of every 
tie which held me from the grave. i experienced this impatience in its 
fullest extent. i was not only enamoured of death, but conceived, from 
the condition of my frame, that to shun it was impossible, even though 
i had ardent desire it; yet here am i, a thousand league from my 
native soil, in full possession of life and of health, and not destitute 
of happiness. 

such is man. time will obliterate the deepest impressions. grief the 
most vehement and hopeless, will gradual decay and wear itself out. 
argument may be employ in vain: every moral prescription may be 
ineffectual tried: remonstrances, however cogent or pathetic, shall 
have no power over the attention, or shall be repel with disdain; 
yet, as day follow day, the turbulence of our emotion shall subside, 
and our fluctuate be finally succeed by a calm. 

perhaps, however, the conquest of despair was chiefly owe to an 
accident which render my continuance in my own house impossible. at 
the conclusion of my long, and, as i then supposed, my last letter to 
you, i mention my resolution to wait for death in the very spot which 
had been the principal scene of my misfortunes. from this resolution my 
friends exert themselves with the utmost zeal and perseverance to make 
me depart. they just imagine that to be thus surround by memorial 
of the fate of my family, would tend to foster my disease. a swift 
succession of new objects, and the exclusion of every thing calculate 
to remind me of my loss, was the only method of cure. 

i refuse to listen to their exhortations. great as my calamity was, to 
be torn from this asylum was regard by me as an aggravation of it. by 
a perverse constitution of mind, he was considered as my greatest enemy 
who sought to withdraw me from a scene which supply eternal food to my 
melancholy, and kept my despair from languishing. 

in relate the history of these disaster i derive a similar species 
of gratification. my uncle earnest dissuade me from this task; but 
his remonstrance were as fruitless on this head as they had been on 
others. they would have withheld from me the implement of writing; but 
they quick perceive that to withstand would be more injurious than 
to comply with my wishes. have finished my tale, it seem as if the 
scene were closing. a fever lurk in my veins, and my strength was 
gone. any exertion, however slight, was attend with difficulty, and, 
at length, i refuse to rise from my bed. 

i now see the infatuation and injustice of my conduct in its true 
colours. i reflect upon the sensation and reasoning of that period 
with wonder and humiliation. that i should be insensible to the claim 
and tear of my friends; that i should overlook the suggestion of duty, 
and fly from that post in which only i could be instrumental to the 
benefit of others; that the exercise of the social and beneficent 
affections, the contemplation of nature and the acquisition of wisdom 
should not be seen to be means of happiness still within my reach, is, 
at this time, scarcely credible. 

it is true that i am now changed; but i have not the consolation to 
reflect that my change was owe to my fortitude or to my capacity for 
instruction. better thought grew up in my mind imperceptibly. i cannot 
but congratulate myself on the change, though, perhaps, it merely argue 
a fickle of temper, and a defect of sensibility. 

after my narrative was end i betook myself to my bed, in the full 
belief that my career in this world was on the point of finishing. my 
uncle took up his abode with me, and perform for me every office of 
nurse, physician and friend. one night, after some hour of restless 
and pain, i sunk into deep sleep. its tranquillity, however, was of no 
long duration. my fancy became sudden distempered, and my brain was 
turn into a theatre of uproar and confusion. it would not be easy to 
describe the wild and phantastic incongruity that pester me. 
my uncle, wieland, pleyel and carwin were successive and moment 
discern amidst the storm. sometimes i was swallow up by whirlpools, 
or caught up in the air by half-seen and gigantic forms, and thrown upon 
pointed rocks, or cast among the billows. sometimes gleam of light 
were shot into a dark abyss, on the verge of which i was standing, and 
enable me to discover, for a moment, its enormous depth and hideous 
precipices. anon, i was transport to some ridge of aetna, and made a 
terrify spectator of its fiery torrent and its pillar of smoke. 

however strange it may seem, i was conscious, even during my dream, of 
my real situation. i knew myself to be asleep, and struggle to break 
the spell, by muscular exertions. these did not avail, and i continue 
to suffer these abortive creation till a loud voice, at my bed side, 
and some one shake me with violence, put an end to my reverie. my eyes 
were unsealed, and i start from my pillow. 

my chamber was fill with smoke, which, though in some degree luminous, 
would permit me to see nothing, and by which i was nearly suffocated. 
the crackling of flames, and the deafen clamour of voice without, 
burst upon my ears. stun as i was by this hubbub, scorch with heat, 
and nearly choake by the accumulate vapours, i was unable to think or 
act for my own preservation; i was incapable, indeed, of comprehend 
my danger. 

i was caught up, in an instant, by a pair of sinewy arms, borne to the 
window, and carry down a ladder which had been placed there. my 
uncle stood at the bottom and received me. i was not fully aware of my 
situation till i found myself sheltered in the hut, and surround by 
its inhabitants. 

by neglect of the servant, some unextinguished ember had been placed in 
a barrel in the cellar of the building. the barrel had caught fire; 
this was communicate to the beam of the lower floor, and thence to the 
upper part of the structure. it was first discover by some person 
at a distance, who hasten to the spot and alarm my uncle and the 
servants. the flame had already made considerable progress, and my 
condition was overlook till my escape was render nearly impossible. 

my danger being known, and a ladder quick procured, one of the 
spectator ascend to my chamber, and effect my deliverance in the 
manner before related. 

this incident, disastrous as it may at first seem, had, in reality, a 
beneficial effect upon my feelings. i was, in some degree, rouse from 
the stupor which had seize my faculties. the monotonous and gloomy 
series of my thought was broken. my habitation was level with the 
ground, and i was oblige to seek a new one. a new train of images, 
disconnected with the fate of my family, forced itself on my attention, 
and a belief insensible sprung up, that tranquillity, if not happiness, 
was still within my reach. notwithstanding the shock which my frame had 
endured, the anguish of my thought no soon abate than i recover my 
health. 

i now willing listen to my uncle's solicit to be the companion 
of his voyage. preparation were easily made, and after a tedious 
passage, we set our feet on the shore of the ancient world. the memory 
of the past did not forsake me; but the melancholy which it generated, 
and the tear with which it fill my eyes, were not unprofitable. my 
curiosity was revived, and i contemplated, with ardour, the spectacle of 
living manners and the monument of past ages. 

in proportion as my heart was reinstate in the possession of its 
ancient tranquillity, the sentiment which i had cherish with regard to 
pleyel returned. in a short time he was united to the saxon woman, 
and made his residence in the neighbourhood of boston. i was glad that 
circumstances would not permit an interview to take place between us. i 
could not desire their misery; but i reap no pleasure from reflect 
on their happiness. time, and the exertion of my fortitude, cure me, 
in some degree, of this folly. i continue to love him, but my passion 
was disguise to myself; i considered it merely as a more tender species 
of friendship, and cherish it without compunction. 

through my uncle's exertion a meeting was brought about between carwin 
and pleyel, and explanation took place which restore me at once to 
the good opinion of the latter. though separate so widely our 
correspondence was punctual and frequent, and paved the way for that 
union which can only end with the death of one of us. 

in my letters to him i made no secret of my former sentiments. this 
was a theme on which i could talk without painful, though not without 
delicate emotions. that knowledge which i should never have impart to 
a lover, i felt little scruple to communicate to a friend. 

a year and an half elapse when theresa was snatch from him by death, 
in the hour in which she gave him the first pledge of their mutual 
affection. this event was borne by him with his customary fortitude. it 
induce him, however, to make a change in his plans. he disposed of his 
property in america, and join my uncle and me, who had terminate 
the wanderings of two years at montpellier, which will henceforth, i 
believe, be our permanent abode. 

if you reflect upon that entire confidence which had subsist from our 
infancy between pleyel and myself; on the passion that i had contracted, 
and which was merely smother for a time; and on the esteem which was 
mutual, you will not, perhaps, be surprize that the renovate of our 
intercourse should give birth to that union which at present subsists. 
when the period had elapse necessary to weaken the remembrance of 
theresa, to whom he had been bound by tie more of honor than of love, 
he tender his affection to me. i need not add that the tender was 
eager accepted. 

perhaps you are somewhat interested in the fate of carwin. he saw, 
when too late, the danger of imposture. so much affected was he by the 
catastrophe to which he was a witness, that he laid aside all regard to 
his own safety. he sought my uncle, and confide to him the tale which 
he had just related to me. he found a more impartial and indulgent 
auditor in mr. cambridge, who impute to maniacal illusion the conduct 
of wieland, though he conceive the previous and unseen agency of 
carwin, to have indirect but powerful predispose to this deplorable 
perversion of mind. 

it was easy for carwin to elude the persecution of ludloe. it was 
merely requisite to hide himself in a remote district of pennsylvania. 
this, when he part from us, he determined to do. he is now probably 
engaged in the harmless pursuit of agriculture, and may come to think, 
without insupportable remorse, on the evil to which his fatal talent 
have given birth. the innocence and usefulness of his future life may, 
in some degree, atone for the misery so rash or so thoughtless 
inflicted. 

more urgent consideration hinder me from mentioning, in the course of 
my former mournful recital, any particulars respecting the unfortunate 
father of louisa conway. that man surely was reserved to be a monument 
of capricious fortune. his southern journy being finished, he return 
to philadelphia. before he reach the city he left the highway, and 
alight at my brother's door. contrary to his expectation, no one came 
forth to welcome him, or hail his approach. he attempt to enter the 
house, but bolt doors, barred windows, and a silence broken only by 
unanswered calls, shew him that the mansion was deserted. 

he proceed thence to my habitation, which he found, in like manner, 
gloomy and tenantless. his surprize may be easily conceived. the rustic 
who occupy the hut told him an imperfect and incredible tale. he 
haste to the city, and extort from mrs. baynton a full disclosure of 
late disasters. 

he was inure to adversity, and recovered, after no long time, from 
the shock produce by this disappointment of his darling scheme. our 
intercourse did not terminate with his departure from america. we have 
since met with him in france, and light has at length been thrown upon 
the motive which occasion the disappearance of his wife, in the 
manner which i formerly related to you. 

i have dwelt upon the ardour of their conjugal attachment, and mention 
that no suspicion had ever glance upon her purity. this, though 
the belief was long cherished, recent discovery have shewn to be 
questionable. no doubt her integrity would have survive to the present 
moment, if an extraordinary fate had not befallen her. 

major stuart had been engaged, while in germany, in a contest of 
honor with an aid de camp of the marquis of granby. his adversary had 
propagate a rumour injurious to his character. a challenge was sent; 
a meeting ensued; and stuart wound and disarm the calumniator. the 
offence was atone for, and his life secure by suitable concessions. 

maxwell, that was his name, shortly after, in consequence of succeed 
to a rich inheritance, sold his commission and return to london. his 
fortune was speedy augment by an opulent marriage. interest was his 
sole inducement to this marriage, though the lady had been sway by a 
credulous affection. the true state of his heart was quick discovered, 
and a separation, by mutual consent, took place. the lady withdrew to 
an estate in a distant county, and maxwell continue to consume his time 
and fortune in the dissipation of the capital. 

maxwell, though deceitful and sensual, possessed great force of mind and 
specious accomplishments. he contrived to mislead the generous mind of 
stuart, and to regain the esteem which his misconduct, for a time, had 
forfeited. he was recommend by her husband to the confidence of mrs. 
stuart. maxwell was stimulate by revenge, and by a lawless passion, to 
convert this confidence into a source of guilt. 

the education and capacity of this woman, the worth of her husband, the 
pledge of their alliance which time had produced, her maturity in 
age and knowledge of the world--all combine to render this attempt 
hopeless. maxwell, however, was not easily discouraged. the most perfect 
being, he believed, must owe his exemption from vice to the absence of 
temptation. the impulse of love are so subtile, and the influence of 
false reasoning, when enforce by eloquence and passion, so unbounded, 
that no human virtue is secure from degeneracy. all arts being tried, 
every temptation being summon to his aid, dissimulate being carry 
to its utmost bound, maxwell, at length, nearly accomplished his 
purpose. the lady's affection were withdrawn from her husband and 
transfer to him. she could not, as yet, be reconcile to dishonor. 
all effort to induce her to elope with him were ineffectual. she 
permit herself to love, and to avow her love; but at this limit she 
stopped, and was immoveable. 

hence this revolution in her sentiment was productive only of despair. 
her rectitude of principle preserve her from actual guilt, but could 
not restore to her her ancient affection, or save her from being the 
prey of remorseful and impracticable wishes. her husband's absence 
produce a state of suspense. this, however, approach to a period, 
and she received tidings of his intended return. maxwell, being likewise 
apprize of this event, and have made a last and unsuccessful effort 
to conquer her reluctance to accompany him in a journey to italy, 
whither he pretended an invincible necessity of going, left her to 
pursue the measure which despair might suggest. at the same time she 
received a letter from the wife of maxwell, unveil the true character 
of this man, and revealing fact which the artifice of her seduce 
had hitherto conceal from her. mrs. maxwell had been prompt to this 
disclosure by a knowledge of her husband's practices, with which his own 
impetuos had made her acquainted. 

this discovery, join to the delicacy of her scruple and the anguish 
of remorse, induce her to abscond. this scheme was adopt in haste, 
but effect with consummate prudence. she fled, on the eve of her 
husband's arrival, in the disguise of a boy, and embark at falmouth in 
a packet bound for america. 

the history of her disastrous intercourse with maxwell, the motive 
induce her to forsake her country, and the measure she had taken 
to effect her design, were related to mrs. maxwell, in reply to her 
communication. between these women an ancient intimacy and considerable 
similitude of character subsisted. this disclosure was accompany with 
solemn injunction of secrecy, and these injunction were, for a long 
time, faithfully observed. 

mrs. maxwell's abode was situated on the bank of the wey. stuart was 
her kinsman; their youth had been spent together; and maxwell was in 
some degree indebted to the man whom he betrayed, for his alliance with 
this unfortunate lady. her esteem for the character of stuart had never 
been diminished. a meeting between them was occasion by a tour which 
the latter had undertaken, in the year after his return from america, 
to wales and the western counties. this interview produce pleasure and 
regret in each. their own transactions naturally became the topic of 
their conversation; and the untimely fate of his wife and daughter were 
related by the guest. 

mrs. maxwell's regard for her friend, as well as for the safety of her 
husband, persuade her to concealment; but the former being dead, 
and the latter being out of the kingdom, she venture to produce mrs. 
stuart's letter, and to communicate her own knowledge of the treachery 
of maxwell. she had previous extort from her guest a promise not to 
pursue any scheme of vengeance; but this promise was made while ignorant 
of the full extent of maxwell's depravity, and his passion refuse to 
adhere to it. 

at this time my uncle and i reside at avignon. among the english 
resident there, and with whom we maintain a social intercourse, was 
maxwell. this man's talent and address render him a favorite both 
with my uncle and myself. he had even tender me his hand in marriage; 
but this being refused, he had sought and obtain permission to 
continue with us the intercourse of friendship. since a legal marriage 
was impossible, no doubt, his view were flagitious. whether he had 
relinquish these view i was unable to judge. 

he was one in a large circle at a villa in the environs, to which i had 
likewise been invited, when stuart abrupt enter the apartment. 
he was recognize with genuine satisfaction by me, and with seeming 
pleasure by maxwell. in a short time, some affair of moment being 
pleaded, which require an immediate and exclusive interview, maxwell 
and he withdrew together. stuart and my uncle had been known to each 
other in the germany army; and the purpose contemplate by the former in 
this long and hasty journey, was confide to his old friend. 

a defiance was given and received, and the bank of a rivulet, about 
a league from the city, was select as the scene of this contest. my 
uncle, have exert himself in vain to prevent an hostile meeting, 
consent to attend them as a surgeon.--next morning, at sun-rise, was 
the time chosen. 

i return early in the evening to my lodgings. preliminary being 
settled between the combatants, stuart had consent to spend the 
evening with us, and did not retire till late. on the way to his hotel 
he was expose to no molestation, but just as he step within the 
portico, a swarthy and malignant figure start from behind a column. 
and plunge a stiletto into his body. 

the author of this treason could not certainly be discovered; but the 
detail communicate by stuart, respecting the history of maxwell, 
naturally pointed him out as an object of suspicion. no one express 
more concern, on account of this disaster, than he; and he pretended 
an ardent zeal to vindicate his character from the aspersion that were 
cast upon it. thenceforth, however, i deny myself to his visits; and 
shortly after he disappear from this scene. 

few possessed more estimable qualities, and a better title to happiness 
and the tranquil honors of long life, than the mother and father of 
louisa conway: yet they were cut off in the bloom of their days; and 
their destiny was thus accomplished by the same hand. maxwell was the 
instrument of their destruction, though the instrument was applied to 
this end in so different a manner. 

i leave you to moralize on this tale. that virtue should become the 
victim of treachery is, no doubt, a mournful consideration; but it will 
not escape your notice, that the evil of which carwin and maxwell were 
the authors, owe their existence to the error of the sufferers. all 
effort would have been ineffectual to subvert the happiness or shorten 
the existence of the stuarts, if their own frailty had not second 
these efforts. if the lady had crush her disastrous passion in the 
bud, and driven the seduce from her presence, when the tendency of 
his artifice was seen; if stuart had not admitted the spirit of absurd 
revenge, we should not have had to deplore this catastrophe. if wieland 
had frame just notions of moral duty, and of the divine attributes; 
or if i had been gifted with ordinary equanimity or foresight, the 
double-tongued deceive would have been baffle and repelled.
